

OceanofPDF.com

ARCHITECTURE AND
PROGRESS
This volume proposes an interdependent relationship between progress and
obstacles in architecture and the built environment. It challenges the
positive notion of progress, and the conception of progress and obstacle as a
dichotomy.
For shapers of the world, finding 'the solution' is often a mark of
progress that becomes embedded in culture, society, and history. Progress is
ever-present. Through the exploration of diverse positions in history, theory,
and practice, this book explores the potential utility of the progressively
problematic rather than the natural tendency towards the progressively
solved. Chapters draw on historic spaces, technological advancement,
incorporation of the natural world, alterative production, and the
consideration of human experience both sensory and psychological.
Challenging the positive connotation of 'progress', the writings also explore
reversing the notion of obstacle from disruptive anti-tool to obstacle of
utilitarian method, a useful tool, pressing approach, and overarching value
system.
This will be interesting reading for upper-level students and scholars of
Architecture, Urban Design, Philosophy, and Sociology.
Mark Alan Blumberg is an Assistant Professor at the Auburn University
College of Architecture Design and Construction, USA. Mark researches
the spaces between cognition and representation through diagrams, and
between abstraction and analysis in mapping. His interests in the urban
scale and complexities serve as a connection between present

environmental, cultural, and societal conditions and potential future
outcomes of current decisions and practices.
Matt Hall is a Professor at the Auburn University College of Architecture
Design and Construction, USA, and partner in the design firms Superunison
and Obstructures. His work is concerned with the rift between intentions
and consequences, the dilemmas of decision making, and the uncertain
future contexts that all design is inevitably subject to. His is the co-editor of
Lewerentz Fragments (Actar, 2021) and an A+U feature issue on Swedish
architect Bernt Nyberg (2017), along with other publications on post-war
Swedish architecture. He has lectured, published, and exhibited
internationally on topics related to theory, criticism, and pedagogy.

CONTRIBUTORS
Matthias Altwicker is an architect and professor. His firm, Studio A+H,
works on New York City projects from new construction to adaptive reuse.
He is committed to research and has worked tirelessly on researching and
exhibiting new perspectives on public housing, open space, and housing
density to the broader public. He is the senior architect for 3XN-North
America, managing constructed projects in Canada and the US.
Brian Ambroziak is an Associate Professor and Chair of Architecture
Undergraduate Studies at the University of Tennessee, USA. His research
engages the creative process, the development of one's artistic conscience,
and focuses on the complex relationship between design and methods of
representation and visualization with an inherent bias towards writing and
collage.
Katherine Bambrick Ambroziak is an Associate Professor and Associate
Dean of Academic Affairs in the College of Architecture and Design at the
University of Tennessee, USA. Her research and teaching examine how
designers and users become conscious of their built and natural
environments. She focuses on spatial theory related to sensory response and
body perception, ritual theory, and contemporary memorial theory.
Mark Alan Blumberg is an Assistant Professor at the Auburn University
College of Architecture Design and Construction, USA. Mark researches
the spaces between cognition and representation through diagrams and
between abstraction and analysis in mapping. His interests in the urban
scale and complexities serve as a connection between present

environmental, cultural, and societal conditions and potential future
outcomes of current decisions and practices.
Cristina Bueno is Assistant Professor of History and Theory of
Architecture at Universidad San Francisco de Quito, Ecuador. She holds a
master's degree in the Architecture, Theory and Project Program from the
Escola Tècnica Superior d'Arquitectura de Barcelona and a PhD from the
Universitat Politécnica de Catalunya, Spain. Her architectural practice
combines urban form and theory, and her research focuses on the graphic
representation in the early cartography of Quito and how it shaped the
construction of the local territory.
Carolina Dayer, PhD, is an architect and Associate Professor at Aarhus
School of Architecture in Denmark. She is the co-editor of Confabulations:
Storytelling in Architecture (Routledge, 2016), Activism in Architecture:
Bright Dreams of Passive Energy Design (Routledge, 2018), and
Imaginaries on Matter: Tools, Materials, Origins (AADR, 2023). In 2021,
she exhibited the artistic-research project 'Insiders, Othered Histories' at the
On-Site Gallery in Denmark. She was design associate editor of the Journal
of Architectural Education from 2017 until 2021, during which she theme-
edited two issues: A/To Project and Built. She currently works on
intersections between cultures of maintenance and humor in construction
details.
Mustafa Faruki is the founding partner of theLab-lab for architecture, a
narrowly known purveyor of unbuilt work, unpublished volumes, and
unsolicited pleasures. His practice centers self-observation, feeling, and a
studied consideration of the resonance of our deeply entangled histories.

Jonathan Foote, PhD, is an architect and Associate Professor at Aarhus
School of Architecture, Denmark. He has lectured, chaired conferences, and
published widely on architectural drawing, materiality, and stone in the
humanities, including work on Michelangelo's paper template drawings and
the recent co-edited book Imaginaries on Matter: Tools, Materials, Origins
(AADR, 2023).
Matt Hall is a Professor at the Auburn University College of Architecture
Design and Construction, USA, and partner in the design firms Superunison
and Obstructures. His work is concerned with the rift between intentions
and consequences, the dilemmas of decision making, and the uncertain
future contexts that all design is inevitably subject to. His is the co-edtior of
Lewerentz Fragments (Actar, 2021) and an A+U feature issue on Swedish
architect Bernt Nyberg (2017), along with other publications on post-war
Swedish architecture. He has lectured, published, and exhibited
internationally on topics related to theory, criticism, and pedagogy.
Wes Jones is a partner in Jones, Partners: Architecture, a California-based
practice known for technologically inspired design. His work has been
exhibited widely and can be found in the permanent collections of major
museums around the world. Four monographs of this work have been
published over the years by Princeton Architectural Press and ORO
Editions. A recipient of the Rome Prize from the American Academy in
Rome, and the Arts and Letters Award in Architecture from the American
Academy of the Arts and Letters, he currently serves as a Professor in
Practice at the USC School of Architecture, USA.
Nathan Matteson is an Associate Professor at DePaul University's School
of Design, USA. A ruthlessly collaborative designer whose work merrily

ignores perceived boundaries between disciplines, his practice situates itself
at an intersection among dead ends, superhighways, and goat paths,
wringing its metaphorical hands over relationships among computation,
intention, materiality, and immateriality.
Sonia Sobrino Ralston is an Assistant Teaching Professor in Landscape
Architecture and Art + Design at Northeastern University's College of
Media, Art and Design, USA. Educated as a landscape architect and
architect at Harvard and Princeton, respectively, her work explores how
plants and information systems collide in the built environment.
Patrick Rhodes is an Associate Professor at the University of Sharjah in
the United Arab Emirates. His scholarship focuses on design-build and
community engagement through social, economic, and environmental
design, and has been awarded, published, and exhibited widely. Since 2013,
he has taught architecture in the heart of the Arab World.
Gregory Spaw is an Associate Professor at the American University of
Sharjah in the United Arab Emirates. Concurrent with his academic
engagement, Spaw is a principal of SHO Architecture, a design
collaborative that straddles the territories of teaching, research, and
practice. His scholarship and practice centers on digital visualization,
harnessing parametric workflows, intelligent material fabrication, and
responsive environment design.
Constance Vale is Chair of Undergraduate Architecture and Associate
Professor at Washington University in St. Louis, USA. She is also a
registered architect and founding partner of AVV A. Her research explores
intersections of architecture, theater, urbanism, and technology, examining

how artificial intelligence and other emerging technologies can shape more
equitable and resilient environments.
Michael Young is an Associate Professor at the Irwin S. Chanin School of
Architecture at the Cooper Union, USA. He is the author of The Estranged
Object (Graham Foundation, 2015) and Reality Modeled After Images:
Architecture and Aesthetics after the Digital Image (Routledge, 2022).
Michael was the recipient of the 2019-20 Rome Prize from the American
Academy of Rome.

INTRODUCTION
Obstacles in Progress
Matt Hall and Mark Alan Blumberg
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-1
Progress is both innate and inevitable. To progress is to exist, but life can
unfold more or less progressively. Progress is ever-present. In any given
scenario, circumstances can become progressively worse or better. While it
is inherently human to strive for progress, within that pursuit lie obstacles,
foreign territory and the unexpected.
Our world consists of obstacles in progress, which become progressively
problematic as we advance, overcoming challenges only to uncover others
lying beneath. Culture tends to dismantle progress even as it is being made,
and this dismantling is often by design. Given the profound impact of
design on the world, it is pressing and relevant to critically examine its role
in human progress by asking: How do we define the progressively
problematic within the discipline of design?
The editors propose that progress and obstacles are interdependent,
challenging the conventionally positive notion of progress through diverse
positions in the history, theory, practice and representation of architecture
and the built environment. For shapers of the world, finding the solution is
often seen as a mark of progress, but perhaps historical case studies in the
past, provocative visions of the future and assessments of our complex
present context may have utility for thoroughly understanding, if not

breaking, the problematic cycle of our designs. To do so requires
intellectual and practical approaches to reveal that the byproducts of design
often produce more unknowns than knowns, most of which are discovered
when it is too late. Although progress is commonly understood as linear,
positive and durable, a more precise inventory of its consequences suggests
otherwise.
Progress
Philosopher and author John Gray situates progress as "any kind of advance
that is cumulative, so that what's achieved at one period is the basis for later
achievement that then, over time, becomes more and more irreversible."1
He acknowledges the durability of progress in tangible, empirical contexts
such as science and technology, but does not suggest that this definition of
progress extends to civilization itself. According to Gray, cultural progress
(relative to ethics, behaviors and governance) is fragile, never having a firm
footing and often losing ground. In contrast, technological development
often tends to progress in a more solid, cumulative way. This accepts the
improbability that, as a culture, we will choose to tackle our contemporary
problems by regressing technologically, as our relationship with it has
become existential, if not outright religious.
There is also a tendency to equate technological progress with moral
progress. Just as architectural movements have historically progressed via
their relationship to technology, so our notion of civilization is as much
defined (and secured) by its advanced technology as much as by any value-
based order. Within the context of this publication, the distinction between
technological and cultural progress is more thoughtfully explored when
intentionally blurred. Architecture and design are complex disciplines at the
intersection of technology and culture—serving both, mastering neither, yet

highly influenced by both areas of human production. They are professions
tangled between the calculable and the incalculable, subject to a
multifaceted array of often conflicting values. In this sense, architecture and
design are situated relative to both cultural and technological progress,
simultaneously shaped by and shaping both, with no clear delineation.
It is worth reminding ourselves of the consequences of not progressing.
From a technological perspective, one can easily imagine what the world
might look like if we refused to iterate relative to the tools and objects that
enable our lives. (Of course, this is relative and dependent on geography,
wealth and politics.) We continue the advance of technological innovation
and refinement—shaping and distorting, making some lives more
convenient, others more difficult, and all infinitely more complex.
Throughout history, various dogmatic positions, ranging from the economic
to the political and spiritual, have resisted progress. Illiteracy once
preserved the power of kings and gods, while notions of the collective
good, productivity, or mere profit kept (and continue to keep) millions
enslaved. It's clear that technology and information have been used to
coerce, control and profit rather than inform, enlighten and enrich. These
are of all forms of progress, demonstrating that progress is not universal
and not inherently positive. One can easily assume that a cultural issue,
right or privilege held dear is being repudiated at this very moment.
The rejection of cultural progress is becoming more probable by the day,
as evidenced by the current state of our polarization. Yet to renounce
technological progress risks earning the title of Luddite, primitivist or
romantic. In any case, there are few champions of the idea that we regress.
Our world seems defined by growth, and the advance of the technological
progress machine persists with seemingly unwavering continuity. Nothing
historically is more certain than the persistent march towards the smaller,

faster, cheaper and more abundant—at least for those who can afford it.
Conversely, there is nothing more simplistic than proposing that we simply
stop and accept the current state of technology. Repudiation of progress is
frequently dismissed as romantic, evident in those who reenact the past or
choose inefficient and outdated modes of living as an act of protest,
practicality, experience or aesthetics. An outright abandonment of
technology is naïve; virtually all forces of contemporary culture demand
submission to it. Try living without an internet connection.
Paul Kingsnorth offers a scathing critique of this ever-present reality:
"Romanticizing the past" is a familiar accusation, made mostly by
people who think it is more grown-up to romanticize the future. But
it's not necessary to convince yourself that Paleolithic hunter-gatherers
lived in paradise in order to observe that progress is a ratchet, every
turn forcing us more tightly into the gears of a machine we were forced
to create to solve the problems created by progress. It is far too late to
think about dismantling this machine in a rational manner—and in any
case who wants to? We can't deny that it brings benefits to us, even as
it chokes us and our world by degrees.2
The "choking" of our world has produced both freedom and slavery, with
an ever-shifting definition of each. It has led to advancement and
regression, enabling diverse civilizations while also destroying them. The
compression of variables (the solving of problems) creates order by
resolving issues, but problems persist no matter how diligently we attempt
to tame and control them. Progress is dependent on culture, defined perhaps
as our construct for keeping our most primal instincts at bay, metaphorically
holding the barbarians at the gate. To achieve this, culture must constantly
adapt, maneuver and advance—perhaps its most defining characteristic.

Civilization and Culture
The progressive human condition unfolds within a landscape of conditions
requiring actions and decisions, often framed as problems. We have evolved
to solve these problems using concepts, models and tools. Culture generates
concepts for addressing problems (ideas), which may become reliable
models (that sometimes require tools for execution). While the story of
human culture can be told via innumerable narratives, the development of
tools (technology) and their constant refinement presents a compelling
argument for human evolution.
This begs the question: Why do we constantly need to evolve our tools?
Invariably, it's due to the reliable flow of problems that culture presents. As
we evolve, so do our problems, and we race to catch up. In essence,
obstacles are both the generators and the consequences of problems. The
illusion we perpetuate is that we can actually solve these problems.
This brings us to question the fundamental nature and durability of
progress. Can progress be achieved without knowing and understanding
what obstacles it may overturn? Can it be achieved in one state while
simultaneously producing obstacles in another, or can it happen in
ignorance of the problems it will produce?
In A Short History of Progress, Ronald Wright describes the concept of a
"progress trap," where a solution appears beneficial in the short term but,
upon reaching a certain scale or intensity, results in catastrophic
consequences. He warns of the fatal acceleration of technological change
that occurs as our own ideas move much faster than our hands and minds
can truly comprehend. To avoid these traps, Wright advocates for the
"precautionary principle"—a slow and cautious approach to innovation. Yet
this principle is surely at odds with the demands and rapid pace of cultural
and technological evolution.

The human character is that we are very clever and are extremely good
innovative short-term thinkers, but we are extremely bad at foreseeing
the long-term consequences of what we are doing and also extremely
bad at limiting our immediate desires ... we tend to let things rip until
they crash.3
While this suggests a crash, the reality is that collisions seem to be
occurring constantly at various scales. Our misalignment of intention and
consequence stems from our inability or unwillingness to fully consider the
negative outcomes of our designs, if doing so is even possible. As Wright
puts it in his interview:
The trouble is that you can't really tell the difference between good
progress and bad progress until it's played out, by which time if you've
been mistaken in your idea of what true progress is it may be too late
by that point.
In addition to these traps, we are faced with the fact that our understanding
of function in the world of design is both limited and crippling. What
functions well in one context often fails in another. In a chapter titled "Art
and Science. Energy. Results" from his 1978 book The Nature and
Aesthetics of Design, David Pye delivers a scathing critique of function
where he makes the provocative claim that nothing truly works.
The concept of function in design, and even the doctrine of
functionalism might be worth a little attention if things ever worked ...
I have sometimes wondered whether our unconscious motive for doing
so much useless work is to show that if we cannot make things work
properly we can at least make them presentable ... never do we
achieve a satisfactory performance. Things simply are not fit for their

purpose ... Every thing we design and make is an improvisation, a
lash-up, something inept and provisional. We live like castaways. But
even at that we can be debonair and make the best of it. If we cannot
have our way in performance we will have it in appearance.4
Pye alludes not only to unintended consequences but also perhaps to
incompetence. His critique raises fundamental questions about the
responsibility of designers. What design professional has the foresight to
predict every adverse effect of their design? Which harms are intentional
compromises given the inability of a design to equally meet all criteria
equally as well? Do all designs ultimately become obstacles, or is the act of
design an obstacle in itself?
Our world never ceases to present us with obstacles, often giving us no
choice but to design our way around them. Some designers believe
themselves meticulous in measuring and responding to problematic
contexts and conditions. Yet most operate like bricoleurs, drifting from
problem to solution and somehow getting by, blind to the outcomes and
constrained by myopic views of what conditions matter. The ability to deal
with obstacles via design might be analogous to survival: we survive, more
or less, but not without the manifestation of obstacles that are co-dependent
on our progress. The relationship between progress and obstacle is never
neutral; its dance is categorically understood in a contextual way,
continually in flux according to vantage point, time, intention and
consequence.
Obstacle
Almost every object, process and human endeavor is impeded by
something, whether from an external context or an internal state. The first

obstacle humanity encountered was nature. We entered the world tool-less,
naked and afraid. We made progress by contending with our given
condition. Obstacles took the form of large rocks or saber-toothed tigers,
excessive heat or cold, hunger or infertile soil. For each of these "natural"
obstacles we have progressed beyond them, using technology as a lever to
dislodge them, inadvertently replacing them with obstacles of our own
creation.
Obstacles are evidence of progress. If progress is an aim, then the
obstacle is a result. Akin to the same story told from different vantage
points, each product of progress has multiple impacts. One party's progress
becomes another's obstacle, and, even for the party that progresses, the
artifacts of that progression are not always positive. Instead, they come with
more obstacles to overcome. This unyielding cycle of progress, solving
problems to create obstacles that then serve as future problems, is what
philosopher Vilém Flusser calls the "internal dialectic of culture," assuming
we define culture as "totality of all objects of use." In his chapter aptly
titled "Design: Obstacle for/to the Removal of Obstacles," from The Shape
of Things (1999), he sums up the dialectic:
I come across obstacles in my path (come across the objective,
substantial, problematic world); I overturn some of these obstacles
(transform them into objects of use, into culture) in order to continue,
and the objects thus overturned prove to be obstacles in themselves.
The more I continue, the more I am obstructed by objects of use.5
In essence, Flusser's central thesis is that "the world is objective,
substantial, problematic, as long as it obstructs." He elaborates upon this,
ultimately asking, why? The answer is simple: "they were projected as
designs on the part of people who went before. They are projected designs

that I need in order to continue and that obstruct me from continuing."6 In
other words, most obstacles in our technological history are designed,
produced and utilized in the name of progress. Yet, these leave traces or
traps that others must overcome. Each act of design can be thought of as
specific to a task or constituent, hyper-sensitive to a particular context, and
often violently indifferent to everything beyond it. The consequences are
felt by innocent bystanders left to deal with the scars of progress, setting up
future generations to deal with our "solutions." In another chapter, "The
Non-Thing 1," Flusser reconceptualizes the solution as problem removal, or
at least management as a process of "production," in the context of delaying
the inevitable:
'To live' means to proceed towards death. On the way, one came
across things that blocked one's path. These things called 'problems'
had therefore to be removed. 'To live' then meant: to resolve problems
in order to be able to die. And one resolved problems either by
transforming intractable things into manageable ones - this was called
'production' - or by overcoming them - this was called 'progress'.
Until eventually, one came up against problems that could not be
transformed or overcome. These were called 'last things', and one died
of them.7
In an effort to simply survive, progression may be desensitizing us to
consequence, isolating us within our desires and delusions. This forward
momentum we value may be hurling us into an abyss that we are unable to
design our way out of. The relationship between progress and obstacles is
clearly interdependent. It challenges the positive notion of progress; at best,
progress can be framed as neutral—a state rather than a process. It is a

general tool we can aim at the world, but we must use other tools, such as
design, to be precise in what we achieve.
We were naturally selected for forward movement, and in a Darwinistic
view, those who didn't progress in life rapidly moved towards death. If an
innate and instinctive drive to progress is our genotype, then perhaps the
obstacle is the phenotype. Every solution has a consequence; therefore, the
true record of our evolution is only evident in the wreckage and memory of
obstacles we've overturned—or inadvertently generated.
Primer
This publication does not aim to critique the general concept of progress in
its entirety. Instead, the goal is to offer a primer of perspectives through the
lens of the built environment—understood as both a game board for human
choices and as an active agent that directs, reflects and often determines our
range of choices. Design is more than just the backdrop to our actions; it is
the physical manifestation of our progress and our obstacles. As editors, our
task is not to prescribe this relationship but to open the dialog. In the pages
that follow, readers will encounter various positions on these questions,
situated within the design disciplines—architecture in particular. While
progress unfolds in every human context, design is uniquely situated to
contend with it (and just as uniquely complicit in its adverse side effects).
So what is architecture? Is it progress or obstacle—or both? Is
architecture an inescapable embodiment of the obstacles it blankets, or is it
a linearly developing scenario of if progress, then obstacle? These are not
questions or propositions this book intends to answer or fully support.
Rather, we hope to offer a series of critical, practical and speculative
approaches of addressing these questions as opening statements to broader
inquiry. The intent of reframing the co-dependent concepts of progress and

obstacle is to create space for contributors to explore how designers
command agency in navigating humanity's path through the perpetual
dystopia on the horizon. These conceptualizations of conflated
progress/obstacle relationships aim to frame polemics, propositions and
dialogs on how we have historically approached this relationship—and how
we might adjust our methods in a world that too often compounds its
mistakes rather than learns from them.
We contend that this volume of thought can be formalized into novel
modes of design thinking—approaches that move beyond the linear, the
predictable and those compartmentalized to a particular system, site or
"client." The writings offer potential shifts in architectural production that
do not limit themselves to utopian proposals. Instead, they engage with
dystopian potentials by analyzing past consequences, synthesizing current
realities with alternative circumstances and speculating on future
conditions. As dire as the past has been, and as uncertain as the future may
be, the design of the problem—rather than the solution—emerges as both an
ethical and a practical imperative to chart a better (or at least, less harmful)
form of progress.
Why do we progress? And specifically, within the context of architecture
and design, what are the consequences of being progressive? The
contemporary conditions of our age are neither clear nor universally shared,
yet we must contend with them. With each step forward there is something
to gain, and something to lose. For every vision of what the world should
be, there is another to contest it. For each work of architecture deemed
"complete," there are circumstances that render it unfinished, unprepared
and unsuitable for changing conditions.
The problems we die of may be of our own design, yet our designs may
be our only possibility of escape. Are the obstacles we seek to design our

way around the inevitable products of previous progress, or is there such a
thing as overturning them in a decisive act of permanent problem solving?
Somehow, we doubt it, but we'll leave that question to our contributors. Our
hope is that these essays will invite further questions, even if our
interrogation of progress fails to keep pace with our compulsion to act.
