1
EX, EST, UT
Wes Jones
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-2
Any discussion of progress will address three things—time, improvement,
and a goal toward which that improvement is directed. Each of these in turn
depends on a prior understanding of the role technology plays, because
technology provides the medium and framework for their measurement, and
progress is above all a concept of measurement. In fact, everything humans
have brought into the world—from ideas to space shuttles, Acheulean axes
to Shakespearian sonnets—is a gift of technology. And technology is the
gift humanity has brought to the world: it is everything besides humans and
nature. Progress is the name for technology's connection between them
through time.
This has not always been clear. Until the rate of technological change
increased to the point where it could become visible, progress was not a
thing. Technology was always there, but time was missing from the
equation so the improvements in technology could not be connected. Since
the Enlightenment, though, and the technological and scientific revolutions
it sponsored, progress has come to be expected, and that expectation has
come to define the world. Architecture's part in this has tracked that
technological development, but it has also been an agent for retrospectively
projecting the idea back into history, before a time when it had the meaning

it has today. The scale of architectural time allows the otherwise
disconnected moments of improvement to be linked up, even outside of
their own technological relevance. In this way the idea of progress is
broadened beyond simple improvement to a concept that could motivate,
and judge, a world.
Ideal: Substance [TIME]
Before there were clocks or calendars, or even recordings of the movement
of the heavens, architectural artifacts marked the passage of time. As one
generation's monuments became another's ruins these erstwhile bulwarks
against the passage of time instead became reminders of its inexorable flow.
Change was not a gauge for measuring what is thought of today as progress.
There was no value associated with it. Instead, the difference between the
city of Paestum and the Parthenon viewed today as progress was seen at the
time as simply different attempts to achieve an ideal presented by the gods.
Pitching the goal at a divine level meant its achievement was forever put
off. The marble temple better manifested the eidos that the wooden temple
more crudely approximated, purging the wood of its non-ideal, contingent
material effects, but it still paled before the ideal. Though tuned to ideality
and purged of idiosyncrasy, refined in proportions and finish, with
perceptual errors overcome by optical corrections and bright polychromy
obscuring brute materiality, the long series of temple forms from Paestum
to the Parthenon and beyond could never definitively solve the corner
triglyph problem or agree on the visually optimal ratio of column diameters
to height and spacing. This was not frustrating though because they were
not in fact seen as a series or understood within a framework of progress
that would have connected all the attempts and judged the differences as
improvement. Instead, each solution was offered on its own merits, without

comparison to the others; and it is only in retrospect that the timescale
offered by architecture could put them together as a series aiming at a
culminating form that is now understood to be the Parthenon.
These examples were produced within an ancient Greek worldview that
was more cyclical, and the passage of time was experienced as more like
what could be thought of as eternal recurrence or repeating cycles rather
than linear advancement. While advances in fields like philosophy
occurred, where knowledge and understanding grew through dialectic and
inquiry, these were seen more as advancements within particular crafts or
fields rather than part of a broader concept of progress, and time was not
credited as a factor in that movement. The past was just remembered or
forgotten, the present lived, and the future anticipated or feared. Works did
not happen quickly enough for anyone to see beyond the envelope of time
encompassing a single work or their own part in it. Depending on the scale
of the project or its importance, or the resources of its sponsor, its creation
might outlast generations of workers.
Conversely, the crafts producing those larger works were measured by
the skill of the workers. When that showed improvement, the judgment was
confined to the individual's efforts, isolating the example so that the
relationship between the compared works did not point beyond to larger
possible improvements that would be called progress today. In each case,
the difference between the work and the form guiding its production was a
closed loop: the ideal was the end, and improvement was limited to the
distance between the two in any individual project. Just as the effort was
circumscribed by the parameters of that ideal, so the judgment of the
difference between them was constrained to known factors of production.
Another work's shortcomings or success might be avoided or emulated, but
in either case its own steps were not seen as putting it on a trajectory

outside of that ideal. Even historians (such as they were) like Giorgio
Vasari, who would venture opinions on the relative merit of their subject's
work, did not couch these differences as tracing any larger path toward
some eventual, ultimate specimen.
The goal in this courting of ideality, as described by Plato, was harmony
with the world as given by the gods, evident in the logos naming the
essence of the thing, its form. In such a world nothing called to be an
improvement on another, even as each new thing strived to get closer to the
perfection of its form. The form itself, as granted by the gods, was never
questioned. Plato considered the form to be not just the model for judging
the quality of an instance, but also the means to recognizing and
understanding all examples of that type. Since the ideal was known by the
form it took, the primary basis for judgment of work in relation to ideality
was visual. It was through the "mind's eye" that the ideality in the
"intellectual realm" was apprehended, so it was through appearance that a
work connected with the ideal, and through form that its proximity was
measured. Functionality, logic and performance were united in form as
techné. While the performance of a thing might also be assessed by its
efficacy, or its rationality by its sensibleness, the final judgment of anything
related to a condition of ideality came down to form, and a preference based
on form would trump a more functional work with less ideal form. When
the wooden temple grew into its outsized marble apotheosis it lost most of
its sheltering functionality, but this was never questioned, and no attempts
were ever made to regain it.
Ideal: Substance [IMPROVEMENT]
The advent of the scientific and then industrial revolutions reversed this
preference for form-based judgment, introducing another standard that led

to the sense of progress understood today. Ideality fell from Olympus and
Heaven and settled into nature, where performance was what mattered.
Unlike the rarified milieu of the gods or Plato's intellectual realm, nature
was not beyond reach. The harmony with nature sought by the Greeks
meant something different to the new proto-scientists. As the measure of
this harmony switched from expression to efficiency and efficacy, human
effort began to know more success. In addition, the church introduced a
positive direction to time, giving history a beginning and an end, and
tasking humanity with an imperative to work towards salvation, so that
improvement and time were now wedded.
Work was no longer judged solely in comparison to a timeless,
unattainable perfection. Now the different attempts were compared to each
other as well as to the standard of nature, and the shape of a larger effort
began to emerge. Efforts judged as more successful provided suggestions
for others. A goal might be achieved by incremental improvement, each
attempt building on the previous. An idea of progress that might be
recognizable today began to emerge.
Nature's standards are tested over eons in an unforgiving environment,
through an evolutionary process that weeds out whatever performs less than
optimally. In nature survival is everything, so the relationship that united
such standards as techné is given a different valence. Where form had been
the standard for the Greeks, in nature it is function that rules. The focus on
efficacy broadened the field of relevant examples beyond simple visual
similarity. Form remained an important element but was understood as a
result rather than a cause. Eventually of course the completely
interdependent relationship between form and function in nature became
itself an ideal for later architects, but until then the reversal from their order

of priority under the Greek system led to loosening of the classical hold of
beauty and its policing of potentially disruptive variety.
The new criteria for judgment were as exacting as any intellectual ideal
the form had expressed, but were validated by actual stuff that could be
touched and analyzed and compared. The unbridgeable distance between
the intellectual ideal and the physical reality collapsed back into an
experienced reality and the direction of understanding reversed from
deduction to induction. The natural phenomena that provided the starting
point for ideas became the end point validating the result of those ideas.
Nature supplied both the inspiration and the test. Francis Bacon started this
process when he suggested turning to induction because he was no longer
interested in merely achieving harmony with a world given by God, where
the tradition of deductive reasoning remained trapped by its premises.
Bacon recognized nature as a treasure house of premises that must be
allowed to lead where they might. He saw knowledge as more than just a
source of harmony, but as a means to power—to give humanity the
dominion over nature promised by God.
This demanded more generalizable forms of measure, which could be
useful for comparison across previously isolated categories of inquiry and
creation when appearances could either be deceiving or even absent
entirely. This new standard became efficiency and was applied to endeavors
requiring the expenditure of energy. Since all endeavor requires energy,
whatever its form, function or goal might be, this meant that the
determination of the efficiency of the effort could be the basis for judgment
that cut across all categorical boundaries.
In nature the expenditure of energy is economized. When efficiency sets
the goal for any performance, the quality of expression and the measure of
functional efficacy diverge. Reflecting this, engineering and architecture

gradually became separate disciplines, evolving different judgment regimes.
One was devoted to quantifiable measurements of functional efficiency, and
the other to nonquantifiable considerations of expression. Both found their
models in nature, however.
Architecture was a canvas upon which the arts and technology forged a
new relationship apart from techné or the church's conflating them in the
mind of God. While the classical suite of forms continued to be the default
medium, the arrangement of those forms followed the emerging scientific
rationality into logical program-driven compositions and planning
organizations. Both science and art traditions supplied goals to this larger
project, with the planning being driven by a logic of efficiency while the
forms sought to demonstrate the geometric rationale underlying the
traditional ideals of symmetry and balance. New construction technologies
also contributed as more advanced geometric techniques informed
stereotomy and steel was gradually introduced behind the scenes, spurring
more complex and daring compositions. Progress was assessed by the
degree to which the classical forms were able to translate the discoveries of
the scientific worldview into the only vocabulary architecture knew. It was
not yet time to let go of those forms, which were still synonymous with
architecture in a way that building alone was not. That step awaited the
modern movement and its expression of the new materials and systems that
technology was discovering, and the culture's movement out of the
theocentric worldview that sponsored the classical forms.
Ideal: [THE GOAL]
Through continual advances in technology across every conceivable human
enterprise since then, and less continuous but still marked changes in
culture, progress has come to be expected as the natural state of things. The

once extraordinary effort put into "making progress" has become business
as usual. Only the most spectacular overnight advance or dramatic change
gets noticed today. The world has become obsessed with novelty and
innovation, but if it's not revolutionary it doesn't register.
This creates a dilemma for architecture's primary responsibility for
placing humans in their world, as previously exemplified in the Greek idea
of projection. Architecture is the greatest statement of human presence. To
the ancient Greeks, the orders personified human value. The buildings the
orders composed housed the gods that structured the world; the stoa
surrounding them were the upright citizenry, side by side in witness to the
democracy unfolding in the agora, all firmly establishing a supportive
environment while nature raged without. Even as that world changed from a
democratic pagan soap opera to an authoritarian Christian dramedy, the
conventions adapted and survived. Eventually they were unable to keep up,
though, and new forms were needed to support a new world. Modernism
extended architecture's duty to place humans in this rapidly changing new
world with updated conventions.
Those changes set in motion a new normal that didn't stop or even slow
down for the new forms. Indeed, the new forms seemed to increase the pace
of turnover, each time leading to a demand for yet newer forms. With each
iteration modernism struggled to keep up. Finally, when digital technologies
came online, the "will of the epoch" reached exit velocity and escaped
modernism's grasp entirely. Architecture and building were fully "liberated"
from the stability and presence they had provided since the ancient Greeks,
without any clear direction going forward or obvious world to support.
After 2000 years of abiding conventions and expressive congruence with
function, form was left to please itself or follow technology into an
uncharted future.

This future is uncharted because the new technologies are erasing the
limits of formal possibility. Between robotics, nanotechnology and
Artificial Intelligence (AI), technical progress is removing the last physical
and imaginative boundaries architecture depended on to describe
meaningful possibility. Those boundaries had served as a starting point for
new work and allowed it to understand improvement over time. While
architecture pretends to chafe against these constraints and in some quarters
celebrates its liberation from them, it needs them to prove its worth and
measure its progress. As the rate of change exceeds architecture's capacity
to react, though, the iterative idea of linear progress is lost, and liberation
starts to look more like abandonment. In response, architecture begins to
spew out work with no connection to earlier work and no hope of
improving upon those examples as a way towards something excellent and
worthy of the title.
The technical advances occurring now should be cause for optimism,
given today's general cultural bias towards novelty. And these current
trends are bringing the revolutionary sort of changes the culture craves. But
the culture that architecture is constitutionally bound to support is different
now from that which modernism celebrated when it was novel itself. While
today's culture is devoted to novelty, that devotion is tempered by an equal
helpless regard for irony that casts all earnestness and optimism as
suspicious. Consequently, modernism's own helplessly positive, hopeful
outlook, even when addressing the nastiest problems, has come to seem
dangerously naïve. World events and cultural shifts have not borne out its
sunny disposition, and it has been rendered defenseless by the postmodern
turn to critique and introduction of a cynical practice into architecture.
Given all this it becomes hard to understand how architecture could
sustain a progressive practice these days. When anything is possible but

criticality ensures that nothing will be good enough, and cynicism forestalls
any attempt at improvement anyway, how does architecture move forward?
When novelty is the highest value and solutions are avoided out of fear of
critique, what direction is forward? When difference rather than efficacy is
the rule for a technology that can do anything, what is better? Finally, when
all this is compounded by the current increasing rate of change that
condemns any example to obsolescence before it is even built, why even
bother?
In this case a general appreciation of progress as a linear process of
measurable improvement seems unlikely. When the possibility for
comparative judgment (to give difference its positive direction) is
eliminated, architecture becomes something more like conceptual art.
Conceptual art famously avoids any generalized standards for judgment that
would allow comparison between different works. In fact, most examples
are recognizable as such only by their label since they share no other
intrinsic feature, and no exhibitions of conceptual art show any discernable
pattern or improvement in quality from year to year. As the Guggenheim's
website puts it, "Conceptual art is based on the notion that the essence of art
is an idea, or concept, and may exist distinct from and in the absence of an
object as its representation." Likewise, architecture assumes for itself an
intellectual essence but has never previously relinquished the requirement
for that essence to be visible and particular. That has changed, and now
architecture must be labeled to be recognized as such, lest it become merely
synonymous with building.
Further, as conceptual art requires no skill by its practitioners, so in
architecture the range of skills traditionally required has narrowed to
professional concerns of economics and construction, while the disciplinary
concerns are lost in the cynical welter of critical possibility. Previously the

skills required by the discipline provided for ready comparison, from
draughtsmanship to design. This state lasted through the first introduction
of digital tools into architecture, when drafting gave way to building
information modeling (BIM) and sketching was done with a mouse rather
than a pencil, in perspectival three dimensions rather than orthographic two.
During the early digital period the relative mastery of the new applications
like Alias and Maya remained discernable in the results even though the
real work was done by the machine and to the untrained eye it all looked the
same. Oddly, this apparent similarity did not lead to comparative judgment
outside the question of the skill necessary for wielding these applications.
When everything started to look like it came from the same alien universe
there were no meaningful standards for comparison—because it was all so
alien. None of it had any connection to the lived experience of the viewers,
and the world it placed them in was unrecognizable.
As the technology improved the skill required to use it decreased, so that
even this basis for comparison eventually disappeared. Now its use is
widespread, and the variety of form requiring no skill to produce has
exploded. The early singular alien world it represented has been swallowed
within a universe of novelty and difference that continues to proliferate
untethered to any reality its authors or audience have known. Each example
seems determined to stake out its own new territory to distinguish itself
from its rivals and preclude any basis for comparison beyond its
chronological priority (which soon enough becomes a liability when the
next new thing arrives). Needless to say, it is hard to understand any kind of
progression among them. It is unclear where they are all heading, what they
are trying to achieve. The practitioners of this work explain that they aren't
going anywhere because they are already there. Comparison is pointless.

Progress aside, it becomes difficult to understand how they could all be
said to be architecture with no way to compare works. Beyond a common
association with building (which in the case of the early digital work was
itself a stretch), they all live in their own separate worlds. Like conceptual
art, sophisticated work today is dependent on an external frame for its
identity as architecture. Instead of a single architecture today, there are
multiple architectures—one by each practitioner. This is certainly a view
consistent with a culture of novelty, irony, and cynicism. But it still begs the
question of how any individual work could even identify itself as a singular
architecture or explain how it shares that label with other separate examples
when its abiding interest is differentiating itself from them. Perhaps the only
answer to this is the simple belief that there is such a thing as architecture,
recognizing some sense of lingering association among these various
examples even if it can't be pinned down. Architects still think they are
doing architecture even if they can't say what it is.
Ideal: Substance [TECHNOLOGY]
Admittedly this isn't much of an answer. Not only has technology sped past
the point where progress can be measured, but society has adopted an
attitude that suspects any attempt before it has even been made. And
architecture has compounded the problem by responding to society's
cynicism with its own defensive variety. This is the point in a Greek tragedy
where the deus ex machina would be introduced to resolve the dilemma.
The deus ex machina was a mechanical stage prop that allowed a god to
magically appear on stage, as if from Olympus or Hades, to resolve by fiat
the dilemma presented in the drama—a sort of dramaturgical sleight of
hand that avoided all the obstacles to resolution raised by the plot. Here the
plot has brought architecture to the threshold of non-existence, where it

hangs in the balance as an ongoing viable cultural practice, apart from
building construction. In the tragedies, the deus ex machina did not always
solve the problem positively. Sometimes it ratified a negative end, bringing
a god onto the stage to declare the tragic outcome to be their intention, often
as a punishment for hubris.
The deus ex machina embodies an idea that often occurs when facing
disaster or stuck in an unmanageable predicament. In these situations when
they are out of other ideas humans have appealed to a higher power for
help. At first this was probably a primitive form of hope, maybe expressed
through marks on the cave wall, but, as humans acquired the skills and
knowledge to advance their interests more positively, that higher power
often also took the form of a new, possibly just imagined, technology acting
on that hope. The ancient Greek's appeal to a god and his answer through
the machine dramatized a truth they would probably have been surprised to
know, that deus ex machina was in fact all along deus est machina. This
meant that, ultimately, humans had only their own resources to call on; even
when they interpolated a layer of religion between them and an answer, they
were working with a technology they had introduced and for which they
were ultimately responsible. This is a point which should be kept in mind as
architecture copes with its current crisis.
The current deus ut machina is Artificial Intelligence, coming down from
the cloud to fix everything. In fact, the current situation of architecture
looks a lot like something a Greek playwright might have come up with.
The protagonist, architecture, finds itself overwhelmed by the success of its
erstwhile support, which threatens to take over but has no idea what it's
doing or how to manage the consequences. The stage has been set to
provide the god with a clear landing zone: the barricades are down, chaos
reigns, and the clock is ticking. The canon has been questioned and the

discipline it embodied has lost its luster, the vocation has become merely
vocational, and the discourse has all but left the field to environmental
science and social justice issues. What the protagonist doesn't suspect yet is
that the god is not necessarily arriving to save the day. It might be here to
punish architecture for its hubris.
Artificial Intelligence will arrive on the stage by proliferating the exotica
believing itself to be architecture. Because of the way it will do that,
though, it may also lead to the dissipation of that belief and ultimately the
fading away of the label of architecture it preserves. As the increasing
volume of AI production grows and the work wanders into irredeemable
otherness, then the architectural belief that set each example on its way may
get left behind. If, on the other hand, the belief remains, then effort will be
required to justify the label of architecture after the fact. And the work this
will require may be what rescues architecture in the end.
In its current state, AI brings facility and speed to the game. What it
lacks, so far at least (probably not wise to bet against it long term), is
judgment. Architecture is ultimately, literally, judgment embodied, as the
record and culmination of the generative and curatorial judgments made
during the design process. Where judgment is lacking architecture suffers.
But unexpectedly, here is where AI might also enter the game on the side of
architecture, even though it does not exercise judgment. While AI is trained
on a synthetic compendium of data, with each datum itself reflecting the
judgment in its production and the set as a whole marked by the judgment
shown in assembling the training set, the AI goes on to blend these
examples without constraint. Then, after it has absorbed all this data and
recombined it in the infinite ways judgment would never consider, the
voluminous output must be winnowed down to useful alternates and then a
final choice made. Here the judgment required is highlighted. By moving

the exercise of judgment from the design process to the selection process,
that judgment is made more visible, and a more explicit explanation for the
choice will be necessary. In the first generation of this process, it will be
humans making the choices and offering well-understood reasons for them;
but by even the second generation the AI trained on the first generation's
decisions will be contributing novel, unforeseen choices that will force the
humans to look deeper into their default, heretofore intuitive decisions. If
the humans care about maintaining the belief in architecture and continue to
pay attention, recognizing their belief must be justified, they will discover
more about the architecture inspiring that belief than they would have
allowed themselves to consider without the help of AI.
Interestingly, this new "design" process recalls some earlier methods.
The generation and comparison of alternatives in design, for example, is
something that remains popular among the larger corporate firms which
have no native design signature or philosophy. This was the preferred late
modern design method taught in schools where the principals of those firms
were being trained. These schools were influenced by structuralism and
design methodology studies that wanted to turn architecture into a science,
or at least engineering. The generation of interns coming after, though, who
were asked to crank out these alternates, chafed because they were all
taught in contrast that design was a strictly linear process, moving from a
rich parti straight through to a finished design. Then came another
generation of architects who matriculated during the earliest days of the
digital revolution. These architects were raised in the critical tradition of
"versioning," a digitally enabled iterative design technique that believed no
single instance of any iterative design is preferable to any other since they
all contain the genetic code of the algorithm producing them. The corporate
method and the digital method together echo elements of the present

situation. The work of the digital kids was a primitive foreshadowing of
AI's current capabilities, legitimized by the economically expedient
selection practices of the corporate firms. Meanwhile the howl of untapped
architectural awareness, from the interns in the middle who preserved the
sense of architecture as a sacred vocation with mysterious effects, can still
be heard from desks below.
Either outcome—demise or salvation—will arrive much quicker than any
previous revolution in architecture, and the speed of this change will be a
major factor. AI is celebrated as much for the transparency of its interface
as the impressiveness of its effects. Unlike the elaborate design software
that transformed design from analog to digital, AI will not require users to
learn a complex array of menus, commands, tools, and protocols that take
years to master and change constantly. Instead, AI relies on natural
language for its user interface. This will facilitate the ease with which it can
be adopted and lead to much quicker widespread use. Further, this use will
happen much earlier in the design education process, where it will be taken
up by students who have not yet been enculturated in the discipline and/or
have not experienced any basis for judging results that will be let loose in
the world as "architecture" without being properly vetted.
In addition, AI's first forays into architecture will be affected by its own
incomplete development. Like any new tool, users will have to learn how to
work with it, whether that necessitates learning a whole language or not.
Initial efforts will be governed less by architecture's needs than AI's, and
this will push design in novel directions to conform to those requirements.
Such a move is not unprecedented: the introduction during the earliest
digital period of software from Hollywood intended to model dinosaurs led
to the spline-focused surface-modeled alien spaceships in architecture that
were as much an effect of what the computer demanded as what it allowed.

The so-called "advanced" architecture this produced in the schools followed
its advocates into the field when they graduated. There it informed what
even the large corporate firms came to think of as "innovative" and
"contemporary" without any corresponding architectural logic for judging
its worthiness.
Even if a sense of architecture survives this phase and AI enters the field
like any other tool, according to the interests of architecture rather than its
own idiosyncratic demands, it will still have a transformative effect. AI's
ease of adoption will finally bring about the end of whatever architectural
expertise remained after the earlier round of digital tools was introduced.
The ability to produce acceptable work will no longer be limited to those
who have put in the time and effort to gain even that mouse-drawn ability or
the judgment to wield it. Beginning at the earliest levels of the architectural
education, design will become a process streamlined into something more
like a prompt lottery, the acquisition of skills will be confined to the
keyboard, and the accumulation of knowledge will become a collection of
browser tabs.
With all design production operating at the same high level from the start,
architecture will become overwhelmed with possibilities out to and beyond
otherness, and there won't be important reasons to do anything. Imagination
and creativity will be outsourced, leaving humans with curation at best and
simple sensationalism at worst. The conceptual turn in art predicted this
moment: the ostensible artist has become an impresario and manager rather
than a creator. AI will be a further step in that trend, when the artist no
longer bothers to have an idea but just issues prompt or asks questions and
AI takes it from there. For architecture there will be no choice in the matter
since the pencils have been long abandoned and all the tools of the
profession have been sucked into the computer.

Conceptual art relies on wall texts to get its point across since the visible
media is merely the vehicle for the idea, with no intrinsic value. This is how
critical architecture started out, relying on accompanying texts to explain
the otherwise obscure moves since the imagery was solely concerned with
being cool but had no connection to any conventions that would signify that
coolness. Luckily for current and future rounds of AI-generated work, short
explanatory wall texts are a particular specialty. While a couple of
generations of alien work has weened current architects off feeling a need to
explain their work, this time around there is no strong architectural frame
like what the earlier critical architects relied on to backstop their work and
guarantee its identity as architecture. AI's users will have to go further to
signal their belief that architecture is what is being done.
Assuming architecture survives as at least a claim by its practitioners, it
will do so into a future when progress no longer exists to frame its
improvement. This doesn't necessarily mean that improvement won't
happen, just that it won't be able to add up to some larger idea of progress.
A sense of progress will of course continue to exist outside of architecture,
in the world made by technology that is judged objectively. We may yet get
flying cars and brain storage. But in architecture there will just be more
stuff. Some of it will be good, some of it will be bad, and most of it will just
be different. This could lead to a sense of disciplinary time that is not unlike
what existed before the industrial revolution. During those 2000 years, time
was experienced as just an endless now, with a past that can be remembered
and a future that is coming, but no sense of a present in relation to either. If
that is architecture's future, then while the rest of culture moves on with its
new toys, architecture may just cease to be a part of the story anymore.
OceanofPDF.com

2
I JUST WANT TO SAY ONE
WORD TO YOU, JUST ONE
WORD: PLASTICS
Nathan Matteson
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-3
Modernity has a problem with progress. Or, perhaps stated differently, the
problems that we face today from our unhealthy preoccupation with
progress are uniquely modern. This is not to suggest that progress itself is
uniquely modern, or even that it presents problems that have only begun to
appear during the modern era. But the problems hidden within our current
orientation towards progress are specific to this time, place, worldview and
no other. The historian Ronald Wright in his Short History of Progress
suggests that
most people in the Western cultural tradition still believe in the
Victorian ideal of progress, a belief succinctly defined by the historian
Sidney Pollard in 1968 as "the assumption that a pattern of change
exists in the history of mankind ... that it consists of irreversible
changes in one direction only and that this direction is towards
improvement."1

An Incomplete History
In the context of the Victorian era, one might be inclined to identify this
"one direction" as an essential drive for geographic expansion, or land
grabbing—the acquisition of empire. Whether the Raj, the Scramble for
Africa, or Westward Expansion, progress over the past two centuries has
often been in the service of the acquisition of land and the subsequent
extraction of its resources. This wasn't a new hobby of humanity either.
We've been merrily grabbing land to farm or pillage perhaps for as long as
we've been human. But the scale of the endeavor had changed. This version
of progress was built upon the bones of sturdier stuff. The economists of the
eighteenth century (Adam Smith, David Ricardo, Thomas Malthus)
provided the early theoretical foundations of what we call "growth" that has
had profound effects on the trajectories of perhaps every aspect of our lives
to this day. And progress "in the Western cultural tradition," then as now,
marches in step to the tune of economic growth and productivity. Defined
as a function of labor, capital, and technological change, economic growth
was bound to require progress to be made across all three. Within this
context, the notion of "irreversibility" that Pollard identifies is a powerful
one—if gains can never be reversed, then they are worth making at almost
any cost.
When the Western cultural tradition reached the post-war years of the
mid-twentieth century, economists had further abstracted these relationships
into objects such as the Cobb-Douglas production function and the Solow-
Swan growth model.2 Fast forward to the early twenty-first century and
these very basic functional forms have crept into integrated assessment
models (IAMs) of the interactions between the climate and the economy.
These models catch flak from myriad audiences: for example, appropriate
regulatory taxes to mitigate disaster are wildly overestimated, or economic

output continues to increase without abating even though the seas are on a
rolling boil. These hyper-rationalized, oversimplified, mathematically
concise and differentiable visions of production and economic growth can't
possibly capture the complexities of, for instance, real labor markets and the
damage caused by increasing volatility in the climate.3 Yet they further
entrench the irreversibility and unidirectionality that are fundamental to our
conceptualization of growth and progress.
This approach to knowledge, analytic and rational above all else, is
essential to our understanding of progress and how it functions within
modern culture. How, in fact, modern culture couldn't exist apart from it—
or it apart from modernity. Given modernity's obsession with the past, any
progress towards a better future must begin with an analysis of where we've
been:
Who can foretell the human course through time? But I think we can
answer [this question], in broad strokes ... If we see clearly what we
are and what we have done, we can recognize human behavior that
persists through many times and cultures. Knowing this can tell us
what we are likely to do, where we are likely to go from here.4
The belief that we can predict the future, and design our way to a better one,
if we understand our relationship to the past is critical to what makes
macroeconomics such a compelling myth about the modern world. And the
lens of rationality that we peer through, that limits and constrains our view
of the world, also impacts our capacity for designing new worlds—and
limits design's capacity to affect change.

A Poorly Conceived Parable
The brief snippet of conversation whence the title of this chapter comes,
uttered by Mr. McGuire in Mike Nichols's 1967 film The Graduate,
perfectly encapsulates the state of a world defined largely by economic
growth and industrialized design.
"Exactly how do you mean," you might be inclined to ask. This paean to
progress appeared on screen just a year before Pollard's pronouncement
about the nature of progress, nestled neatly in between the birth of the now
ubiquitous plastic bag and the polycarbonate helmets that took us to the
moon. Humankind's relationship with plastic was significantly different
then than it is now, nearly six decades later.
This line of dialogue, fictional though it is, represents an apex of
technological progress: the height of optimism about the possibilities
inherent in invention, the unfettered joy in discovering new utility, and the
near moral imperative to make such things available to the masses—even
while the artificiality of it all, the deafening thump of the drumbeat of
rational progress, begins to induce a soulless malaise. It's a classic
representation of the cycle of technological change, perhaps best known in
contemporary design culture in the formulation of philosopher Donald
Schön:
I would like to use "invention" to mean the process of bringing new
technology into being ... "Technology" will mean any tool or
technique, any product or process, any physical equipment or method
of doing or making, by which human capability is extended.
"Innovation" will mean the process of bringing invention into use, and
"diffusion" the spread of its use beyond the first instance.5

Born of a Victorian urge for empire,6 plastic was meant to extend our reach
through time and space.7 Telegraph cables covered in gutta-percha allowed
our invasive presence to be felt across oceans. Industrial cellulose allowed
us to create photographic records of untold numbers of innocuous activities
that still haunt us today. And NASA engineer Jack Kinzler's Lunar Flag
Assembly ensured that the United States could symbolically expand its
territory onto the moon using a nylon flag.
Plastic, as inescapable as it's become, littering everything both near and
far—from the insulation in the walls that surround us to the most distant
reach of seas we'll never explore, as likely to be found on top of Mount
Everest as at the bottom of the Mariana Trench, on earth as it is heaven (so
to speak), as both signifier and material—is the sine qua non of modern
progress. A great future in plastics, indeed.
Before Mr. McGuire had even uttered his one word, we'd recognized that
the diffusion of innovation in plastics had created a hell of a predicament.
By the time The Graduate hit the movie screens, researchers had found
plastic pellets chockablock inside birds in New Zealand.8 Another decade
gone by and the US had enacted the Resource Conservation and Recovery
Act to regulate the disposal of solid and hazardous waste. By the late 1980s
the Basel Convention regulated the transportation of waste around the
globe. Now the legacy of plastic invention, innovation, and diffusion can
also claim credit for the Great Pacific Garbage Patch, drinking straw and
plastic bag bans, and ongoing existential dread over BPAs and PFAs.
But none of these nefarious knock-on effects was the intention of the men
and women who had spent their careers inventing new materials to aid in
the preservation of food, storage of memories, convenience of
communication, transmission of signals of all kinds, and in increasing the
efficiency of energy consumption. Certainly it wasn't the future of plastics

that Mr. McGuire had in mind. As far as plastics are concerned, we've
stumbled directly into what Ronald Wright would call a "progress trap":
Progress has an internal logic that can lead beyond reason to
catastrophe. A seductive trail of successes may end in a trap. [...]
Material progress creates problems that are—or seem to be—soluble
only by further progress. Again, the devil here is in the scale: a good
bang can be useful; a better bang can end the world.9
The future of plastics that Mr. McGuire dreamed of probably hewed closely
to Schön's "rational view of invention": "According to this rational model,
the process of invention is like other corporate activities—sales, accounting
or production, for example—and like other corporate functions, lends itself
to analysis, management and evaluation."10 Sounds delightful. And
productive! However, at some point the sales department decided that
designing plastic containers to preserve food so that we could satisfy the
nutritional needs of a growing population wasn't generating enough return
on capital, so we added micro-beads to our toothpaste, and now we're
clogging our infrastructure with fatbergs.11 How did the best of intentions
become a hellscape of off-gassing, gyres full of garbage, and microparticles
coursing through our bloodstreams?
Civilization is an experiment, a very recent way of life in the human
career, and it has a habit of walking into ... progress traps. A small village
on land beside a river is a good idea; but when the village grows into a city
and paves over the good land, it becomes a bad idea.12
Design is complicit in the creation of this hellscape with its problem-
mongering—defining each and every aspect of our over-rationalized
existence as a problem to be solved. Each problem solved begets a
modicum of progress and, lo, another problem to be solved and, behold, yet

another modicum of progress. This problem-mongering is facilitated by the
proliferation of new problem-"solving" techniques: predictive analytics,
machine learning, multivariate regression analysis all offer a degree of
leverage that can crack even the most "wicked" of problems.13 For
example, the UK Design Council's Double Diamond is less a design
process and more of an effective risk mitigation strategy that allows product
designers to rapidly build solutions for clients while protecting everyone's
bottom line.14 Let's say that another way: the Double Diamond is a
technological innovation that allows labor to ensure increased productivity
whereby users can spend more time rejuvenating the labor force while
simultaneously increasing capital through savings.
The growing number of wicked problems that we face is testament to our
inability to successfully, repeatedly apply rational tools against the
problems of the day to realize better futures. Rationality is running out of
road on its journey towards progress. After all, "the rational view of
invention and innovation is more nearly correct for more nearly marginal
inventions. The less significant the invention, the more the process tends to
be orderly and predictable."15
No Silver Bullets
If Wright is correct in supposing that progress traps are largely a matter of
inappropriate increases to scale, we're in for a world of hurt.16 As a point of
reference, the "fivefold surge" in population of the collapsed Mayan city of
Copan "in just a century and a half is exactly the same rate of increase as
the modern world's leap from about 1.2 billion in 1850 to 6 billion in
2000."17 How exactly are we to go about creating an environment in which
these 6 billion people (and counting) can live? Here are three things
practicing designers and educators should keep on their radar.

Degrowth is a growing intellectual movement that is highly critical of our
assumptions around the perceived necessity of economic growth. A startling
concept, and not without its detractors, degrowth suggests that economic
growth is not only a death sentence for the environment in which we live;
it's also by definition finite (at some point growth will end, likely
cataclysmically if nothing else is done), and it doesn't correlate to measures
such as happiness or satisfaction.18 Growth doesn't make us happy; it just
makes some of us wealthy. Central to this idea is the fact that the modern
economic machine that defines so much of our modern life is only about a
century old. Humankind existed long before modern economic growth, and
managed to build civilizations, create advanced technology, enjoy art and
culture, and so on. The world in which we live today depends heavily on a
growth-based economy—but it's possible to imagine a world that does not.
It may even be desirable. However, critics would point out that without
economic investment (investment made possible largely by economic
growth) into new energy technologies—decarbonization, carbon capture,
advanced renewable and nuclear power technologies—the future may be
grim indeed.
As in economics, so too in design: paradigms are expanding.
Participatory design isn't new, but is gaining momentum in many design
disciplines, including architecture and planning.19 Participatory design aims
to create more democratic design processes by increasing the number of
stakeholders involved in a design process, thus building deeper insights
through robust conversations in order to address greater breadths of issues
across longer timescales. Sounds too good to be true? Probably it is.
Participatory design is joined by a host of new(ish) design paradigms that
aim to subvert the hierarchical design practices of the past. Darlings of the
academy, can these paradigms that are by and large progeny of intellectuals

rather than industrialists—speculative design, design fiction, design futures,
planet-centric design—be expected to operate "at scale" in the built
environment? Perhaps not. But in a world where the average lifespan of
residential building in the US is a staggeringly short 61 years20—ten years
shorter than the average lifespan of a human, and a whopping 15 years
shorter than the average lifespan of a person living in the US21—can we
afford to continue with our same methods?
Dialogue is critical. As a design educator, the need for dialogue is
incredibly obvious. It's difficult to imagine design education without the
influence of Donald Schön's Reflective Practitioner, to imagine a classroom
without crits, a studio without conversation. But once the designer leaves
the academy, they enter an industry fueled by deadlines and bottom lines,
driven by productivity, preferring to demolish a 60-year-old house than to
create one to last for a century or more. The design industry is an
environment that doesn't, can't, won't always make time for dialogue and
conversation. But we must. The German philosopher Hans-Georg Gadamer
held that all understanding was mediated by language, and language always
requires an other—so that conversation is the necessary path to greater
understanding. Through conversation we expand our horizon of
understanding as it merges with the horizon of the other. The trick, and not
a trivial one, is to keep the conversation going.
It may be that not all of these are effective at redefining progress in a way
that sustains us into a long and indeterminate future. Perhaps none of them
are. But they have something in common, which is that they require us To.
Slow. Down.
Extremely complex societies can evolve in the absence of much
technological change, an idea that is highly counterintuitive to us because
our lives are so affected by rapid and powerful innovations.22

We are addicted to modernity. Most inventions are attempts by us
humans to project ourselves into matter beyond our bodies. This gives us a
sense of power, of permanence, the illusion that we will continue to exist.23
It's time for the cosmic pub lord to ring the bell and call time on a design
that arrogates itself to be the world's go-to solver of problems and improver
of lives; one that promotes the minimization of cognitive dissonance for the
sake of optimizing our own consumption and maximizing the profits of
others; whose primary purpose seems to be creating technology not for the
sake of making lives happier and more satisfying but for the sake of
creating growth itself. Whatever joy remains in our future depends upon a
slower, more humble design, a design that rejoices in the problems that it
finds (and creates) along the way, rather than wallowing in the malaise of
paving over them.

suggests that increased economic output, Y, at some point in time, t, is
