3
THE MEDIATION OF DOUBT
AND THE DOUBT OF
MEDIATIONS
Michael Young
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-4
Architects are asked to address a multitude of problems ranging from the
everyday pragmatic—program, structure, envelope—to the culturally
complex—environmental collapse, social injustice, technological
acceleration. An architect's primary agency in relation to these issues is
through the organization of space, form, and material—that is, through
design. This is why, when architecture is seen as falling short, as it often is,
the blame is typically laid on design. This fault can be expressed in several
ways, but one common complaint is a lack of realism.1 The design lacks an
understanding of the real ways people use space, the real behavior of
material over time, the real demands of construction, the real performance
of environmental forces, the real ways in which precedent is tied to history,
the real economies of a client's means, the real carbon footprint over a
building's lifespan, the real impacts on the community that architecture is
built within.
Considering the problems that architectural design faces, it is one thing to
translate a given programmatic function into a measurable area describable

through drawing; it is quite another to address through design how the
pressures of rising global temperatures due to energy consumption force
mass migrations that then face discrimination, exclusion, and exploitation in
the locales that drove the crisis in the first place. It should be obvious that
these problems are of different orders. Some form the basis of how the
profession values its services, what deliverables we charge for as architects.
Others, however, are so entangled with extra-architectural factors that an
architect can offer responses either so marginal or so speculative that the
impacts will only be understood years after the building has been built, that
is after it actually becomes "real." I'm going to do my best not to fall into
defending or denigrating architectural design if it fails at solving these
problems, but this does not let it off the hook, for it is affected by and in
turn affects cultural and environmental conditions. When architecture is
negligent in addressing realities, the shortcomings often begin with how
architects understand, translate, and imagine problems through
representation. Put simply, the problem of "problems" begins with how they
are mediated.
Throughout the following discussion I will attempt to use the term
"mediation" as opposed to "representation." There will be moments where
the terms unavoidably slip into an equivalence given how prevalent it is to
talk about architectural drawings and images as representations, but it is
important to articulate that mediation is both between things relationally
and a thing in-itself, as opposed to the traditional understanding of
representations as secondary images re-presenting a primary reality.
Furthermore, there is even the suggestion that mediation precedes what it
mediates. "Cultural techniques," as formulated by media historian Bernhard
Siegert, establish sensible distinctions between things, altering how the
world is sensed and made sense of. "In other words, the analysis of cultural

techniques observes and describes techniques involved in operationalizing
distinctions in the real. They generate the forms in the shape of perceptible
unities of distinctions."2
Mediation assumes distance; a separation between entities that cannot
relate directly and must be bridged in some manner.3 If the mediation is
transparent, if it disappears, there lies the belief that it has successfully
described reality as it appears to our unmediated senses. The effect of these
mediations is one meaning of "realism." If the medium is apparent in its
material, conceptual, and aesthetic qualities—the distance or difference of
its artifice laid bare—then the mediation is considered an "abstraction." As
we will soon find though, the distinctions between these are far from clear
as they twist and flip, overlap and intersect through various manifestations,
rarely sitting comfortably in opposition. It may even be posited that the
binary of real/abstract is itself generated by cultural techniques of
mediation.
Paradoxes of Mediation
Given the task of solving the problems of environments at a temporal and
spatial distance, architects are trained to substitute abstractions for the
appearances and behaviors of the world. The belief is that these translations
of phenomena into measurable information can be used to objectively
legitimize solutions, like the procedures found in the sciences via
mathematics. This process includes a plethora of cultural techniques, for
example: grids, metrics, diagrams, simulations, models, and language itself.
Much of what establishes architecture's disciplinary and professional
expertise lies in how abstractions mediate design concepts, which in turn
forms the basis for how architecture legitimizes and values its labor. Quite
often, these technologies of mediation are built on operations that compress,

project, or section three-dimensional reality into a two-dimensional plane,
processes which distance graphic representation from the three-dimensional
materiality of the world but are fundamental for architectural design. A plan
drawing is an abstract horizontal section cut, an image never to be visually
experienced in the built work, yet this diagrammatic notation allows
programmatic functions to be sequenced, areas calculated, structures laid
out, expenses budgeted, schedules organized, material quantities estimated,
building codes adhered to, property taxes valued, ownership determined,
etc. These medial processes are base techniques for architectural "problem
solving."
Abstract mediations, however, also have profound cultural implications,
as the dangers of oversimplifying complex problems tend to follow. The
metric translations of space into locatable, numerically described points is
part of what transforms the world into data, and from this, people, places,
and things are surveilled by governments, recorded by bureaucracies, and
exchanged as commodities.4 When these mediations become the basis of
knowledge and prediction, they in essence are substituted for material
reality, establishing value, power, and influence over the physical character
of the environment. These resulting aspects of abstract mediations should
not be surprising; they are direct extensions of their intended operations as
cultural techniques. Abstraction is not the cause; it is the outcome.
Abstractions visually appear different, distinct, and distant from what
they represent, but technologies of mediation can also strive for an ever-
increasing verisimilitude to reality. In these instances, the goal is to have the
artifice of technology disappear completely, to have it come closer and
closer to our biological senses. We call these images a form of realism, but
to point out the obvious, there is a high level of abstraction in their
operations. Imaging technologies associated with realism include the

histories of artificial perspective, the rendering of light and shadow in
chiaroscuro, the traditions of still life and trompe-l'œil painting, the
chemical/mechanical photographic processes, and the detection/storage of
photons in digital sensing/scanning. Consider stereoscopes and stereographs
for instance. Originating in the nineteenth century yet still very much with
us in the media of virtual and augmented reality, stereographs are double
images, mathematically constructed to match the binocular discrepancies of
two eyes to create illusions of depth. When one looks at these double
images through the technologies of stereoscopics, the images are no longer
out there in the world; the perceived distance is artificially altered so that
they feel as if they are in our eyes. Depth is no longer receding behind a
veil, a framed window, or a proscenium stage; it is in your sensorium. A
similar principle applies to stereophonics and the headphones we wear
when listening to music. Sound is no longer created by an identifiable
external source; it is instead sensed as if originating within our heads,
moving with us internally as a personal soundscape.
For many designers, verisimilitude is desired, for it brings the mediation
of design decisions closer to embodied experience. However, the
disappearance of the medium as a perceptible abstraction also triggers fears
that we will not be able to critically engage the image since we cannot
attend to its artifice, thus allowing it to persuade us to believing things to be
true even if they are false. Be they the seductions of commodity
commercialism, propaganda for political ideology, or the biased constructs
of artificial intelligence (AI), our image-saturated world holds grave
dangers in how it alters human relations and understanding. But, like
abstraction, realism is not the cause; it is the outcome. We have been
training our sensorium towards the realism of generative AI since at least
the emergence of the stereoscope, if not the techniques of artificial

perspective. The frequently expressed fears of a loss of criticality or a loss
of humanism through technological media are misplaced, for the human and
nonhuman are and always have been entwined and defined through cultural
techniques of mediation.5
There are other ways to consider the paradoxes of mediation that may
help the discussion. Considered within the aesthetic discourse of
modernism, abstraction has connotations to the formal, while the objects
and events of daily life are seen as aformal, as not-art.6 It follows that to
move art towards a more socially engaged practice is to reject the
formalism of abstraction and embrace informal material happenstance, the
action of events, the performance of ephemerality, the concrete directness of
the as-found, the shared spaces of the collective, the noise of the real. There
is a wide range of artists that could fall into this discussion. Art historian
Alex Potts references the multi-material assemblages of Robert
Rauschenberg, the art brut of Jean Dubuffet, the COBRA group and Asger
Jorn, and the happenings of Fluxus and Allan Kaprow. I would argue that
this approach can be extended to Fischli & Weiss, Rebecca Horn, Wade
Guyton, and Eva Hesse, among many others. These artists that practice an
aformal approach appropriate and recombine the stuff of the world.7 These
processes of decontextualization and recontextualization, extraction and
assemblage purposefully draw attention to the framing mechanisms of
media, often treating media itself as a found object in the world. Even
though traditional expressions of formalism may be rejected, all these
practices actively situate the concrete rawness of matter in tension with the
abstractions of mediations. Within the conversation of cultural techniques,
the use of language, number, procedure, diagram become tied to material
substrates of paper, pigment, chemicals, diodes, and the technologies of
press, print, photograph, scan. This art operates through conceptual and

aesthetic tensions between abstraction and realism, reveling in how the
concrete and symbolic alter each other.
To bring us back to architecture, there is a lesson to learn from these art
practices that focus attention on the chain of mediated relations.
Architectural mediation is more than simply one where the three-
dimensional is captured/flattened, designed, and then projected back out
into reality. It links concepts and objects, disciplines and discourses into
assemblages. What is important to trace is how these alter concepts,
sensations, and attention, what aesthetic qualities emerge, how and where
power is maintained or disrupted, and in which ways problems become
articulated. Can speculative fictions possess a critical edge and,
simultaneously, "instill radical curiosity" about the world to come?8 Can
they be understood as where "abstract realism" meets "real abstraction?"
Real Abstractions/Abstract Realisms
All technological mediations are abstractions in that they substitute their
artifice between a sensing agent and the sensed world. Yet, through these
processes, they also constitute much of what is referred to as realism. Given
this contradiction, let's open abstraction and realism to a general discussion
of how these terms are used in ethical, epistemological, and aesthetic
terrain.
Abstraction means different things depending on its context. Briefly,
when considered within philosophical ethics, it is commonly associated
with notions of transcendence as pure ideas that lie beyond the realm of the
senses. For Plato these are ideal truths such as "the beautiful" and "the
just." In epistemological modes of inquiry, abstraction is understood to
reveal deeper structures, ones we have knowledge of through mathematics.
It is these abstractions that are trusted as opposed to sensorial observations

of material, force, energy, etc. This use of abstraction undergirds the
management of social, political, and environmental relations through
computational data, affecting decisions of governance, economics,
distribution, communication, warfare, and entertainment, extending into the
most exemplary abstraction of the past two centuries: capital.9 It has been
posited that abstraction is the representation of the modern world,
specifically bureaucratic, legal, or technological practices.10
Within aesthetics, abstraction holds slightly different connotations that I
have written about in previous works.11 Often, abstractions disrupt expected
norms of recognition, creating a difficulty, an estrangement in
understanding what one is experiencing as opposed to a clear
interpretation.12 The term is used to describe art forms that do not resemble
the familiar appearance of the world to our unmediated senses. Although
these aspects do have associations with ethical and epistemological
abstractions as being "extra-sensorial," there is also the implication that,
through abstraction, art becomes self-reflexive on its processes,
emphasizing the medium itself.13 This approach has one of its most famous
formulations by the art critic Clement Greenberg, where "medium
specificity" defines art that self-consciously limits its efforts to only those
available in a medium.14 In his argument, painting, for instance, would
focus on flatness and the delimitation of flatness. For Greenberg, medium
specificity demands abstraction and is opposed to realism's desire to
represent visual resemblances as if the medium was completely transparent.
This version of abstraction, however, runs into some strange contradictions.
For example, take the interpretation that Boris Groys provides of Kazimir
Malevich's Black Square (1915). As an iconic early twentieth-century
abstract painting, Black Square is not representative of anything occurring
in the natural world. In this it opens the experience of the artwork to

multiple differing interpretations, one of which, however, is that the canvas
and paint are simply canvas and paint; that is, instead of looking through
the painting towards an external represented world, the viewer looks at the
reality of what literally is there as a material object in the world.15 In this,
the aesthetics of abstraction create the conditions of realism.
Realism in its many articulations is typically associated with what is
immanent to the material world. Realist philosophers believe that reality
exists independent of human perception or understanding, form and matter
are real in material objects. As an ethical stance, realism finds the basis for
behavior in the interactions of entities in the world, not in a transcendent
ideal. Knowledge, likewise, is best founded on close attention to the
processes of nature, regardless of human access or experience of them.
A major blow against realism was struck by Immanuel Kant in The
Critique of Pure Reason (1781), claiming that all knowledge of the external
world is structured by the subject perceiving it, thus it is impossible to ever
truly know anything "in itself." One outfall of this argument was that
"realism" began to be associated with naivety, and with this sentiment
lingered a distrust of representations that presented the world simply as it
appeared. This sentiment led to philosophical investigations into the
mechanisms of perception and belief, interrogating how conceptual
categories alter what is believed to be "real."
The parallel emergence of a psychology of perception in the nineteenth
century sought to understand the human sensorium by observing perceptual
responses through an assortment of technologies. The stereoscope,
zoetrope, phenakistoscope, praxinoscope, telestereoscope, and the
chronophotographic not only allowed the identification and study of blind
spots, persistence of vision, afterimages, and binocular fusion; they also
became the forerunners of the media that would later become cinema,

animation, and virtual reality.16 The technologically extended sensorium
brought to light questions of how important unconscious reactions are to the
sensing of reality. These experiments exposed a tension between the
"reality" effects of these mediations and the abstract artifice of the devices
creating and measuring the response.
Occurring concurrently, but different in expression and attitude, are
several nineteenth-century aesthetic movements. The writings of Gustave
Flaubert and the paintings of Gustave Courbet were considered Realism
because they produced a tension between what was depicted and how it was
represented.17 The content was the mundane background occurrences of
everyday life, articulated with specific details to the ordinary, but
simultaneously these works emphasized the artifice of the technique, the
mediums of language or painting. By directing attention to medial
structures such as syntax and pigment, Realism used a great deal of what
could be considered abstraction. Philosopher Jacques Rancière would go so
far as to claim that abstraction in modern art begins with Realism as an
"aesthetic redistribution of the sensible."18 It is important to point out that
the attribution of realism here is not that of naturalist representation.
Instead, both the psycho-perceptual machines and these nineteenth-century
aesthetic movements sought to increase the tension between sense and
sense-making, reality and its representation.
Do we find this tension in other manifestations of realism in art, Socialist
Realism for example? Socialist Realism provided the counterpoint to
abstraction in Clement Greenberg's 1939 essay "Avant-Garde and Kitsch."
Here, realism was positioned as consumable by the masses, used for
propagandist purposes, and adhering to notions of realism in its naïve
form.19 In Greenberg's argument, modern art was abstract and avant-garde
(American and Capitalist), while realist art was retrograde and populist

(Soviet and Communist). Even when the political/economic subtext and
bias of the time Greenberg was writing is discounted, this dichotomy still
lingers in many common definitions of the difference between abstraction
and realism in art. For example, it underlies many of the beliefs in
architecture that photorealistic rendering is populist propaganda while
abstract orthographic drawings are disciplinary and rigorous.
Boris Groys again offers a convincing counternarrative. For Groys,
Socialist Realism is positioned as the outcome of avant-gardist abstractions;
it is not naïve, but instead a sophisticated abstract code that conveys
meanings to those who could read them, and as such it influences political
behavior through aesthetics.20 Groys argues that this was exactly the
revolutionary drive of avant-garde abstract art, to shift political beliefs
through a fusion between art and life. A quick dismissive response would be
to now view both sides of Greenberg's argument—abstract avant-garde and
realist kitsch—as propaganda for different politics. Of greater interest,
however, is to try to understand how these art forms as cultural techniques
link aspects of abstraction and realism into different assemblages. It is the
cultural and political impacts of aesthetics that are at stake.
An aligned contemporary argument has recently been formulated as
"Neoliberal Realism," where in similar ways coded messages beneficial to
capitalist economies are conveyed through realism in photography from
advertising to fashion to fine art. Jörg Colberg builds from Groys, claiming
that many of the most celebrated art photographers today use realism to
support capitalist profits and agendas, with all of the oppressive, extractive
injustices that this entails.21 Although Colberg presents several compelling
points, specifically around the ways in which photographic realism operates
within the advertising industry, there is also something latent in his critique
that harkens back to realism as consumable propaganda for the masses à la

Greenberg. But, to dismiss photographic realism wholesale is to miss the
point of Groys' argument. For Groys, the political and social influence does
not stem from the simply representational: this art is not trying to represent
life; it is trying to transform it.22 The key questions lie in how the
mediations mobilize cultural techniques to stabilize or destabilize
assumptions regarding the appearances of reality. There may be nefarious
motives behind it, but it is to the mediated gaps and tensions that we must
critically attend. Two of the photographers that Colberg critiques—Gregory
Crewdson and Andreas Gursky—use realism in their photographs to open
exactly these possibilities of doubt through gaps and tensions between what
they image and how it is mediated.
There are two hybrid phrases to conclude this section: "real abstraction"
and "abstract realism." "Real abstraction" comes into cultural discourse
through the reflections of Alfred Sohn-Rethel on the economic theories of
Karl Marx.23 Labor is valued not for what is produced or used, but for how
it can be exchanged as capital. Physical action is abstracted from specific
material and temporal locations to become economic power, a key idea for
twentieth-century political and economic theory still debated today.24
Capital itself is the mediator, one which relates and allows exchanges
between things, people, time, and labor. In this guise, much of what counts
for spatial politics is solely that which can be counted. Real estate
investments, scanning and tracking surveillance, demographic
demarcations, climate fluctuations, economic exchanges, planetary shipping
regulation, target marketing, etc. are all monitored, recorded, and processed
as real abstractions. As discussed several times above, architectural
mediations are fully part of these techniques. The mathematical description
of a physical point in space through two interrelated projections describes
both the scanning of the environment by surveillance cameras, drones, and

satellites and the techniques of descriptive geometry as formulated by
Gaspard Monge. It matters little if the interpreter/compiler of this data is
human or machine; the outcome is real power consolidated through
abstractions.
"Abstract Realism" is a less established term loosely associated with art
movements as divergent as Surrealism and Conceptual Art describing
purposeful mixtures of abstraction and realism. Why I bring it up here is
that recent digital image making has added an interesting pressure on the
combination. The most prevalent "fear" of AI-generated images is that
humans cannot tell the difference between "real" photographs and
artificially generated images. This fear is not new, it has followed every
change in technologies of mediation; but what is important for this
discussion is that the most provocative work in AI art—Hito Steyerl, Trevor
Paglen, Mario Klingemann—not only reveals how data abstractions
determine much of reality as currently scanned, processed, stored, and
evaluated through digital sensing and computation; they also offer an
aesthetic of enstrangement.25
The images are often unstable, uncertain in their status. They are true and
false, human and machine made, nefarious and enchanting. Generative
Adversarial Networks (GANs) are constantly classifying images based on
the pattern recognition of pixel color adjacencies, algorithms both
deterministic and generic, operating through a series of yes/no
identifications.26 As the AI searches for solutions through massive training
sets of labeled images, what will win out is the average, the middle, the
generic. We can describe the results of these processes as a strange abstract
realism, but the machinic logic is looking for the probabilistically right
answer, the most normal, most generic answer. Possibly what is so uncanny
is not the distance from our expectations, but the proximity—the

recognition that these "alien" processes are built from the cultural history of
media, which have been training us to sense the world through techniques
and technologies constantly defining and redefining what is real.
Doubt
A standard critique is that realist aesthetics are in the service of those with
money and power, continuing a world that supports one group while
oppressing others. Rendered images in architecture are often produced to
convince investors of the "positive" change that a new project will bring.
They are used to assure the public that the new environment will be safer,
happier, more active and vibrant. Ultimately, they are sales pitches used
promotionally for wealth accumulation in real (royal) estate. As discussed
earlier, they are part of capitalist neoliberal propaganda. The reader may
protest that this is only one type of image making and should not be used to
dismiss speculative imagery wholesale, and I would absolutely agree with
this objection; but, if an image is used to propose a solution to problems
that operate at the levels of complexity we are discussing, it will most likely
do its best to convince the viewer that it is "good"—that is, it will try to
convince you that the world will be better if this architecture is built. And in
this, there is always something suspicious about how the theoretical meets
the image in relation to the scope of these problems. These propositions can
begin to feel like a gimmick, an empty promise that formulates positions
but has little relevance in the real world of lived space.27
Anarchist anthropologist David Graeber held that left-leaning politics
traditionally believes that reality can be redefined through the imagination,
while right-leaning politics condemns this approach as being disconnected
from reality.28 Theoretical posturing from the privileged distance of the
university can be seen as a kind of liberal balm to appease unease, while

conservative politics stresses solutions to real problems through pragmatics.
But, however important a pragmatic approach to problems every building
must address, if the practice of architecture is treated solely as a service
profession it will always be indebted to the individuals and institutions that
hire architects, a position which ultimately upholds corporate capitalism
and, given that these entities and ideologies are a primary source of the
crises that our environment currently faces, is very problematic.
What is necessary are acts of imagination that critically engage
contemporary crises through speculation about the near future. The genre of
art practice that goes under the moniker of Para-fictional offers examples.
This work purposefully blurs realities to situate a fictional proposition as
something fully believable in the world.29 Their critical edge comes from
moments of doubt addressed to political or social realities that the work
calls out for assumptions and biases. These practices include the work of
Walid Raad (The Atlas Group), The Yes Men, Michael Blum, Aliza Shvarts,
and others. In many ways this art revels in the uncomfortable sore spots that
architects experience in dealing with the too real image.
Para-fictional art posits interventions into reality through multiple
mediums: installations, film, video, news media, performance, scientific
notations, charts, maps, economic records, and of course the photographic
image. When Para-fictional art manipulates and combines this material it
introduces abstractions into the realism of the artifacts. However, this art
does not initially emphasize the difference—the seam—between what is
evidence and fabrication; instead it purposefully blurs the boundaries. The
tension such works create produces doubt at the conceptual level of what is
conveyed. Much Para-fictional work is difficult, requiring attention to be
focused and diffused throughout the media. The viewer initially accepts
what is being shown as real, but there is something off, and it is in which

ways, and to what, these doubts draw attention that constitutes the agenda
of the artwork.30
In a way these are lies proposed as truths, something that sounds simply
wrong, or at least suspicious, when situated within ethical standards. The
best Para-fictional art, however, uses the tension between truth and fiction
to open conversations about what it is that is driving a particular belief, and,
in the process, it asks the viewer to critically think about how they believe
in what they see. Furthermore, it does this not by dismissing aesthetics.
Instead, it places aesthetics right at the junction of fact and fiction to initiate
a more intense consideration about what is reality and how it is constructed
through media. Para-fictional Realism is adjacent to those discussed earlier
as Socialist and Neoliberal Realism, but with a difference. All three use
combinations of realism and abstraction for a political agenda; but, while
Socialist and Neoliberal Realism are medium specific to painterly and
photographic effects, respectively, Para-fictional work is medium
promiscuous. Its allusions are to the newsroom, the archeologist, the
anthropologist, the corporate board meeting, the archive, the courtroom. It
uses these and twists them against themselves to reveal systems of
mediation as things in-themselves, constructing both content and receiver
through cultural techniques chained together into assemblages. Para-
fictional work calls out the institutions and their operations that determine
the qualities of fact and fiction, which is a very different question than what
is ontologically true or false.
Traps of Enchantment
There are aspects of the discussion thus far that bring up the arguments of
Alfred Gell, specifically two essays: "Vogel's Net: Traps as Artworks and
Artworks as Traps" and "The Technology of Enchantment and the

Enchantment of Technology." Both essays ask a similar question: What is
the difference between art and not-art?—a question raised earlier in relation
to abstraction and realism in aesthetics, and fundamental to looking at
mediations as cultural techniques. Gell is an anthropologist, and he uses this
background to poke at the ways disciplines, institutions, ideologies, and
material objects construct differences between art and artifact, between
magic and technology, between science and religion. In both essays there is
a significant amount of attention paid to the mediations that disciplines use
to define and defend their borders. Gell is unconvinced by the explanations
that art theorists give for distinguishing art from the things of daily life, a
division that comes down to a series of assumptions about what is useful,
what is complete, what is intentional, what is symbolic, what is beautiful.
Instead, he articulates two intriguing ideas. First, that all artworks and
artifacts of cultural value exhibit "complex intentionalities" that include
their ability to operate functionally and meaningfully to those who know
how to attend to them. This demands a plurality of determinations that
change over time and between different cultures as opposed to any eternal
objective division. It means that the objects of everyday life can shift in and
out of consideration as "artworks" depending on both the institutions that
frame their reception and the interpretations that the objects discursively
generate. In Gell's words, "I would define as a candidate artwork any object
or performance that potentially rewards such scrutiny because it embodies
intentionalities that are complex, demanding of attention and perhaps
difficult to reconstruct fully."31
The second idea is developed in the "Technology of Enchantment" essay.
Here Gell debunks traditional western aesthetics as akin to religious belief
in magical powers. Art uses technology to conceal the labor that is put into
the art object, making it appear as if its qualities are the result of some

unknown, more powerful influence. When the observer of an art object
cannot imagine how it was produced it takes on a halo effect of value, it
becomes enchanted, and, as such, exerts outsized political influence.32
Technologies of enchantment, as described by Gell, can be considered as
cultural techniques. There are aspects here that should remind us of the
disappearance of apparent mediation described as "realism," but with an
important caveat. When technologies of enchantment become the
enchantment of technology, an uncertainty is raised regarding mediation.
The observer knows that the artwork is artificial given that the effects it
generates are in excess of the familiar, yet the enchantment remains. The
question here is never simply abstraction vs realism, for any mediation will
combine these in some manner; what is important is to identify how their
combinations destabilize and/or restabilize notions of the potentially real.
The problem of uncertainty is, therefore, not opposed to the notion of
knowledge and the pursuit of rational technical solutions to technical
problems, but is inherently a part of it. If we consider that the magical
attitude is a by-product of uncertainty, we are thereby committed also
to the proposition that the magical attitude is a by-product of the
rational pursuit of technical objectives using technical means.33
Enchantment is a trap, one we willingly fall into, especially when the
tensions of uncertainty are a key component. Gell's point is not to remove
the world of its magic, but to locate how enchantment happens in artworks
through cultural techniques, not the mystical manifestations of
transcendence or cult-like quasi-religious notions such as genius. To quote
Gell again, "Social relations are the relations which are generated by the
technical processes of which society at large can be said to consist."34 This
approach has implications for our discussion on the mediation of

architectural problems and the problems of architectural mediation.
Architecture can be considered a "cultural technique," both between things
and a thing itself. The questions to ask are not what it "solves" but what
assumptions it alters and adjusts, what relations it defines and redefines,
what power structures it conceals and reveals, what tensions it creates
between abstraction and realism, and, hopefully, whether it can offer an
enchantment of reality through the uncertainty of doubt. In the end, design
speculation is just this, a considered reflection of that which has yet to pass,
and a hope that it can be other than we assume it to be.

DRAWING ON HISTORY
Mustafa Faruki
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-5
Salvaging Chaos
It is September, and I write this essay awash in that month's familiar aural
landscape, where the sleeping bears of the architectural academy rise from
their summer hibernations serenaded by a chorus of faint scratching
interspersed with spasms of silence that can only warn of the first-year
drawing class, already in progress. And, while the sound of pencil tips
dragged and raised is—to the apoplexy of the orthodox—sometimes
suffused with the alternating clicks of mice and hotkeys, the pedagogical
agendas that drive these many and far-flung acoustic happenings emphasize
the importance of representation; a graphic vocabulary where, first line, and
then, not long after, plane and volume instigate communication by making
themselves available for plastic definition by a designer's ideational
projections in 2-D.
By thus establishing a compositional framework within which students
will rhetorize their studio experiments, "Drawing 101" makes explicit the
academy's emphasis on representation, the deployment of this, and the
resulting creation of meaning. Here, we see an underlying desire for
semiotic explication that references Gyorgy Kepes in Language of Vision
(1944), where the author outlines the conditions and possibilities of a

universal representation at the mid-century mark.1 It's unsurprising that
Kepes' 300-page argument for universal visual communication resonates
with architectural pedagogy. After all, the Hungarian-American artist,
theorist, and teacher advocated for a new visual literacy for artists and
designers in particular. An emphasis on universality therefore aligns with
the academy's commitment to establishing early benchmarks for visual
communication, while also occasionally sating a hunger for abstraction that
lingers in our techno-progressive Bauhaus roots.2
However, our agenda of communicating certain agreements to our
students with respect to representation frequently avoids addressing the
foundational progressive anxiety in Kepes' Language—specifically, his
depiction of a regressive "chaos," which he highlights in the very first
sentence of his introduction.3 While Kepes identifies near-historical
turmoil, alongside concomitant anxieties and optimism around 20th-century
technological progress as the instigators of his representational polemic in
Language,4 the architectural academy today—as far as drawing is
concerned—has largely dispensed with these and other too-dry, pedantic
foundations theorizing representation, opting instead to lazily graze on the
moist muffin-top of (formally driven) precepts blooming lusciously above.5
Here, we can imagine an appropriate intervention of the "readjustment of
conduct" encouraged by Meredith TenHoor's proposition with respect to
"tending to the apparatuses" of history. By blaming "technical disruption,
the shifting foundations of disciplinary institutions, and the unpredictable
tremors of capitalism"—all possible markers of progress—for the murder of
history/theory, we avoid these (alleged) murderers in our teaching. But what
if we embraced these markers: what if we learned about architectural
representation through a lens that reckons with history, rather than one that
ignores its occlusion? TenHoor's call for a more robust import into

architectural theory of questions not typically asked inside our discourse
has gained ground in the arena of studio culture, where educators such as
Ozayr Saloojee, Nathalie Frankowski and Cruz García, and Joyce Hwang
interrogate the fraught histories and politics of images, create new
pedagogies for anti-racist studio practices, and promote architectural
imaginaries that extend to include non-human species.
While these studios deploy representation to project their design
ambitions, the initiative to promote innovative, socio-historically positioned
agendas through interdisciplinary "import" appears not to extend into
syllabi addressing representation specifically. But this should not be the
case, especially if we take seriously the valuable admonition that it is not
only "crucial to articulate which modes of mediation will best aid an
architect in an accurate forecast of future projections, but also equally
important to dig deeper into the roles that representations play in the
construction of discourse."6 I wonder, then: What are the possible roles for
drawing—famously positioned by Sir Peter Cook as the motive force of
architecture—when it intentionally impacts historical pressures,
contemporary turmoils, and the advances, emergencies, and "chaos"
associated with progress?
Drawing and/as Reckoning
My curiosity around representation and its intersections with chaos, context,
and history surfaced several years ago when I began to reflect on drawing in
the historical-cultural moment. I believed it necessary to do so through the
prism of George Floyd's extrajudicial killing in May 2020, and the
aftermath of this, which saw broad and for the most part progressive
reactions to compound tragedies—about a tragic kind of violence that is
also tragically a commonplace. These reactions—ranging from curiosity to

condemnation to horror to rage—were augmented by the murder's viral and
videographic transmission in and through a quarantined and individualized
society, which peered out into itself desperately through Zoom-boxes. The
screen, now both communal life-line and proof of life, became also a tele-
communed death zone, where footage from CCTV and police body cameras
began to routinely disrupt corporatized, televised productions of remote
conviviality. Here, Americans found a landscape of basement-cum-studio
broadcasts, replete with appropriate levels of jocular informality and even
sporadic interruptions by children and pets, jarringly interspersed with
footage of the snuff variety.
In response to this constellation of events, the United States witnessed
agitations for a "reckoning" with respect to the protracted and insidious
permeation of white supremacy in every aspect of its society and its history.
This reckoning elicited some degree of allyship in various corners of the
architectural academy, where a culture of anti-racism-by-administration
pushed institutional strategies in response. While not designed to address
longstanding, inward-looking pedagogies directly, such moves risk giving
these cover beneath a much-needed change in demographic distributions.
That is to say: they circumvent TenHoor's call for "import" conceptually,
addressing this sideways (if at all), in quantitative (and perhaps even
cynical) terms while offering a carte blanche to educators by applying the
pressure of responsibility for "progressive" attitudes and actions on
bureaucratic rather than pedagogical levers. These reactions from the
academy to the unfolding events of the recent past have led me to the
question at hand: in our capacity as educators of drawing, are there
representational tactics that we can draw—tactics that foreground our own
impulses to address our volatile and sometimes chaotic present, one carried
forward from a history that is asking to be reckoned with?

This question led me to a note offered by Irene Cheng, Charles L. Davis
II, and Mabel O. Wilson to the editors of the Journal of the Society of
Architectural Historians when asked to reflect on the transformations
needed to write a critical history of race in modern architecture, specifically
with respect to the treatment of historical evidence:
[W]e must ... make visible the ways in which race explicitly affected
the legitimation and selection of the canonical buildings and spaces of
modernity. [And] we must develop, or adapt from other disciplines,
critical methods that will enable us to recognize the role of racial
thought in familiar objects and narratives, including—perhaps
especially—those in which race does not appear at first glance to be
operative.7
I wonder how educators of architectural drawing might push our teaching to
be inflected by this note? Can our commitments to progress vis-à-vis a
reckoning with the evidence of history dovetail with our pedagogical
imperatives? Could the space of our drawings—the "familiar objects"
which speak the narratives we choose to tell—offer an ulterior surface to
expose legitimations affected by race? And, are there critical methods
offered by practices adjacent to architectural drawing that illuminate
avenues for the recognition of racial thought?
Culture's Pull
While one could indict modernism's turn away from figuration towards
metaphysical association as the justification for architectural drawing's
incapacity to reckon with historical context in a coherent way,8 the
anecdotal ruminations of none other than Sir Peter himself offer a useful,
competing perspective:

I like to think of one's position—as a maker of drawings—being
pulled by the culture around you, or the culture that you've set up
around you, and that the drawing can sometimes push that culture. It's
involved in a dynamic condition.9
Cook suggests that manifestations of "culture's pull" act alongside craft and
creativity to ensorcell audiences into consuming our drawings as sublime.
This argument should emphasize the relevance in our drawings of the
gravitational "pull" of our present, in which conversations asking about
dismantling exclusionary practices based in our histories of racism have for
some time been underway.
Yet, this affirmation of culture's pull on drawing is not satisfying for the
question at hand: that is, what is this pull vis-à-vis the racial coming-to-
terms of our present moment? As a possible lens through which we might
explore this question, Cook's "culture pull" paradigm falls short, probably
because his understanding of culture hinges on some problematic
abstractions. The Tyrolean Walter Pichler's architectural visions, for
example, appear on paper by way of a negotiation with a "valley culture"
which Cook associates more broadly with valley regions of the former
Austro-Hungarian empire. On the other hand, drawings by, say, British
architect James Stirling exhibit a stoicism that is obviously the patina of
work emerging from the windswept and fog-filled realities that are Cook's
abstraction of the "culture" of the North Sea. This outline of culture—
emphasizing timeless qualities embedded in landscape and climate—
provides a striking aesthetic contextualization of both drawing and drawer
that is compelling and even beautiful. However, in doing so it administers a
sanitized, broad-strokes painting of culture in which both longstanding and
granular aspects of history—particularly the more inconvenient bits that

might disturb the picture-perfect alignment of the scene—are rendered
invisible.
Although it's tempting to dismiss Cook's understanding of our
representational culture and its "pull" as far too fast and loose, his
dehistoricized portrayal of this makes sense when we consider it in the
context of architectural representation and its imbrications with 20th-
century tendencies of a universalizing, metaphysically charged modernist
visual language so profusely posited by Kepes in 1944. Seen in this light,
the proclivity for abstraction makes sense, and for multiple reasons.10 After
all, it's through this modality that architects make sense of most things, to
ourselves and the worlds we encounter. This is partly because abstraction
allows us to gloss over aspects that we deem to be "given" or "typical"—
that is, not requiring representation. By liberating us from having to simply
represent, abstraction allows us to bypass imaging what we regard as
mundane (in our history or otherwise) and engage instead with the
challenge of communicating a sublime "higher truth"—architectural ideas
that require architectural drawings because any other language is
insufficient.
But if, as literary critic and philosopher Fredric Jameson famously tells
us, "History is what hurts, it is what refuses desire and sets inexorable limits
to individual as well as collective praxis," then the initial curb appeal of
Cook's abstracted cultural pull is somewhat degraded, inasmuch as it offers
both the drawing's author and audience a visually transmitted absolution.11
Cook's circumvention of history—inherited from an intoxication with
technical advancement-seen-as-progress that finds kindred spirits in
modernist antecedents from Moholy-Nagy to Kepes to Gropius at Dessau12
—renders the drawing reduced: innocent, eternal, and therefore unequal to
the formulation of a response to the near-historical "chaos" that, for Kepes,

was a call to arms, and whose response could be instantiated through
representation.
How then might we inflect our representation's cultural bend towards a
history that "refuses desire"? In her narration of the transatlantic slave
trade's unfinished past, Saidiya Hartman outlines an understanding of this
past that is instructive:
If slavery exists as an issue in the political life of black America, it is
not because of an antiquarian obsession with bygone days or the
burden of a too-long memory, but because black lives are still
imperiled and devalued by a racial calculus and a political arithmetic
that were entrenched centuries ago. This is the afterlife of slavery—
skewed life chances, limited access to health and education, premature
death, incarceration and impoverishment. I, too, am the afterlife of
slavery.13
While our cultural moment is pulling our drawing to look at our present, the
disruptive realities of this present are weighed down by a deeply embedded
history. It follows that a serious attention to our culture and the pulls of
progress must be projected through the lens of our history and its afterlives,
not outside of these; we must reflect on these as part of culture's pull. The
chaos of our moment is impelling us to reckon with—to draw—a literal,
contested, and damaging history in which racist violence has been (and is)
so given and so typical that to simply state that Black life matters amounts
to a politically charged and radical act. In this fraught scenario, our
drawings should reckon with the dysfunctional limitations of our history
and its presently occurring afterlives because these constitute our culture.
We should challenge our drawings to give these afterlives a voice, in the
same way that Pichler's imagery recalls stridently—for Sir Peter—the

culture of the valley. If Cook insists on the projection of our culture in
drawing, how might the evidence offered by our history and its afterlives
likewise be insisted into our representation? And could this insistence offer
us a drawn reckoning, or at least a step towards one?
Surviving Abstraction
In his description of Pichler's work, Cook elaborates on the former's
preoccupation with "the predicament of 'the body' ... whereby the soil, the
artifact, the bones ... and the threatened flesh become immersed in a series
of necrogenic speculations." Cook connects Pichler's joinery of
architectural elements and bones to—of course—"a very particular
'Austrian' mode of racing the pencil across the paper ... a form of attack"
that finds kindred spirits in the work of other regional practitioners.14
However, Cook's illumination of the surface of Pichler's drawing-cum-
ossuary points me not towards geography or local culture; instead, I see
them as in dialogue with the viscerally abstracted history that the artist
Torkwase Dyson names as a lodestone in her paintings, which represent
"the geographies, infrastructures and architectures that have structured and
delimited Black life." For Dyson, this visceral abstraction
prioritizes meaning that comes from living deeply in sensoria ...
whether it's the everyday act of loving the beauty we make,
transcendental meditation, or something more extreme, abstraction
helps me work the ins and outs of it all ... the art objects need to bring
new sites into ongoing freedoms but also create additional accounts
for our history.15
Dyson's meditations uncover obscured accounts of our history, which in
turn stimulate a "sensory threshold" whose appearance on the page is both

wraith and revelation—a ghost of history un-invisiblized in a "moment
when race fails to sustain its stability."16 The work challenges embedded
hierarchies by making them visible. In so doing, Dyson makes unstable a
world that is shadowed by the transatlantic trade in human bodies, a trade
whose promulgation and afterlife were/are "built to undermine" the very
senses she resuscitates through abstraction.17 This resonates deeply with
Marie-Louise Richards' discussion of the complementary workings of
"invisibility" and "hyper-visibility" in the constitution of race, where this
"is mediated by the work of abstraction, and establishes the double function
of race ... a poetics of race, a relation of form to matter." A tracing of this
"poetics of invisibility" reflects "a desire to put a discipline and practice of
architecture 'within reach',"18 inasmuch as it searches for racial tendencies
not in difference but in covert definitions embedded in whiteness,
abstractions that give shape to race. Dyson's space paintings present a
challenge to this race-making abstraction by offering a new language in
response, one in which "our ancestors' innovative means of self-liberation
... create new ideas that move us into freedom, now":
Part of my work is to know what came before me, what I inherit ...
My goal is to take on things that have already been practiced by Black
bodies and invent a form for them, so someone like you can come in
and take it further. This is abstraction as a form, a position, a politic,
which carries a deep intention to bring about revolutionary positions.
It's surviving abstraction, through abstraction.19
It is a useful way of working for architects in particular because it draws out
radical environmental histories in spatial terms: for example, in the
exhibition 1919: Black Water, Dyson generates a historical mapping of the
"hot" and "cold" zones whose specter and invisible demarcation in the

waters of Lake Michigan led to the drowning of Eugene Williams and the
subsequent Chicago "race riots" in the summer of 1919. Here, Dyson
deploys her praxis of "black compositional thought"—where an
understanding of "waterways, architecture and geographies" as formulated
by actions and limitations placed on black bodies instigates a search for
other abstractions pointing away from limitation, towards liberation that is
all about feeling.20
Dyson has emphasized the need for a revised black compositional
thought in drawing our responses to
increasing water levels, land being continuously exhausted, climate
migration, and global warming ... It's a matter of thinking about ...
representation as function, as tool, and as a new kind of visual thinking
that has to do with the agency of space and people taking that space to
liberate themselves.21
Dyson's leitmotif of "bird and lava"—where the latter evinces human-
sponsored forces of tremendous state change that compel the former to
develop vectors for securing survival—demonstrates this new kind of
thinking. The paintings "undrown" the histories of submerged towns
flooded by rapacious extraction and racialized displacement.22 By
abstracting the black body into a cipher through which threat and freedom
are in constant tension, the works find resonance in the architecture-and/as-
bone imaginings of Walter Pichler—whose "brooding" speculations
succeed in so enchanting Peter Cook.
Spatial Detectives
While Torkwase Dyson unveils historical progress through a visceral
abstraction, equally compelling precedents for an architectural drawing that

reckons with history exist in the realm of figuration. Stefano Boeri has
pointed to the "excess" of architectural surveys mapping urban
morphologies as a political act: conceptualized thus, representation
performs as evidence, propping up political agendas and rendering its
creators as "spatial detectives" who possess "the chance to be a political
actor. The extent of their political role depends on the reticence they have in
sharing ... the results of their work in the field."23 This outline has affinities
with the urban recordings of Chinese-American painter Martin Wong,
whose canvases poignantly reconstruct scenes of ruin and outposts of
resilience on New York City's Lower East Side. Wong, who described
himself as "the human instamatic," fits comfortably in this role of spatial
detective—but his scenographies of 1980s' New York are less a mimetic
exercise and more an analytical one, with each work communicating in
coded vocabularies that include astrology, graffiti, newspaper headlines,
and sign language. Rather than clarifying, Wong's detective work evinces
"an artist in an unending process of analyzing and interpreting his
environment, searching for points of access ... that are resonant with his
own experience."24 Like Dyson's paintings, Wong invents a critical-
historical (and personal) narrative code to intimate architecture's less told
lives, thus breaking open hermetically sealed accounts of history.
In her ink and watercolor drawings, Jennifer May Reiland carries forward
Wong's scenography, but reinvents Boeri's spatial detection as both
illuminating chronicle and historiography: Reiland's scenes not only
unearth and recount the layered histories of their protagonists; they also
challenge historical telling inasmuch as the drawings offer these copiously
spoken-for figures an alternative voice. Thus, the scenes of torture and
psychic ostracism endured by Justine in the Marquis de Sade's 1791 novel
capture, through Reiland's unfolding fiction told in vertical perspective, the

non-fictional violence-as-schadenfreude that historically marks as its site
the female body.25 Reiland's canvases draw from an unapologetically
adulterated well of past events, one compromised by grand historical
narratives as well as fragments of lore, superstition, and fablulation. In
Diana in Paris, the Princess of Wales appears in a frenetic urban scene,
where actual and fantastical events are chronicled in a cramped adjacency
that foreshadows the Princess's demise in an underground tunnel after
failing to outpace her suffocating paparazzi pursuers. She appears again in
Six Views of Diana and the Unicorn, After the Hunt, where her "private"
life as a public object of scrutiny and sexual anxiety is drafted through the
prism of The Hunt for the Unicorn, a series of early 16th-century Dutch
tapestries loaded with patriarchal allegory. And it is this suppressive
patriarchy that, in these and other accounts of Princess Diana's life, Reiland
impresses with architectural load—the stone and mortar of Kensington
Palace, Balmoral Castle, the Ritz hotel, and even an informal tent for the
press all share a heavy presence that record historical pressures impacting
the figural tale. The scenes position patriarchal violence inside the limits of
a familiar and usually canonical architecture that is—by implication—
always staid and rationalizing: Reiland's work reminds us how just the
image of the buildings we draw can act as both symbol and haunt.
Parallel Histories
In an essay about the imbrications of domesticity and spatial configuration,
Robin Evans implements an analysis of architectural interiority that
positions drawings of a building alongside art historical examples
representative of that building's temporal context.26 This analysis is useful
because it centers an ocular conflation of architectural drawing and figural
image, where Evans relies on the latter as a supplementary descriptor for

latent cultural tendencies unseen in architectural representation alone.
Thinking on this, I am both returned to the preceding overlay of Richards
and hyper-visible invisibility and also pulled towards Curry Hackett's
experiments with artificial intelligence (AI) in the imaging of spatial
moments drawn from an other, possible present. Hackett's verdant scenes
center the spaces of this alternative now in a context of black joy,
conservation, and eco-human coexistence—cultural loci arrived at via a
road untaken by history. As such, his prompts imply an absence in our own
reality that an "artificial" world urgently compels us to confront: Evans'
search for the revelation of cultural cues lost in drawing is here inverted, as
our own cognizance of the hand of AI pushes us to likewise record the
image's rhetorical anti-veracity. Hackett generates architectural imagery in
the tradition of challenges to space as seen in, for example, We Know How
To Order, a 2015 performance designed by Bryony Roberts and
choreographed by Asher Waldron of the South Shore Drill Team in
Chicago. The performance, which enters an all-black military drill and
dance team into the plaza of Mies van der Rohe's 1950 Federal Center,
instigates a sensorial undoing of the otherwise highly "rational" space it
occupies—a reaction likewise elicited by Hackett's AI experiments, which,
through the jarring representation of an alternative history, demand an
alternative telling of history outright.
For Hackett, his images
[make] a case for the so-called mundane, especially as it pertains to
Black ordinary life, to be a legitimate area of study in architecture and
design. To get us to think more expansively about the terms that we
use to describe and qualify and represent architecture.27

This ambitious agenda gets to the core of Cheng, Davis, and Wilson's call
for new methodologies that "make visible the ways in which race explicitly
affected the legitimation and selection of ... the spaces of modernity."
Hackett's relentless markings of quotidian black life not only emphasize the
absence of these from our typical view; his scenes also question the logics
that support this void, including a "canon" that sees black space as
fundamentally exterior to architectural discourse.
Of course, with new technologies come newer and more ominous pitfalls,
and artificial intelligence is no different, something unpacked by
Frankowski and García in a recent article, under the subheading
"Cowardice and AI." They warn of complacency, especially inside of an
architectural discourse whose sui generis combination of proclivities for
tech-rubbernecking and narcissism will naturally silo discussions around AI
inside of existing debates about "authorship" and conditions of labor that
seem to always be in "crisis"—while AI's contributions to weapons and
policing, for example, are left unconsidered.28 Hackett, whose reels must
take shape after sometimes hours of massaging prompts, is no doubt aware
of the challenges AI poses to the image making and the rapidly evolving
applications and workarounds that allow for images to be evermore
seductive. One hopes that, as his and similar experiments with these images
continue, a layer of criticality emerges with respect to the tools behind their
generation.
Bearing Witness
"What is the correct response to a ruinous history?"
Asked rhetorically and, in part, ironically, Zadie Smith's question is
prompted not only by the drawings of Kara Walker that detail meticulously
and without decorum the day-to-day violence of enslavement in the United

States, but also by the response to these drawings. Puzzled by criticisms of
prurience and sadism directed at Walker's imagery, in particular its
dramatization of an everyday that is simultaneously humdrum and
catastrophic in its relentless unfolding, Smith wonders at the alternative. As
architects charged with the education of drawing, we should wonder the
same. How exactly should we draw architecture in the context of our
history—and its relentless pressures on our present—correctly? Smith
concludes that Walker's task as a maker of drawings is, and should be:
To gather all the ruins of her own, and our, history—everything abject
and beautiful, oppressive and freeing, scatalogical and sexual, holy and
unholy—into one place, without attempting perfect alignment, without
needing to be seen to be good.29
Perhaps the first step towards reckoning with our history and its afterlives
in our drawings is to accept an outcome that is not just misaligned or
uncomfortable, but one in which we ourselves—and all of our notions
around "progress"—do not necessarily emerge as perfect, or even good. To
this end, we should resist "abstraction" when it appears as a cheap and
facile handmaid for petrified, prudish decorum; and, likewise, we should
challenge tendencies for "figuration" that liberate us from discomfort by
way of architectural representational politeness, where an abrogation of
history (that is either convenient or desirable) is conflated with architectural
convention in exchange for absolution. If our culture is pulling our
drawings to reckon with our history, then perhaps representation itself could
be liberated from decorum, so that our drawings can begin to import
questions and agendas that unearth the unspeakable, imperfect, everyday
devastation of our ruinous past. Rather than spiriting away discomfort to the
syllabus of a distant sociology seminar, and in its place underlining debates

around shape or attachments to historical styles, I am curious about how we
might—like Wong, Reiland, Hackett, or Dyson—create representations of
architecture that simultaneously represent intentional, meditated
ruminations about the world, its histories, anxieties and the fraught
relationships that are limited and pressured by these insistent realities. By
leaning into these realities and their discomfiting presence—by asking for a
bit more criticality of abstraction, and more veracity of figural
representations—perhaps we can not only add to the (necessary) work of
drawings "that conjure rich disciplinary and cultural references and
relationships as the background against which spatial inventions operate,"30
but also begin to rethink drawing as a tool, more globally: in particular, its
capacity for "racial thought" that Cheng, Davis, and Wilson emphasize as
latent in the conventional narratives of architectural learning.
One reason that Fascism has a chance is that in the name of progress
its opponents treat it as a historical norm. The current amazement that
the things that we are experiencing are "still" possible in the 20th
century is not philosophical. This amazement is not the beginning of
knowledge—unless it is the knowledge that the view of history which
gives rise to it is untenable.31
This rendering of progress is predicated on Walter Benjamin's "view of
history"—one that is fundamentally unable to bear serious scrutiny. As
constructors of architectural representations, can we bring the scrutiny
back? Rather than taking historical "progress" for granted in the same way
Cook modifies "culture" into geography, I wonder about the possible
outcomes when architectural drawing brings new explorations—new
scrutinies—of history's advance into view. How might drawings that

scrutinize history start to foster new reckonings of and challenges to what
historical progress might actually be?
To conclude this constellation of questions around history, progress, and
the possibilities and responsibilities of representation, I offer some
observations by Mahdi Sabbagh in his introduction to an anthology of
essays reflecting on the development of new solidarities with Palestine.
Sabbagh rests on the notion of "witnessing" as a productive tactic, not only
because of the importance of recounting in creating the "foundation for
undeniability" necessary for real solidarity to take root. Sabbagh notes that
witnessing "can also be applied as a spatial and visual lens to see buildings,
streets, cities, and how they operate and behave [allowing] the witness to
develop a spatial index of injustice."32 By dwelling on and thus bearing
witness to history, the practitioners mentioned in this chapter open up a
caesura that marks a rupture in the view of history's advance that Benjamin
finds so untenable. An exploration of these and similar drawing experiments
with history—where representation is expanded into a field of witnessing—
might be useful for students and educators of architectural drawing who
hope to import progressive visions that not just respond to culture's pull
around chaos and reckoning, but that also begin to scrutinize an
understanding of progress imaged uncritically as "historical norm."

1. Kepes' ocularcentric exhortations in 1944 are certainly a product of the
ashes of (World) war in which they were written and, as such, contain
a utopian optimism for universalism through (World) vision. But his
book is also a container for distinctively Western modernist notions of
technical progress, abstraction, and iconography—these distilled

through developments in the early Bauhaus which were further
developed later, primarily in Chicago. Gyorgy Kepes, The Language of
Vision (Chicago: Paul Theobold, 1969), 13-14. ⏎
2. In the opening paragraphs of his essay "Fear of the Mediated Image,"
Michael Young isolates a distinction layered over architectural image
making in which "images that abstract aspects from reality are given a
place of privilege, while images that 'look like' reality are devalued."
Young's essay elaborates on this "curious divide" through a discussion
of the photograph's tensioning of both the image and its veracity in a
regime where representation is a touchstone that captures both labor
and discursive practices. Michael Young, "Fear of the Mediated
Image," Cornell Journal of Architecture, 11, 2020, 148. ⏎
3. Michael J. Golec's expansion of Kepes' anxieties about this "chaos"
into the territory of psychology emphasizes his work as a call to
action: "Wherever Kepes mentions the need for 'readjustment' as a
response to his sense of an all-pervasive sense of disorder, chaos, and
what he identifies as a 'tragic formlessness,'" he is recalling
contemporaneous research conducted by the pragmatic psychologists
of the Chicago School. "Kepes' 'language of vision' can be considered
as a system of behavior from the point of view of pragmatics and a
theory of significant symbols. Change the symbol and you change, or
readjust, human conduct." Therefore, when Kepes expresses a desire
for "educating man to a contemporary standard," he is stating the
function of the symbol as a means to form a more coherent social
world. Michael J. Golec, "Chicago Schools: György Kepes and
Function in Design," Prometheus, 2, 2019,
https://escholarship.org/uc/item/9sn5p5d6#main. Emphasis is mine. ⏎

environment, its often pitiful ugliness and disorder have resulted from

(Springfield, OH: Collier, 1962), 71. Emphasis in the original. ⏎
