5
TYPES AND SPECIMENS
Plants, Digital Assets, and
Ecological World-Building in
Spatial Design
Sonia Sobrino Ralston
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-6
At some point during my education as a landscape architect, I was sitting in
the studio faced with an empty 3D file, faced with what felt like the
impossible task of modeling a plant. The exercise was part of a course that
develops basic skills to represent and understand ecology, invaluable to the
work of landscape architects who use vegetation as a primary tool to shape
space. The exercise, to that end, aimed to teach students how to use 3D
tools for vegetation as part of coursework and, later, as part of professional
practice. Faced with deadlines and a lack of knowledge of algorithmic
modeling tools, but with an interest in modeling spatial proposals in
computer-aided design (CAD) software in great detail, I decided to modify
and download a tree that came from SketchUp's 3D Warehouse that
resembled the species I was going to "plant" in my digital space.1
The tree I set out to replicate was a spicebush (Lindera benzoin). As I
worked on modifying the shrub, painstakingly using Rhinoceros 3D to edit

branches to better replicate its structure, a friend passed by my desk.2 As an
earnest suggestion to make my task easier, my colleague said: "Why don't
you just select a tree for your project that's available in the V-Ray library?"3
At the time, I didn't have an answer. I had known about the hundreds of
digital tree assets in the V-Ray software's built-in Chaos Server, a library of
assets ranging from furniture to people to plants, and I knew that I could use
them to quickly make high-resolution, realistic renders of a space. And yet,
something about orienting my design process around what was available in
the digital library felt off. If plants like trees, shrubs, and ground cover were
meant to represent my meaningful ecological considerations, selecting
plants based on this asset library made me wonder how this library was
formed in the first place: What algorithms did it use? How did it develop its
plant list? Who owns those plants anyway? And, what's more, if this small
project was a rehearsal for other academic and professional projects—often
dealing with brownfield sites or ideas of repair—what would it mean to
deploy vegetation to represent progressive politics and climate-driven
questions that came from a library I knew little about?
Designers across the spatial disciplines—from landscape, architecture,
and urban design—use vegetation in rendered images for many reasons,
sometimes from an apolitical standpoint driven by solely aesthetic choices,
or with specific ecological motivations. Fundamentally, the rendering in
general intends to transmit an "experiential" view of an architectural
proposal to its viewer as a type of visual communication tool.4 But much of
the computational and representational power afforded to vegetation is
mediated through software. Spatial designers, as it has been argued, might
be understood primarily as software users; the limitations of software have
the capacity to delimit what designers might represent, or even know, and
rendering plants is no exception.5 Scrolling through a library of trees in the

software's window, users can easily place a tree in the frame to add to the
composition to support their intended effect. Unconstrained by real-world
considerations like soil or climate, the digital space of the render allows for
impossible ecological realities to emerge in service of the image.6 This
phenomenon is not just limited to plants, but is also germane to the use of
digital assets of people, furniture, and more, placed to give the rendering
scale, atmosphere, and meaning, eliciting an experiential reading from the
viewer.7 When making a rendered image, it's not unusual for digital assets
to make it more appealing without becoming concerned about issues of
provenance in terms of the digital file, or its ecological relevance.
While this practice is common, it does demand questions about how
designers deploy assets, especially those that use greenery to signal
ecological and climatic politics. In recent years, landscape architecture and
architecture generally have become increasingly concerned with
environmental and ecological design in light of worsening climate change
and an interest in environmentally just futures.8 From buildings coated in
greenery with trees on terraces and vines coating window apertures, to
landscapes of former industrial sites matted in trees that promise to
remediate soil, plants in the digital rendering become shorthand for visions
of environmental progress, not just compositional tools. Through the use of
plant assets on the digitally modeled planes of landscapes and buildings,
designs that are represented with robust vegetation move beyond spatial
proposal to the realm of ecological world-building. By creating speculative
worlds where the designs aim not just to represent a proposal but to instead
shape a more ecologically and socially robust future, designers model and
design the world itself.9 And yet, building in the space of the software
world is still shaped by the limitations, or ideology, of the software itself.10
To that end, digital plant assets do not just adorn the edges of the frame, but

instead also adopt the aesthetic language of plants made available in a piece
of software to signal and digitally enact a lush speculative future.
As ecological assets in the digital space of the render become part of
progressive visions of new futures, environmentally considered or
otherwise, the lush libraries of digital plants are integral to making these
visions possible. And yet, the usage of digital assets in progressive
representations of green futures demands problematization. Drawing from
the history of botanical collections and the development of the digital plant
asset, I examine and critique what it means to click and place a rootless
plant in a digital model. If living plants are sessile, rooted, and intrinsically
connected to place, digital plants and the online libraries that host them and
the algorithmic soils that generate them are also worth understanding as
agents in digital space that shape our visions of reality, present and future.
Flat, Flattening Files
Digital plants are a product of concomitant research in ecology and
computer graphics. In a moment of burgeoning computational power in the
1960s, new research allowed for the digital structures that formed the basis
of computer graphics as well as ecosystems theory that have since
concretized into the robust digital software infrastructures that we have
today. The lineage of the theories of ecosystems and plant collections,
however, is one that is closely associated with the history of colonial
bioprospecting and western ontologies of information. The flat files of
physical herbaria of the era of colonial bioprospecting in many ways are
reflected in the flattened digital plant model. Optimized to be sifted through
at high speeds and held in the walled gardens of corporate ownership, the
digital plant model echoes not just the content of botanical knowledge
production but also the practices through which it emerged.

Since the 1970s, research in the allied fields of computer graphics and
ecology formed the first digital plant models. Against the context of a
bioinformatic revolution where phenomena from human biology to ecology
were translated for the first time into digital systems, cybernetic theories
and systemic organization of the environment were of growing
importance.11 In many ways, the advent of digital computing became an
opportunity to test ecosystems theories that were first proposed in the 18th
century as a way to systematize environmental processes into quantifiable
components.12 Abstracted mathematical models of plant physiology
emerged from this work to describe and simulate plant growth
systematically.
Operating first as purely mathematical abstraction, the work to codify
plant growth eventually led to the development of three-dimensional
models that form the basis of contemporary digital plant assets. Biologist
Aristid Lindenmayer developed the eponymous L-systems in the 1960s,
first to describe the growth of multicellular organisms through fractals that
was later applied to the growth and structure of plants. This work and other
projects by mathematicians and ecologists spurred advancements in
computer graphics to develop three-dimensional, procedural botanical
models.13 By the 1990s, multiple procedural modeling algorithms were
created to generate trees using new, dedicated software. For example, plants
derived from L-systems were transformed into 3D models, as demonstrated
by Lindenmayer and his student Przemysław Prusinkiewicz in The
Algorithmic Beauty of Plants (1990).14 In the span of thirty years, abstract
mathematical models had transformed into three-dimensional plant models
that allowed multidisciplinary artists and designers to begin to cultivate
plants on their screens.15

In the 1990s, several companies formed to create graphical user
interfaces (GUIs) for digital artists in the film and animation industries to
build their own tree assets.16 Formed in 1996 by Oliver Deussen and Bernd
Lintermann at the University of Karlsruhe, Germany, Xfrog was a notable
collaborative enterprise between computer scientists and plant specialists
that aimed to simplify the processes of replicating plant life realistically.
Driven by Deussen and Lintermann's research, the Xfrog system involved
paring down complex L-systems into simpler node and rule systems so that
users could model plants based on general categories, including "spiral
phyllotaxis, branching structures, foliage, global deformations, tropisms,
and growth."17 The aim of the interface was to give users a high degree of
control and specialization of their own plants while making sure that they
accurately simulated real plant characteristics. Understood in another light,
the Xfrog model simplified plant life itself; by developing a standardized
system for tree modeling, users replicated and optimized living beings
through algorithms. Easily modifiable for the experienced graphic artist,
plants could become agents within their digital worlds in the fields of
animation, film, and games.18
By 2001, the company's main output—and its most lasting effect on
spatial design—were its libraries of standard plant assets for ease of use.
Rather than model specialized plants as in film and games, early proponents
in architecture such as the offices of Zaha Hadid and Frank Gehry saw the
digital plant assets as an opportunity to shore up their renders with highly
realistic trees.19 Since then, large libraries of plants designed by Xfrog have
become widely used by practices, academics, and students alike, offering a
digital collection of thousands of high-fidelity plant models. Built into the
rendering engines that practitioners use regularly, an incomplete world of
plants unfolds in the space of a Chaos Server pop-up window when using

V-Ray.20 Alternatively, thousands of high-fidelity models, including 650
species in Xfrog World or 300 species in Xfrog Americas, can be purchased
directly from the company for $600.21 By clicking online offerings or
running rendering software, the flattened digital plant file becomes an asset
to adorn otherwise blank digital landscapes.
Even though the software allows users to place a bevy of plants at the
click of a button, there are notable limitations. Of the millions of plant
species in the world, the libraries offered by Xfrog or other similar digital
plant companies like SpeedTree or Quixel "preclude meaningful variation,"
as ecological game theorist Alenda Chang argues.22 Plants from a library
only offer a select number of plants that are limited in their reflections of
the tree's growth, life, and death. A digital asset might include one tree,
frozen in place, that is infinitely replicated across a project to save
computational power. The loss of variability of species, and of individuals
within a species, creates a homogenized digital landscape where the digital
plants become proxies for a community of plants whose complexity cannot
be captured within a single file. One digital tree becomes representative of
an entire species, littering the models of forward-looking spatial designs
with the same tree, copied over and over.
Aside from the limitations of visual variability and the inability to
represent multiple species in multiple states, limited geographic variability
echoes longer histories of colonial bioprospecting. At the time of writing
(early/mid-2025), Xfrog's purchasable packages of plants from across the
world have been created with roughly 200 to 300 plants in each continent.
Painstakingly modeled to provide geographic variability to their datasets,
it's worth understanding how this method of collecting and owning
botanical knowledge has a history that long predates digital computation.
Housed in the centralized server of the Xfrog database in Los Angeles, the

software company inadvertently replicates centuries of colonial patterns to
document, categorize, and recreate far-flung landscapes within a closed
collection. Approaching the landscape as a site from which to extract
botanical information that is ordered into binomial systems and monetized
as standard formats is highly aligned to the history of modern botany. While
having digital files that speak to geographic variability allows a designer to
use plants from a general region, it's worth understanding what it means to
flatten botanical information into a file as it relates to the history of western
scientific knowledge production.
The flattened files of the digital plant library are reminiscent of the
specimens contained in herbaria, where dried plants glued to sheets of paper
form much of the taxonomic organization in botany itself. Since the
colonial era, collectors have traveled across the world to pick samples of
living plants and carry them back to herbaria in imperial seats of power to
document and categorize all botanical life on the planet.23 By finding and
naming the plant, colonial bioprospecting became a means to promote
western scientific supremacy over botanical knowledge, and in many ways
served as the basis for information systems as we know them.24 Type
specimens, meant to be the material manifestation of a plant's binomial
name (Acer rubrum, for example), are one special herbarium sheet that
become the representative sample of its entire species, as well as the
physical reference for a plant's name.25 On a piece of paper measuring 11¾
inches wide by 16½ inches long (30 × 42 cm), a small sample from a tree
might represent the entirety of its species. Significant work in the field of
botany has aimed to monitor the continued impact of colonial
bioprospecting today as herbaria in seats of imperial power continue to own
the biological resources of colonized countries, and colonized countries
have fewer resources to maintain their own databases.26

Thinking back to the digital plant file, standardized and light like the
sheets of paper in herbaria, many of these questions are reopened in digital
space. While the digital plant is curated with a selection of the most popular
species for digital animation, the herbarium contains the plants that were
deemed worthy of collection in the field by botanists on colonial missions
as they attempted to consolidate the edges of empire (for example, Figure
5.1). Ultimately, these two types of files have similarities in how they affect
environmental knowledge through their ownership and production.

FIGURE 5.1 A dried specimen of Melilotus officinalis,
commonly known as yellow sweetclover, on a herbarium
specimen sheet. Collected by Bruno Klinger, June 6, 1970 in

Colorado. Specimen held in the Kathryn Kalmbach Herbarium.
Public Domain. ⏎
While not all spatial designers are concerned about the historical
underpinnings of botanical information, it does affect what types of
landscapes might be composed using digital libraries. Using a plant may
offer representational opportunities, with a clean selection and placement of
objects into the neat Cartesian space of a digital model. But the ease of its
placement in digital space may encourage a lack of reflection on plants and
their embedded histories. In using digital libraries, plants may be placed in
and accidentally geographically transplanted to sites that are ecologically or
culturally inappropriate. Plants grow in particular soils, light conditions,
regional conditions, and climates, to name a few real-world limitations that
are easily shirked in digital space. But aside from that, designers might
consider how the image of a plant might become a form of representational
colonialism. Digitally harvesting a plant outside its context, soil, and
climate is also enacting a type of flattening and loss that occurs in the
digital clearinghouses of botanical knowledge.
The parallels between digital plants and herbaria reveal the core
assumptions of the representations we use in world-building projects, and
shape the futures we build with them. If our approach to ecosystems is one
that aims to systematically represent nature in bounded entities controlled
within digital and physical libraries, the approaches of designers building
worlds with them are necessarily constricted.27 By fixing and optimizing
entire worlds into flattened files—whether fixed in their physical
dimensions or in their byte size—designers risk approaching ecological
knowledge via the limited structures afforded through the infrastructures of
colonial knowledge production, starting with the herbarium through to the
digital library. As media artist Tega Brain argues, working within the realm

of the system means losing the possibility to engage with the messy,
unpredictable assemblages on the ground.28 If the future is the currency
with which architects and landscape architects design, we ought to use our
present tools with caution to shape futures that avoid replicating harmful
environmental relationships stemming from infrastructures built in the
past.29
Uprooted and Unruly Digital Ecologies
With the click, drag, and release of a mouse, a tree can be planted anywhere
in a digital file. As architectural and landscape designers develop their
designs, digital vegetation is an intoxicating possibility to signify progress,
environmentally considerate thinking, and preferable futures. While it may
be desirable to imagine the future with lush ground cover made possible
through lightweight digital models, it might be worth making our
approaches to digital design space less frictionless. The world, after all, is
full of friction: from designing in polluted sites, social and ecological
injustice, or the devastation wreaked by industrial activity at large, the
reality we design in is one full of instabilities and unruly spaces. If we can
design proposals that "stay with the trouble," as Donna Haraway suggests,
staying with the troubles posed by using digital assets developed by an
algorithm could pose generative lines of questioning.30 Foregrounding
designs that focus on landscape remediation using digital modeling
software, we might start with the roots of the digital plant as a space to
represent our unruly realities.
In the last decade, the American Society of Landscape Architects (ASLA)
has focused on developing accessible information to describe and represent
complex concepts such as sustainability, remediation, and other landscape
processes. In 2017, ASLA enlisted a digital technologist to create an

animated video titled Building a Park Out of Waste that proposed reusing
demolition waste from a building to support the development of a park on
its footprint.31 As the site is slowly disassembled, the hypothetical park is
built up. The video begins with the deconstruction of the site, breaking the
building down into piles of concrete, wood, and glass. The ground is then
excavated, and new soil is brought in to support plant beds with mulch
processed from demolition material. In the final seconds of the video, trees
suddenly appear, resulting in a finalized design proposal that seemingly
effortlessly transforms a disused building into a vibrant public space. The
animation overall does well to describe the complicated nature of the
project by pulling out specific materials and detailing the long process of
demolition and park construction. And yet, the trees suddenly appearing at
once somehow cheapens the material friction that the video attempted to
convey. The mature trees, placed without evidence of their roots on top of a
flat plane on a digital model, seem placeless and timeless.
In digital space, it makes very little sense to model tree roots: a computer
would expend a lot of processing power to render something you wouldn't
see. For this reason, most plant libraries omit the roots, showing the plants
from the ground up. The moment the plants meet the soil on the surface of
the plane is where the viewer is meant to imagine the roots, soil, and
microbial interactions that occur below, and account for the roots' depth and
spread in their designs. Without the spatial information of roots, however,
trees can be placed anywhere. In architectural proposals, this might
manifest as an impossibly placed tree on a skyscraper without adequate soil
depth for the tree to grow. But even in the ASLA animation, landscape
interactions are invisible to the camera, and healthy, mature trees emerge
from the site instantaneously. In reality, the growth of saplings into mature
trees would be slow and, meanwhile, the social life of a newly opened park

would occur. Friction between human activity, soil made of demolition
waste, and the social and economic life of the site would shape the trees'
growth. Trees might not survive, or might grow into strange forms
contorted by pollution, wind, or human activity. That is to say, a designed
space is not a straightforward teleology of construction to completion. The
ways that roots grow and change the soil underfoot would help develop a
fruitful friction to demonstrate that plants are temporally and materially
bound.
I am not, however, advocating for developing models so detailed that
they are simulations or digital twins. As the natural continuation of the
ecosystem models of the 1970s from which algorithms for plant growth first
emerged, the possibility of capturing the computational complexity of the
natural growth of plants is enticing to test and imagine future scenarios. Our
contemporary computational capacity to run large-scale, complex
simulations (think of the supercomputers at Oak Ridge National Laboratory
in Tennessee focused on modeling ecosystem interactions) makes it
possible to model not just complex geometry as visual representations, but
to mirror the real landscape interactions that occur in them through
algorithms.32 In fact, entire ecologies surrounding the computational
infrastructure of supercomputers and data centers have formed, where water
and electricity from the earth are converted into energy to power them.
Powering models to understand climate risk as they over-consume
resources and inflict harm becomes an absurd paradox.33 Digital twins aim
to accurately represent the future drawing from immense computational and
infrastructural power from a systems-based perspective of reality.
As designers, image-making tools are not scientific models; they are
representations. And for the most part, our representations—like in the
ASLA video building a park out of demolition waste—are shown as a

straight line from beginning to completion, where plants help to adorn
future visions for sites once they are completely formed. Despite being the
theoretical and conceptual basis for a project full of greenery and
ecologically robust futures, the digital plant is often a final pasted addition,
a rootless tree missing half of its organismal body. Including rooted plants
and not just lightweight digital models is not an argument for realism;
rather, it is an argument to take seriously the ecological actors of plants as
living, changing, and rooted beings in speculative proposals.
Methodologically prioritizing the digital plant might make our digital
models more unruly, but this unruliness is a productive way of thinking
through the ecological messes and their broader impacts on social, political,
and economic proposals we make for spaces to begin with.
Worlding, Cultivating
Returning to my experience in the studio mentioned at the beginning of this
chapter, I sat looking at the digital plant library wondering if I should
change my plant selection. In the end, I decided to stay with my time-
intensive, overly detailed geometry with an excessive number of polygons
that made my computer lag. With graphics card fans spinning noisily, I—
unknowingly at first—began to develop a methodology for thinking with
digital plants in a way that I had longed to think with their physical
counterparts. As landscape architecture education aims to convey, plants are
living actors that designers learn from; understanding their parameters and
protocols is not a simple set of inputs and outputs, but rather a way to
become immersed in a broader network of beings that live, grow, and die in
response to environmental conditions. Plants are not just objects adorning
the edges of the screen to support a visual future-making argument, but

rather are the result of careful study and observations drawing from a
broader practice of situated engagement.
Since my first experiments modeling the branches, trunk, and leaves of a
plant, I have developed my own non-algorithmic ways of cultivating my
own digital plants into not just a library but rather more a garden. Inspired
by Anna Tsing's "arts of noticing," the slow process through which I spend
time with and model plants has become a method to think of ecological
actors as spatial and agential.34 While this isn't necessarily a fast way to
work, for me, it's a way to world and cultivate ecological relationships on
the screen that require slow, meaningful engagement with plants as opposed
to defaulting to the quick clicks made possible in corporate digital libraries.
In 2023, I created a project titled "Uncommon Knowledge: Practices and
Protocols for Environmental Information" that proposed methods of
representing the environment as its own form of information system, where
I developed my own digital plant models to support this argument (Figure
5.2).35 Sited on the banks of the Columbia River in The Dalles, Oregon, the
speculative proposal focused on the site of Google's first hyperscale data
center, where the company had recently come to pay for improvements to
the small town's water infrastructure in service of their operations.36 In
December of 2022, after years of advocacy by groups in response to gag
orders on local newspapers, Google revealed that it consumed 29 percent of
the town's total water yearly to cool its servers—a figure that is set to rise
with the new computational stresses stemming from the artificial
intelligence (AI) boom.37 The data center complex is built on three sites in
The Dalles, including a former Martin Marietta Superfund site. The site, at
the intersection of massive hydroelectric power and transportation
corridors, has grown from long path dependencies stemming from the
dispossession of Indigenous lands during westward colonial expansion in

the 19th century.38 In response to this palimpsest of infrastructural and
colonial violence that occurred under the guise of technological innovation
and progress, I proposed and represented a project that focused on latency,
natural intelligence, and the slow technology of plants (Figures 5.3 and 5.4).
FIGURE 5.2 A top-down view of digital plants modeled by the
author as a series of wildflowers in a point cloud. From
"Uncommon Knowledge: Practices and Protocols of
Environmental Information," 2023. Image courtesy the author. ⏎

FIGURE 5.3 One of the speculative sites designed as part of the
project using "hand" modelled plants. Pictured in the foreground
is a wildflower endemic to the Columbia River Gorge that is
intersecting with the point cloud from a LIDAR dataset. From
"Uncommon Knowledge: Practices and Protocols of
Environmental Information," 2023. Image courtesy the author. ⏎
FIGURE 5.4 A speculative design for the site near the superfund,
where yellow sweetclover was proposed to be planted alongside
other species as a type of sensor or indication of polycyclic
aromatic hydrocarbon (PAH) pollution. The flower is shown
bending and wilting here in an attempt to model its death. From
"Uncommon Knowledge: Practices and Protocols of
Environmental Information," 2023. Image courtesy the author. ⏎
The speculative proposal was future-oriented, but in many ways aimed to
undo representations of progress using plants that defaulted to digital
proxies. In the four speculative counter sites I designed near the data center,
I selected plants endemic to the Columbia River Gorge and modeled them
in detail using Blender—an open-source, mesh-based animation software.
Referencing multiple image sources from local botanists, I wanted to make
digital plant assets that were the equivalent resolution of the architectural

elements that I designed, including boardwalks, bird blinds, and
buildings.39 Drawing from the work of artist Macoto Murayama, who
develops construction detail-style drawings of plants, I represented plants
and their chemical relationships as a kind of information infrastructure,
playing on the botanical type sheet of the herbarium.40 Modeling from root
to stem to stamen, I aimed to show the complexity of herbaceous and
woody plants to demonstrate how a user might be able to read the landscape
itself as a type of information repository.
The resulting work included 3D animations of the trees in situ with the
other elements of the proposal, including data servers crossed with bird
blinds and laboratory spaces as gardens. Drawing from media artists Christa
Sommerer and Laurent Mignonneau's 1992 "Interactive Plant Growing"
installation that was an aesthetic inspiration for the original Xfrog plants, I
painted the plants as a single color in order to reference the history of
digital plant graphics.41 Intermixed with high-resolution LIDAR data of the
site, my hand-modeled plants collided with the scale of the Columbia River
basin represented as a point cloud. Seeing underground to the roots of the
plants, I alluded to the complex landscape processes happening below the
surface. With this mix of digital plants, platforms, pavilions, and territorial
data, I deliberately clashed resolutions between plants and the
infrastructural territories of information.
Each plant that was featured in the project aimed to take on an
informational meaning or purpose about the site and drew on scientific
research on plant physiology. In this way, I hoped to frame the plants as
sensors or real-time indicators of slow environmental harm, rather than just
entourage in my images. On a site just outside the boundaries of the
Superfund where soils were polluted from years of aluminum smelting, I
modeled a yellow sweetclover (Melilotus officinalis).42 Yellow sweetclover

grows in the disturbed soils of the Columbia River Gorge, and is a
hyperaccumulator of PAHs, chemical by-products of aluminum smelting.43
As I spent time carefully modeling its root structure and flowers, I began to
feel my understanding of the plant shift. The plant's hypertolerance of
pollution is often understood through the lens of environmental remediation
—where plants are enlisted to fix the unfixable problems of industrial
pollution in the soil; but for me, its demise became more important to
represent.44 I started to experiment with ways to model its death: after all, if
the plant was tolerant of pollution, its death due to an overabundance of it
was the important signal to make evident. In the end, the animation I
developed was not necessarily one that accurately simulated plant life or
death with a high degree of realism (Figure 5.5). It was, however, a way to
engage with the timescales and entanglements of industrial and colonial
harm, and its latent effects on plants.

FIGURE 5.5 A specimen of yellow sweetclover presented in the
style of a construction drawing, complete with annotations that

describe its status as a hyperaccumulator species. From
"Uncommon Knowledge: Practices and Protocols of
Environmental Information," 2023. Image courtesy the author. ⏎
Since the development of this project, I have represented all kinds of
plants through bespoke Blender models as the central representational
medium of my practice. I have modeled endemic and rare plant species
from New York to Timișoara (Romania) as well as weeds and dying trees
from Oregon to Boston (Figure 5.6). These plants may not necessarily be
found widely or are not plants that are deemed charismatic, and therefore
are not typically seen as useful or profitable in digital plant libraries. I have
developed ways of modeling plants slowly and painstakingly, without the
use of algorithmic software, to try to become closer to and understand my
subject matter more deeply. As a means to co-labor with the plants, not just
in the physical space of a garden but also in a digital format by paying close
attention to their scale and structure, I have developed a respect for the
plants' complexity of natural intelligence and the labor they do to support
critical, life-giving infrastructure in the environment.45 In some ways, using
my hand-modeled method goes against the technological progress made in
modeling plants, but sometimes it is worth pushing back against the ease
and availability of using a digital library that you can buy access to.
Developing plant models has helped me question what roles plants play in
envisioning the future not just as a progressive, green teleology, but rather
something messy, complicated, and rooted in my own reality. Instead, the
act of modeling has allowed me to think through what kinds of progress and
futures are possible if we show the frictions that lie in plants' broader
environmental, social, and political contexts.

FIGURE 5.6 A field of wildflowers and weeds, with species
endemic to the Columbia River Gorge. From "Uncommon
Knowledge: Practices and Protocols of Environmental
Information," 2023. Image courtesy the author. ⏎
Coda: Types and Specimens
In another studio space, but this time as an educator, I worked with
landscape architecture students at the University of Pennsylvania who were
interested in digital animation to represent their speculative design
proposals. Many of them were eager to delve into the world of plants, or at
least to represent them in ways that complemented their visual
representations of environmentally just futures, climate-oriented designs,
and progressive visions of worlds that could be. Sitting in a windowless
basement room, graphics cards fans whirring in unison, I introduced
students to the slow methodology of modeling plants that I had used myself
in years prior in a class called "Types and Specimens: Representing the
Situated Information of Plants through Digital Media."46 While students
opened Blender for the first time, they were also reading about the history
of botanical information systems and the ways that plant libraries arrived in
their Rhinoceros window. Together, over the course of a semester, students

started to model their own digital ecologies, from weeds in Philadelphia, to
flowers blooming in their grandparents' gardens, to aquatic plants swaying
in the currents of Florida's Lake Okeechobee. As we moved through the
semester, students began to learn animation skills, but also learned to think
intimately at the scale of the plants and the situated information embedded
within them as the starting point for visions of progress otherwise.
As a designer and now as an educator, representing the messiness and
complexity of the environment on screens is not a pursuit of realism, but
rather a way to encourage ecological world-building that takes seriously the
different actors we enlist in our narratives. If the history of digital plants is
inflected with a systems worldview growing from long-standing
relationships to botanical information systems like herbaria and ecosystems
modeling, our visions of designed futures are necessarily framed by and
embedded in these systems. We don't have to do away with digital plant
libraries altogether—in fact, these libraries are quite useful to save time and
resources—but we do have to carefully consider how these models shape
our capacity to imagine what's real and what's possible. By developing a
close relationship to plants by touching them in the field and touching them
with our cursors on our screen, we might help create progressive visions for
the future that aren't just a blanket of green digital proxies, but rather
oriented towards the protocols and assemblages of the environment itself.

6
THE PANECILLO OF QUITO IN
1903
Resisting Modernity in the Andes
Cristina Bueno
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-7
Ecuador is literally besieged and blocked by the stupendous progress
of the 20th century. For this reason, it will be impossible for Ecuador
to escape the imperative demands of the civilization of the greater
nations. The inter-Andean and intercontinental railroad, the Panama
Canal, the opening of trade routes to Australia and Japan, the great
merchant fleet from California that is visiting the entire Pacific coast to
the south, an opulent and powerful maritime station in the Galápagos
Archipelago, an endless stream of immigration, etc., etc. are things that
are already approaching, that are already arriving, and whose noise
deafens the whole world. The current generation will not pass without
seeing these marvels completely realized.1
This political speech by Quito's Mayor Francisco Andrade Marín, delivered
in 1905, was imbued with an idea of progress. It implied the provision of
complex devices for displacement and international communication since
the beginning of the 20th century. His tone seemed antagonistic to a

photograph of Quito from the same year (Figure 6.1). There, its urban hill,
the Panecillo, appeared static as the perfect setting for a population with
immutable rituals.
FIGURE 6.1 Photograph of the cemetery in Quito, with the
Panecillo as background, 1901. Source: Paul Grosser, Archivo de
Fotografía Patrimonial. Instituto Nacional de Patrimonio Cultural
(Code: 5505). ⏎
Just like the photograph of the Panecillo hill, its cartographic
representation has been revealing. In the early cartography of Quito, the
engraving of the Panecillo was crucial for understanding the city. Usually
on the left of city plans, it worked as an organic edge that accentuated the
legibility of the urban layout in the colonial center (see Figure 6.5). But in
1903, two unique maps depicted the city without this iconic hill. Both maps
were related to the possibility of improving the infrastructure in Quito.
Omission of the hill was unusual in the cartographic history of the

Ecuadorian capital. Therefore, the void in the 1903 urban representation
was as eloquent as the silence in Andrade Marín's political discourse about
specific characteristics of this place in the Andes. It seemed to indicate that
the hill was an uncomfortable reminder of modernization efforts.2
The Panecillo: More than a Hill
The Panecillo combines a series of geographical features that delicately
define the landscape. As a result of a partial eruption by the adjacent
Pichincha volcano, it acquired a rounded shape, becoming a strategic
location at a specific elevation. In the middle of the extended high plateau
of Quito, it became the geometrical center between the Turubamba and
Iñaquito valleys. Consequently, it attracted settlements before the colonial
era.
From approximately ad 500 until the 15th century, the indigenous Quitu-
Cara people settled in scattered chiefdoms within this large high valley
divided precisely in two by the prominence of the Panecillo hill.3
Concentrated mainly to the south, the more fertile side of the valley, the
Quitu-Cara seemed to recognize large-scale symmetry as a manifestation of
the Pachamama, the Andean worldview that sought harmony between
nature, community, and cosmos. Furthermore, being near the Equator and
having less height than the volcanoes around it, the hill enabled observation
of the sun from its summit during solstices as it rose and set behind the
surrounding peaks. From there, one could observe how the sun "leapt" from
volcano to volcano depending on the season.4 It was a site of appreciation
of the territory, but also a place for staying still to appreciate time itself.
Later, around ad 1500, the Incas, worshippers of the sun, noted this sacred
significance and superimposed their own system of beliefs to enhance this
condition. According to some ethnohistorians, Yavirak, as the mountain was

called during the Incas' brief occupation, became one of their four sacred
mountains in Quito, and an essential actor in quadripartition, the system of
occupation that would transform the city into a defensive stronghold of the
Incan Empire. Thus, beyond just being a landmark, the Panecillo has held
great symbolic importance since the time of ancient cultures.
Background to Progress in Ecuador
The struggle between the concept of progress and of mountains is not new.
The word "progress" derives from walking forward. It implies dynamism.
In contrast, mountains have been perceived as permanent obstacles. Before
the Italian Renaissance scholar Francesco Petrarch's ascent of Mont
Ventoux in France, mountains were labelled "horrible countries"; they were
a cause of disconnection, inhospitable places, difficult to tame.5
The term "progress" in Spanish was coined around 1570, coinciding with
the arrival of Europeans in the Americas.6 Therefore, although in the Andes,
pre-Columbian cultures perceived mountains as the horizon, as refuge, the
source of water, and connection to the sacred; for the European travelers
who arrived in the new continent, they represented a land in turmoil.
Volcanoes, glaciers, and unprecedented floods represented the forces of
nature opposed to the modern efforts to control the territory. For the
Western conquerors, manipulating the new surroundings meant both to
dominate nature and to recreate Western civilization in a different
environment.
With industrialization and the implementation of new networks such as
railroads and pipes, the possibilities to control this territory became a
reality. The aims of progress and its rhetoric were disseminated in Ecuador
by the conservative president Gabriel García Moreno. During his terms
from 1862 to 1865 and from 1869 to 1875, García Moreno realized the

aspirations of progress of the Ecuadorian elites by conceiving the landscape
as a collection of natural resources to exploit and export, improving the
means to do so efficiently.7
Contemporary authors specializing in modern infrastructure in Quito
noted that the pivotal moment for progress in the city occurred at the turn of
the 20th century. Jorge Benavides Solís identified the beginning of modern
architecture in Quito in 1896, when a group of foreign professionals from
various disciplines arrived in the city to provide technical support for public
projects or to join the academic staff of the National School of Fine Arts.
Inés del Pino considered the beginning of modernity to be the year 1906,
when, after the coup d'état led by the liberal Eloy Alfaro, he initiated a
series of public infrastructure projects. These projects were all contracted to
European or North American companies, which possessed extensive
experience in industrial construction. It was also the year in which the local
newspaper, El Comercio, was established, publicizing sewerage, potable
water, and roadworks. The government utilized the media to promote
progress as a form of propaganda; modernization was shown as a means of
acquiring social benefits, reflecting the political values inherent to Alfaro's
liberal government. Consequently, the process of modernization in Quito
was intertwined with foreign experts taming the territory. Simultaneously, it
was promoted as a value that enhanced the political image of the ruling
government, regardless of its political affiliation. By 1903, these complex
facets of modernization in Quito were starting to emerge due to the
numerous advancements in public infrastructure overseen by Mayor
Francisco Andrade Marín.
1903: A Pivotal Year in Cartography
In a volume titled Damero, Alfonso Ortiz Crespo compiled a remarkable
series of city plans that show the Panecillo as an important element in

Quito's represented image. The collective analysis of these maps
demonstrates that the hill has been a remarkable physical presence in the
city's geography and a significant constant in the collective imagination of
its inhabitants.
However, two maps from 1903 omitted the hill.8 Created respectively by
Antonio Gil and Henry Grant Higley, these maps were related to
modernizing infrastructures in the Andes. Gil's map was limited to drawing
the land parcels of Quito, under hygienist criteria (see Figure 6.2). In
contrast, Higley's map established graphic and contextual links with the
railway construction that would be implemented five years later, a living
promise of connection and movement (see Figure 6.4). In these maps where
the hill ceased to appear, the city of Quito ceased to be recognizable. The
loss of this landmark was destabilizing both geographically and
conceptually. The city could only be understood through the strategic
definition provided by its hill.

FIGURE 6.2 Map of the city of Quito, Antonio Gil, 1903.
Source: Centro Cultural Biblioteca Ecuatoriana Aurelio Espinosa
Pólit, in Ortiz Crespo, Alfonso, Damero. Quito: Fondo de
Salvamento FONSAL, 2007 (Code: 915.4 O775d.). ⏎
Additionally, these maps marked a pivotal moment as from them
onwards the cartographic representation of Quito began to consider the city
south of the hill. This shift in urban representation revealed that progress
had arrived in the city. Indeed, according to the Urban Planning
Department, between 1903 and 1906 the population of Quito increased by
2,000, while the area occupied by the city expanded to 30 hectares to the
north and south of the Panecillo, facilitated by the new infrastructure
developments in paving, roads, potable water, and sewage systems.9
In contrast to 1906, the year 1903 was not recorded in history as one of
significant changes in the modernization process. However, during that
year, the potential economic growth of the public projects under discussion

was already becoming apparent. It is the cartography of this year that
documented the importance of these possibilities in the construction of
territorial imaginaries. From this perspective, the omission of the Panecillo
blurred the topographical characteristics of Quito, allowing for the
avoidance of complicated discussions regarding what the possibility of
progress meant for Andean cities at the beginning of the 20th century.
The Omission to Promote Hygienism
The Rationalizing Plan
During his tenure as Intendente General of the province of Pichincha, under
President Leonidas Plaza Gutiérrez, Antonio Gil commissioned a map of
Quito. The resulting "Plano de la ciudad de Quito" was engraved by J. D.
Laso in September 1903, which displayed the parcels and street names that
made up the city in that year. However, what was surprising was the
orthogonality and the perfection of the square forms of each block (Figure
6.2). The city depicted by Gil appeared to be drawn based on a Cartesian
grid of mathematical precision. The absence of topography, including the
prominence of the Panecillo, exaggerated the abstraction and rationalizing
power of the grid as a tool for control in the Andes.
Indeed, the colonial center of Quito, like that of other cities in the region,
was laid out according to an orthogonal grid implemented by the Spanish
Crown. According to the earliest available plan of Quito from around 1573,
issued by the Ley de Indias (Figure 6.3), the territory was to be divided
according to a perfectly regular and homogeneous grid based on square
blocks of 500 feet (150 meters) on each side, which in turn had to be
subdivided to be allocated to a colonist.10 This grid served as a model for
road and parcel layout, and was the first rationalist method to efficiently

divide the territory into small portions controlled by a Spanish owner.11
However, the execution of this ideal in the rugged topography of Andean
cities was either problematic or outright impossible. The deep ravines, hills,
mountains, and volcanoes rendered this type of diagram indifferent to the
context. This demonstrated the Spanish Crown's ignorance of the conquered
territory in the New World, ruling from a distance.
FIGURE 6.3 Diagram of the city of Quito, Anonymous, circa
1573. Source: Mapoteca Histórica de Límites, Cartografía y
Límites, Unidad Técnica de la Dirección de Relaciones Vecinales
y Soberanías, Subsecretaría de América Latina y el Caribe del
Ministerio de Relaciones Exteriores y Movilidad Humana. ⏎
Just like in the anonymous plan of 1573, the quasi-Platonic grid of Gil's
map seemed artificial due to its complete omission of topography. The
ravines breaking the surface, the slopes of the Pichincha volcano to the east
of the city, and the Panecillo to the south were all missing. Consequently,

Gil's map appeared to represent a simple plan. And, like all plans in the
Americas, it was a contract to civilize the territory; everything that was not
mapped was understood as wild.12 Here, the cartographer interpreted the
topography, including the Panecillo, as part of the wilderness that should
not be considered under the progressive gaze of a 20th-century city. In fact,
Gil's plan exhibited characteristics typical of a diagrammatic scheme of
intentions; in his cartographic compilation of Quito, Alfonso Ortiz
highlights "its small format, the lack of scale indication, and the
simplification of details."13 Thus, rather than an urban survey, Gil's map
represented a Platonic ideal of an orderly city. As a scheme aspiring to
perfection from an illusory starting point, it functioned as a model.
The Hygienist Plan
Antonio Gil's grid was not only rationalist but also contained a hygienist
aspiration as part of an urban modernization strategy. This theme was
visible through the title of the plan, which specified "for the use of the
Police Force." Within the plan, the only institutions that appeared with
names were those of the Clergy and the Police, the latter appearing in
capital letters in the southern corner of the lot belonging to the Convent of
San Francisco.
European hygienist processes were initially associated with
industrialization to apply newly acquired knowledge in health matters. They
had to do with concerns about the quality of working conditions for factory
workers. In contrast, with incipient industrialization in Andean cities, the
reinterpretation of these processes related to the beautification of the city
and the people. This is why, in the Andes, hygienism took on a tint of social
cleanliness. From the beginning of the colony, hygienism was in the hands
of the Clergy, and later, until the 19th century, it came under the control of

the Police - the institution that was initially responsible for urban and social
beauty.14 Therefore, in a corporative way, it ensured that streets and squares
were clean and that it was the indigenous people who cleaned them. The
Police also identified individuals who deviated from the conservative
dictates of the city, succumbing to temptation outside of morality, such as
alcohol or gambling, as well as those who fell outside health dictates, such
as lepers.15 Similarly, the Police supervised personal and moral cleanliness,
which ascended through social scales from the individual, the family, and
the citizenry.
Informed by what was happening in Europe, in Ecuador, public medical
institutions began to take charge of hygienist activities at the beginning of
1901. However, control practices typical of the Police were adopted. In fact,
both institutions worked together in mutual support. One of the most
frequent practices in controlling epidemics was home medical visits. These
visits had the explicit purpose of checking the cleanliness inside the houses
and monitoring personal hygiene practices, and it was the Police that
determined which families should be visited. In this sense, Gil's plan was
extremely efficient because, unlike other plans of the time, it showed the
numbering of each block and each house. Thus, it facilitated the Police in
locating undesirable elements and zoning the city according to hygiene
criteria. It was a graphic mechanism for the identification, classification,
and implementation of modernist hygiene processes.
Antonio Gil, the Hygienist Man
Antonio Gil was a political man particularly close to the issue of public
health, despite being linked to the liberal party of Ecuador. Hygiene, health,
and moral policies were initially advocated by conservative men as they
were innately linked to clerical authority. However, under the mandate of

the liberal president Eloy Alfaro, progressive strategies in science were
understood as new possibilities to modernize the isolated rural population
living in the interior of the Andes, so health policies were implemented for
public benefit. Particularly aligned with these policies, Gil held the position
of General Intendant of Guayas under this president between 1895 and
1906. In that role, he had to contain the bubonic plague that had struck the
Ecuadorian coast. To do so, in Guayaquil, Ecuador's commercial port, he
established the Superior Board of Maritime and Urban Health in 1898. To
contain the illness, it was said that siphons were installed throughout
Guayaquil. In Ecuador, health policies emerged as solutions to combat
waterborne epidemics since European medical advances indicated that
fluids carried diseases.16
Quito, a colder Andean city less vulnerable to contamination from
stagnant water, did not see the need for sanitation institutions until 1903,
when municipal hygiene doctor Manuel Jijón Bello described how the
water from the springs descending from the Atacazo volcano that supplied
Quito was contaminated by livestock processes and poor sewage
management. From this year, the Superior Council of Health and Hygiene
was established in other cities of the Ecuadorian interior. Under this
council, "the period from 1902 to 1906 was one of great municipal activity
in promoting the potable water supply project."17 On January 13, 1903, the
project for constructing a domestic potable water system for Quito was
assigned, concluding a competition that had lasted two years. The winner
was Durini, but the project faced delays and remained abandoned for three
more years. Finally, the contract was awarded to the German engineer
Alfonso Vernimmen in 1905, and construction began hastily in July of that
year.18 In 1906, when Eloy Alfaro took office and declared infrastructures
for "potable water, drainage, and paving as national and charitable works,"

the Superior Council of Health and Hygiene was replaced by the Potable
Water and Drainage Board of Quito.19 Although the construction of the
network did not commence in 1903, supervision and control projects for
filling ravines, drainage, and pipelines preceding the potable water network
were carried out.
Antonio Gil's map was linked to the aspiration for a potable water supply
network. In addition to creating a parcel map intended for the Police to
control the city under hygienist terms, it allowed Gil to create a subsequent,
much more complete and detailed plan. Eleven years later, when Leonidas
Plaza returned for his second term in 1914, he reappointed Gil as General
Intendant, this time of the province of Pichincha, where Quito is located,
and as Intendant of the Municipality of the city. In that position, he created
a map to a scale of 1:6000 that included the old water pipelines, the new
ones, and even the public faucets. Additionally, this new representation
extended the depiction of the city to the south of the Panecillo, and included
contour lines and the ravines descending from the Pichincha volcano.
Hygiene and Extractivism
Antonio Gil became well known for being one of the first entrepreneurs to
colonize the Galápagos Islands at the end of the 19th century. Indeed, in
1893, he founded the Santo Tomás hacienda on Isabela Island after an
unsuccessful attempt to settle on Floreana Island. On the slopes of Cerro
Azul, the hacienda initially had 27 settlers and 80 slaves. There, Gil
systematized agricultural exploitation, then livestock farming, and finally
the production of turtle oil, which was in high demand in Guayaquil.20
In the Galápagos, water supply problems have been frequent. The water
sources work seasonally, and, at that time, Isabela Island had only a few
wetlands that were used for consumption.21 As Octavio La Torre describes,

"people as well as animals suffered greatly from the water shortage" at the
Santo Tomás hacienda. Gil solved significant water supply issues through
rudimentary "barrels in each slave's house to collect rainwater and a pond
in the main house" (that of Gil).22 Despite the water scarcity, Gil managed
to develop a moderate amount of agriculture. By 1911, he had finished
constructing a masonry reservoir to supply the entire hacienda. Thanks to
this system, his hacienda continued to prosper through a mechanism of
systematic predation of giant tortoises, introduced cattle herds, cod fishing,
and sulfur extraction. To this day, there is no potable water system on
Isabela Island, but Gil's property remained in the collective memory
throughout the 20th century as an enterprise of progress and benefit for the
island. Its devastating extractivist methods were only uncovered much later.
Gil's Plan: Abstract, Ideal, Authoritarian, and
Neat
Antonio Gil's 1903 plan of Quito depicted a city aligned with Western
rationalism, evident in the colonial grid layout. At the same time, the
hygienist references showed the idea of progress as control over the
physical and moral cleanliness of the individual and of society. But the
water's path still ran under different rules. In fact, Manuel Jijón Bello spoke
of the initial water distribution in Quito as "a labyrinth ... without order or
measure," a system outside of scientific norms.23 He described water as a
resource in a wild state, and the Panecillo was part of this untamed universe
connected to water.
Before being named "Panecillo" by the Spaniards, the hill was called
"Yavirac," a term coined by the Incas. The water slopes contoured the hill
from west to east and contribute to its rounded shape. Some theorists have
argued that the toponymic basis for the name Yavirac was yahuirca, a

mythological equivalent of an anaconda.24 In fact, the Panecillo's circular
base and gradually ascending platforms resembled a coiled serpent. In
Quechua, Yavirac has also been defined as a waterfall from the heart of a
mountain. This theory was substantiated because the Incas worshipped
water and installed palaces of virgins with pools for priestly initiation on the
north face of the Panecillo. In the 20th century, the laundries and public
pools that were located there, fed by the old springs, were evidence of this
relationship with water.25 From this perspective, the hill represented the
value of water as a source of life, as it had for ancestral cultures. It was an
uncomfortable reminder of the stricter aspirations of urban control, just as it
was for the initial settlers. By omitting the hill, Antonio Gil's plan brought
order to the labyrinth described by Jijón Bello, rationalizing and
partitioning it. The omission of the Panecillo showed that the ancestral
values related to the natural landscape of the Andes clashed with the
Western modernity of the 20th century.
The Omission to Promote the
International Connection
The Representation of a Pseudo-European City
Unlike Antonio Gil's map, which omitted the Panecillo because it was
considered irrelevant in an orthogonal scheme, a second map that appeared
that year (1903) also omitted the hill, but this time in a very detailed plan -
a highly precise panoramic representation by Henry Grant Higley (Figure
6.4).

FIGURE 6.4 Map of the city of Quito, Henry Grant Higley,
1903. Source: Mapoteca Histórica de Límites, Cartografía y
Límites, Unidad Técnica de la Dirección de Relaciones Vecinales
y Soberanías, Subsecretaría de América Latina y el Caribe del
Ministerio de Relaciones Exteriores y Movilidad Humana. ⏎
This map minimized the entire topography of Quito. Preceding the
establishment of the Ecuadorian Military Geographic Institute in 1922, the
graphic interpretation of all geographic features was quite free, and Higley
chose subtlety.26 Delicate curved lines simulated undulations of the relief,
somewhat more frequent in the upper part of the map, in what would be the
slopes of Pichincha, and on the left corresponding to the beginning of the
Panecillo hill. These curved lines lightly shaded the sides of the ravines as if
they were harmless streams, but they were deep trenches in the rugged
territory of the Andes. While the geographical features were muted, the
author exaggerated the historical monuments. To achieve this, Higley
stretched the heights of these buildings and incorporated a lot of detail,

color, and even the suggestion of shine on the domes and facades,
emulating the sunlight they received from the east. This was possible
because the map was a superior panorama. In other words, it was not a
traditional orthogonal map where only the rooftops of the city's layout
could be seen; rather, it depicted the city from a slightly inclined high view,
also allowing a side view of the buildings.
This type of cartographic representation was very common in the United
States during the 19th century as it made maps easier to understand for a
broader audience not initiated in cartography.27 Additionally, Higley's map
was accompanied by a series of photographs that emphasized the beauty of
Quito's historical landmarks. Therefore, it was a kind of highly precise
tourist guide that highlighted places to visit. It was a detailed map that
deliberately flattened the surface of the Andes. The subtext was that Quito
was a readable city of high historical value and a harmless natural
landscape.28 This description matched the observations of various travelers
who categorized Quito within the parameters of a European city. For
example, Charles Reginald Enock noted that it was "a third-rate European
city,"29 among other things, for having "some kind of order despite its
topography."30
The International Movement in Higley
The level of detail of the monuments on both sides was a feature of
Higley's map that had already appeared in another city plan. This was the
first map to introduce accurate elevations, and was commissioned by
Dionisio Alcedo y Herrera in 1734 (Figure 6.5).31 This plan was a bird's-
eye view set at a 30-degree angle from the horizontal, allowing for both the
physical space's coverage in plan and, at the same time, the lateral sides of
buildings to be seen.32 However, unlike Higley's map, here the Panecillo

was not only present but was also so significant that it received the same
level of detail as the constructed infrastructure, showing its features on
several sides of its elevation.33 The Pichincha volcano, with less detail,
appeared as imposing background scenery. The other significant hill to the
east of the city, Itchimbía, was not visible but seems to have served as a
location for the cartographer to create this map. Thus, the image of the city
conveyed by Alcedo y Herrera was that of a fabric defined on all sides by
its marked topography. Under this subtext, the Panecillo contained the city
and established a vertical counterpoint to its buildings.
FIGURE 6.5 Map of Quito, Dionisio Alcedo y Herrera, 1734,
showing the Panecillo on the left, traditionally an inevitable
landmark in the city's representation. Source: Instituto Geográfico
Nacional de España, Creative Commons,
https://www.ign.es/web/catalogo-
cartoteca/resources/html/023397.html). ⏎

In Quito's cartography, maps from a bird's-eye view, like those of Alcedo
y Herrera, typically utilized other hills in the city for positioning and
depicting the city without any obstacles.34 In contrast, Higley's map was a
cartographic view, meaning the city was depicted at a 60-degree angle from
the horizontal. The vantage point from which the illustration was made was
no longer a high point on the land but a virtual point in the sky.
Additionally, in this view, the streets receded in perspective towards a
single vanishing point on a high level of the Pichincha volcano. Therefore,
the colonial roads, ideally orthogonal, converged to this vanishing point on
the volcano. The cartographer seemed to be flying towards the northern part
of the city. The graphic movement in this representation suggested that, to
advance, one should head north. There was an implicit displacement that
moved the observer away from the location of the Panecillo. However, the
hill did not appear. As a significant topographical barrier, the hill was a
difficult element in terms of the construction of a city. The expense,
challenge, and technology required to incorporate the hill into the urban
fabric rendered it an object to "avoid." The modernistic project was very
fond of simplistic solutions, preferring to ignore reality rather than
incorporate complexities; therefore the concept of "omission" in the modern
project was not an oversight but an intended "avoidance."
Without the natural boundary of the Panecillo, the edges of the urban
fabric were not territorial limits but graphic limits. The photographs of
emblematic buildings, the national coat of arms,35 and the legend for
important buildings obscured the three hills surrounding the urban area: San
Juan, Itchimbía, and the Panecillo. Consequently, the city was portrayed as
open to growth, without topographic obstacles and unfinished in terms of
possibilities. On the lower and right sides, Higley's map featured peripheral
vignettes of advertisements for merchandise (see Figure 6.4). All the ads

indicated that they were importers from Europe and the United States.
Some specified that the businesses were from England and Germany and
sold a variety of products such as liquor, medicine, and hats. It was
suggested that the city was a favorable place to attract foreign investors.36
The map seemed to encourage visiting Quito and investing there. Thus, the
implicit movement in the map's perspective representation implied that the
city was open to the world through commercial exchanges.37 It included the
modern idea of connection, from a region rich in natural resources to be
exploited by experts from abroad. According to Capello, this commission
directly alluded to the recent use of cartography to promote a vision of the
capital as a "global centrality in physical, metaphorical, and - increasingly
- tourist terms."38
Reference to the Construction of the Railroad in 1903
To complete the lower part of the advertising rectangle framing the map,
Higley included a small inset, a scaled-down version of a map created by
Higley himself. Titled "Guayaquil/Quito Railway of Ecuador. South
America," it depicted the entire route of the railroad project that was under
construction to connect the capital city, Quito (in the Andes), and the
country's largest city, Guayaquil, on the coast. The conservative president
García Moreno, the mastermind and main promoter of the railroad, began
its construction in 1873 with the section from Guayaquil to Yaguachi, but
the project was halted in 1875 following his assassination. However, in
1898, the project was resumed under new foreign contractors, English and
American.39 In February 1900, torrential rain hampered the construction, so
in 1901 the route had to be redirected through the valleys of Chan Chan and
Alausí to Quito. In the same year, the automobile was introduced to the
area.

By 1903, when Higley's map was created, the railroad had extended to
various Andean cities (such as Alausí, Guamote, Riobamba, Ambato, and
Latacunga), thus shortening the journey time between Guayaquil and Quito.
Additionally, the remaining section to be constructed was the one
connecting Guamote to Quito. This section was the easiest to execute as,
once it reached the mountain range, the project could be resolved with a
gradient of less than 12 percent which had characterized all previous
ascents. Moreover, just before Guamote, the Chan Chan valley was
associated with rain, humidity, poor soil, and, additionally, diseases
common in tropical areas with high temperatures, such as yellow fever. This
is why the project could not employ Andean indigenous workers, who
feared the epidemics of this valley, and had to resort to workers from
Jamaica, Barbados, and Puerto Rico.40 Finally, the arrival of the railroad in
Guamote ensured a source of fuel for energy thanks to available coal
mines.41 Nevertheless, in 1903, private car services were implemented to
cover the remaining section of the railroad, reducing the journey time from
five days to two and a half.42 This revealed a new idea of efficiency in
transportation processes. The construction of the railroad between Quito
and Guayaquil was completed in June 1908, but by 1903 the project was
advanced enough to predict its economic growth benefits. It was pure
potential.
Henry Grant Higley was deeply involved in the railroad project, and was
known as one of the expert technical engineers who would advise on its
construction.43 In fact, Higley and his partner, named Slater, had created a
panoramic map for Guayaquil in 1900, and this map was used by workers
on the project during its execution.44 However, Higley's personal
involvement in the project went beyond technical consultancy. When he
passed through Mosquito, Nicaragua, in 1894, he created a map of the

banana plantations, but, since he owned some of the plots, his map reflected
his personal interests (Figure 6.6).45 Higley was an enigmatic entrepreneur
and technician who had previously profited from his vision of land as a
resource, incorporating economic aspirations into his maps.46 It seemed that
he was interested in the development of the railroad, which promised
significant profits around the stations in the Andes, areas that were
previously disconnected. The 1903 map was commissioned by the
successful businessman Dr. Julio Esaú Delgado, whose name appeared
under the title of the map in Figure 6.4, "Quito 1903." Delgado was a
Colombian entrepreneur and close friend of the liberal president Eloy
Alfaro. The map reflected both Higley's and Delgado's personal interests.

FIGURE 6.6 Map of Mosquito Coast, Henry Grant Higley, 1894.
Source: Library of Congress Geography and Map Division
Washington, D.C. 20540-4650 USA dcu. Digital Id:

http://hdl.loc.gov/loc.gmd/g4852m.ma001018. Creative
Commons. ⏎
The reference to the railroad in Higley's map was a mark of cutting-edge
transportation infrastructure. Just as projects like the Panama Canal were
advertised, the railroad was presented as the infrastructure that would
integrate the Andean city into a new network of high-speed international
connections. The presence of this small map served as propaganda for
Quito's favorable conditions for foreign trade. In fact, Enock described
Quito as a city "remote and inaccessible until it was connected by the
railroad to the outside world."47 At the same time, it showcased another
facet of progress: international connection as a profit-making machine for
foreign entrepreneurs.
Political Rhetoric on International
Connection Progress
The subtext of Higley's map conveyed the nuance of the vision of progress
in the liberal political discourse of the time. At the turn of the century, a
new wave of republican leaders adopted a modernizing zeal in their
political speeches. These speeches continued to imply that advancement
meant surpassing a state of savagery. However, this unidirectional process
was no longer explained solely by the control and homogenization of
territory, but as a shift toward internationalization.
Delving deeper, the speeches of national and local leaders focused on
promoting movement and connection. These concepts, repeated in the
rhetoric of the liberal leader Eloy Alfaro from the late 19th century and in
that of Quito's municipal leaders, such as Francisco Andrade Marín, were
attractive and sufficiently ambiguous to fit the expectations of various

sectors.48 For Eloy Alfaro, who continued the activities started by the
conservative president García Moreno for the railroad, the railway was a
means to advance society. Specifically, he aimed to bring the rural Andean
population out of isolation and instill values of labor and efficiency.
Andrade Marín, while leading the Quito City Council in 1905, emphasized
that the Andean railroad would link Quito with the port of Guayaquil,
describing it as a technology of movement toward the world. What was
crucial was to anchor oneself to the international network of transportation
infrastructure to meet the "demands of the civilization of larger nations." He
mentioned the idea that progress required connecting to a global network of
exchanges.
The railroad also opened Quito to tourism: 20 years after the railroad was
inaugurated, the census showed that, between 1921 and 1923, "4,365
foreigners of 42 nationalities arrived, with an average of 145 visitors per
month and 90 who settled as residents."49 Despite the potential to directly
link Quito to Guayaquil, the primary users of this infrastructure were major
foreign entrepreneurs moving goods, labor forces, and tourists from abroad.
Thus, the areas around industries connected to Quito's railroad ended up
consolidating into "worker neighborhoods."50 However, by 1914, after six
years of railroad operation, the center of Quito was not connected at the
urban level and did not seem to benefit from internal migration within the
country.51 What mattered was the image. It was about consolidating a
graphic memory of a future Quito ready to welcome foreign investors and
their businesses as part of an electoral political campaign focused on
modernity.52

The Image of Progress
To ensure that the Panecillo did not appear on his map, Higley covered this
area of the city with the legend of public buildings and two photographs;
the upper photo featured the Military Complex, a prison located at the base
of the hill; and the lower photo showed the Sucre Theater with the Panecillo
in the background. The hill was present as a backdrop behind the
constructed monuments. In this way, Higley avoided representing the hill as
a static mass counter to the dynamic will or as an obstacle to the city's
openness.53 Instead, it was presented as a picturesque element within the
exotic scenery of the Andean landscape. The Panecillo's omission revealed
the intention to remove it from the idea of an untamed landscape. Thus, the
hill was to be remembered only as a photograph. In this way, Higley turned
it into a sort of mirage.
The hill's symbolic importance was linked to permanence. In contrast to
the subtext of movement, rationalism, and international connection
embedded in the map, the hill could not move, change, or dissolve. Beyond
that, its omission revealed the incompatibility of the Andean relief with the
conceptual drive for progress. Like the other volcanoes surrounding Quito,
the Panecillo was a reminder that geological time imposed itself on the
immediacy of technology and human advancements.
Conclusion
Progress lay in the externalization of a human function into a manufactured
artifact.54 However, this process could be violent as it involved an excision.
In the 1903 cartography of Quito, this extraction was literal. A geological
prominence that gave meaning to the city was erased. This created an
absence that highlighted the darker aspects of progress in Andean cities; the

hygienist phenomenon sought to cleanse the territory of its savagery, both
physically and socially, while the process of internationalization deepened
the rifts between major foreign entrepreneurs and isolated, impoverished
Ecuadorian populations.
Conceptually, these two forms of modernization represented the
rationalization of elements in the Andean landscape that, according to
Andean cosmology, were meant to flow freely: water and pathways. The
water running through ravines was enclosed in pipes, and the ancient paths
were converted into high-speed roads.
In 1903, all the symbolic value assigned to the Panecillo hill by the
ancestral cultures that inhabited the Andes was violently removed from the
graphical memory of the capital in favor of presenting Quito as a city of
progress. In this way, the more progressive efforts of the 20th-century
authorities blurred the ancestral tradition by repeating violent procedures
that concealed the intrinsic characteristics of the Andes.

31. Herrera's plan of Quito served as a graphic justification for the need to
