7
PROVING GROUNDS
Speculative Scenography and
Staging the Future
Constance Vale
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-8
Speculative fiction dominates the development of emerging technology.
There is a direct relationship between fiction and invention, wherein the
"sociotechnical imagination" is predictive and drives innovation through
self-fulfilling narratives. Government agencies, the tech industry, and
filmmakers most commonly drive these narratives,1 favoring compelling
storylines and greater capital gains rather than assessing technology's
impact on the built environment or its social and environmental
ramifications. Architects bring a different perspective that can address these
concerns, examining how to shape technology impactfully rather than
merely accept and adapt to it within architecture, cities, and public spaces.
Architects are ideally positioned to do this, as our capacities in image-
making and co-creation enable us to project sociotechnical fictions. In
applying these capabilities, how can architects effectively contribute to
informing sociotechnical fictions in ways that frame the ethical questions
they raise in public discourse?

To examine this question, this chapter analyzes the immersive stagecraft
used to train machines known as "proving grounds," with a focus on their
use in the ongoing development of urban artificial intelligence (AI) and
autonomous vehicles (AVs). Employed chiefly in military and automotive
industry experimentation, proving grounds reproduce "the real" to examine
how agents, vehicles, systems, and strategies perform in controlled
environments. All too often, these testing facilities reflect predictive
dystopias modeled on worlds without architects. Few include architects in
the design or testing process at all. In order to change the way that we
envision possible futures, it is crucial to challenge the role of these
platforms, see them as possible sites of architectural invention, and
advocate for their potency in staging sociotechnical narratives that elucidate
the spatial potentials and perils of emerging technology.
The Sociotechnical Imagination
Within the realm of emerging technology, narratives are powerful devices
that dominate the development of the future. In his book Smart Cities: A
Spatialized Intelligence, Antoine Picon identifies a direct relationship
between fiction and invention, wherein the "sociotechnical imagination" is
predictive and drives innovation through self-fulfilling narratives. In some
cases, novelists, scriptwriters, and film directors define these narratives,
with science fiction directing technological evolutions through imaginative
portrayals.
However, this space of invention is blurring further with everyday reality,
wherein computer scientists, tech entrepreneurs, corporate entities,
government agencies, and the media are generating speculative future
narratives.2 This is evident in the case of Elon Musk, who described and
later realized the mass production of electric vehicles, reusable private

rockets in space exploration, and brain-machine interfaces to enhance
human cognition through companies like Tesla, SpaceX, and Neuralink. In
such cases, the speculative narrative is authored by and driven to benefit
those who speculate (in the financial sense of the term) on their potential, a
reality even more concerning as such figures become direct actors in
national politics.
Problematically, in the late capitalist present, our shared stories are
restricted to being projected by those who have the authority to elevate
them. Dominant stories of the past are often written by those in power and
presented as absolute truth while marginalizing alternative perspectives.
Similarly, projective narratives can embed existing power structures in their
frameworks. To avoid such continuations of power, it is necessary to have
new authors without financially compromised interests, who can advocate
for more inclusive stories that tackle complex issues like the global climate
crisis, systemic inequities, or technological ethics. The following section
reveals some key reasons why architects are positioned to take on this
critical role.3
The Role of the Architect in Constructing
Fictions
Given the significant influence these narratives have on the evolution of
emerging technology, architects should actively explore how to contribute
to them rather than simply accepting, and thereby endorsing, the narratives
advanced by government agencies, the media, tech entrepreneurs, and other
corporate and private interests, which often overlook concerns about the
built environment, public space, and social and environmental issues
altogether.

While filmmakers and tech industry leaders have proven exceedingly
capable of projecting speculative fictions about technology, it is not their
exclusive domain. As a form of culture, architecture tells stories—our
primary activity as architects centers on representing something that does
not yet exist but might. To reference Robin Evans, architects don't make
buildings but, instead, create images, objects, and other devices to translate
into building.4 Central to this, we rely on the "enabling fiction"5 of
representation to propose powerful narratives and argue for the existence of
the things we envision through that representation.
This narrative capability of architecture is even more present today than
in preceding periods, given the nature of our tools and the ways we work
within images. In the contemporary world, fictions are powerful tools,
particularly with the immersive media at our fingertips. Architects are adept
at using emerging technology in image-making and storytelling, and, as
such, our discipline is uniquely positioned to shape immersive visual
narratives.
Like filmmakers or tech entrepreneurs, architects bring a collaborative
perspective, but one that puts us in a better position to lead this charge. As
co-creators who work with multiple constituencies through community
engagement, coordinators who direct teams of disciplines, and curious
generalists, architects are equipped to approach these narratives from
various vantages, further enhanced by our practice of bringing together
competing forces like culture, context, technology, function, aesthetics, and
more. Some architects also operate in research universities and non-profits,
detached from financial incentivization or profit. It is increasingly pressing
that architects harness our capabilities in this respect, given the
omnipresence and opacity of technology and the scale and scope of
problems we need to confront in the face of seemingly intractable

ecological crises and systemic injustice. That is, architects could deploy our
representational capabilities to challenge systems of power and propose
distributive models of agency to address technology's implications and
innovate in the built environment.
Positioning architects to create sociotechnical narratives raises two key
questions: How can architects expand the scope of our field's influence and
participate in directing the development and spatial implementation of
emerging technology? And how can we challenge existing power structures
and construct collaborative design methods to propose possible future
implementations of technology in the public realm?
Centering Architecture's Periphery
In order to gain a foothold in defining the sociotechnical imagination,
architects should define our discipline more broadly, as not only realizing
singular buildings but also designing the spatial implementation of
technology in the built environment. Such repositioning might involve
situating architects as projective thinkers and project coordinators who
design scenarios, perhaps as actors embedded in government agencies
developing policy and planning, or perhaps as internal contributors in tech
corporations, or as key contributors to film and video game design that form
projective societal discussions about the future of technology.
Redistributing our field's expertise in interdisciplinary partnerships
recognizes architecture as a key player in not only designing the built
environment but also in designing what kind of world we want to share by
addressing issues so complex that they require multiple intelligences and
robust collaborations.
As one avenue to engage such a widened scope, architects might expand
our role to working with those things just outside the periphery of our

discipline, in the strange and uncanny type known as "proving grounds."
These constructions are not conventionally accepted as architecture, but are
so close to the kind of immersive image-making going on in contemporary
practice and so potentially impactful on the built environment that they
warrant serious study in architecture today. Employing proving grounds in
this context allows for examining their potential as imaginative spaces of
public discourse within which architects can conduct research and
collaborate with communities to shape technology, its spatial distribution,
and its ramifications on civic space.
Defining Proving Grounds
Employed chiefly in military and automotive industry experimentation,
proving grounds are testing environments used to situate scenarios to train
for and predict possible futures. A proving ground is a controlled
environment where things that are typically dangerous are rendered safe so
they can be tested, assessed, and improved to minimize potential risks to
human life. This is particularly useful in relation to high-stakes, high-risk
scenarios, especially in cases where it is necessary to gauge the
performance of novel equipment or procedures in complex and
unpredictable environments.
To understand the complexity of these training spaces—and, more
importantly, to challenge them—it is worthwhile to break down the
compound word and examine the roots of "proof" and "ground." According
to the Oxford English Dictionary, "ground" is used in geology, ideology,
architecture, and art. In all its denotations, the stability of "ground" is in
question, based on the material composition in the architectural or
geological context, the foundations of an idea, or figure-ground reversal in
art's shifting stability. The Earth is in great flux, soil can be unstable, and

the authority that determines what is fact or defines the justification for a
motive throws intellectual grounds into question. "Proof" is outlined across
law, mathematics, and art. The legal standard for proof is rendered with a
preponderance of the evidence used to establish a fact or ascertain the truth.
In mathematics, a proof constructs the connection between a theorem and
premises through a series of logical steps. In the arts, proof can refer to a
test used to examine required changes prior to final printing. At its core,
proof necessitates the destruction of mystery and the construction of
certainty.
However, to ascertain truth or fact in the context of proving grounds, one
needs to construct a fiction. That is, for all the investment of "proof" in
veracity and scientific certainty, the "ground" in question must be artificial.
Proving grounds constitute such an intentionally unstable ground in that
they are not the real thing they pose as being; they are instead a particular
variety of "Potemkin Village" made to create a contained world in order to
test possible futures.
Typically, proving grounds are conceived and built by engineers and
contractors without input from architects, urban designers, landscape
architects, or other spatial design fields. Given the lack of input from these
fields, proving grounds often inadvertently reflect predictive dystopias
modeled on worlds without designers. In turn, architects have not
historically had an interest in these experimental platforms, overlooking
them as having little to no architectural value in terms of their design or
discursive potential. With this in mind, locating proving grounds in relation
to architecture's existing working space might be useful in assessing their
relevance to our field.

Locating Proving Grounds in Architecture
Bringing proving grounds into a relationship with architecture allows for
this emphasis on fiction and multiple potentialities to be heightened. Proof
and its requisite ties to truth, fact, certainty, logic, and precisely predictable
outcomes can be challenging to discern, particularly in architecture. The
ground, as it were, is shaky at best. While some architectural concerns are
measurable and controllable, many factors are based on actions of
unpredictable actors and systems, followed by subsequent reactions, for
which it is challenging to account. Moreover, architecture is a cultural
practice and is not merely demonstrative but also engages audiences,
provokes questions, and creates dialogues. In this space of fiction that is so
vital to proving grounds, there is ample room for the projective explorations
of architecture.
Building on this relationship between proving grounds and architecture
through fiction might be further helped by a better understanding of the
working space that such constructions could occupy within the world of
representation or building. We might categorize proving grounds,
particularly those built to a scale, as models. When built to a scale, proving
grounds are large enough to be classified as buildings, but they are variably
lacking the requisite program, structure, systems, or even sometimes
interiors required for human use or habitation. They are also rarely as
singular as a building; an entire environment must typically be built to train,
inclusive of infrastructure, landscape, and wider swaths of the built
environment. Perhaps it is more apt to think of proving grounds not as
models or buildings but as related to scenic designs, ones built not for use in
a performance but to assess performance. That said, they do not sit
comfortably here either. These are immersive stagecraft with engineered

illusionism made to trick not an audience but, instead, the actors being
tested within them.
Proving grounds might be best categorized as simulations. While
simulations are found in the sciences, they are also deployed in the creative
arts, particularly in fields that employ the techniques of worldbuilding, such
as filmmaking, game design, and architecture. One cannot create a
convincing game environment without defining all the parameters of its
model world. Similarly, all rules, properties, orders, systems, spaces, and
more must be specified in the proving ground. Like film sets or gaming
environments, proving grounds have the potential to stage stories or even
create co-constructed stories, wherein both users and designers are engaged
in activating a story together. This is precisely what has been overlooked or
underestimated concerning proving grounds: the powerful potential to
become narrative vehicles.
These proving grounds possess certain properties of interest to
architecture, specifically in their ability to translate the urban environment
into scenic constructions. However, they cannot do much in terms of
exploring the architectural and spatial implications of introducing AVs and
urban AI technologies in cities. To explore architectural issues, we might
look back to the discipline of architecture for a useful example: Cedric
Price's unbuilt "Fun Palace," a theater planned for London's East End,
designed for and with director Joan Littlewood. Designed as a "laboratory
of fun" and "a university of the streets," the project was conceived as a steel
space frame structure with a completely flexible program that gantries
could rearrange as its users wished.6 The interior was to be filled with
movable walls, floors, plug-ins, and circulation, as well as heating,
ventilation, and air conditioning (HVAC) systems made to emit
atmospherics for theatrical and participatory adaptability.7 The result is

more of a scenic design inside a flexible soundstage than conventional
architecture. This operability forms its strength as a simulation, which
functions as a medium to generate environments and stage events through
the agency of the architect and the public, informing its transformations.
This example offers ways to consider how architectural and urban issues
might be explored, wherein space and atmosphere can be transformed over
time. This operable platform mediates the environment, enabling multiple
actors to participate in its redesign over time. In addition, its design is
infrastructural, a system that facilitates its transformation. These properties
are helpful to keep in mind when considering what kinds of proving
grounds architects might create.
Machine Scenography
The Autonomous Future of Mobility is a project that I have undertaken over
the past several years that exemplifies proving grounds as architectural
enterprises exploring sociotechnical imaginaries. This larger project frames
a series of nested ones, all exploring architecture understood through the
lens of scenography for imagining possible futures. I consider this work to
be parafictional. Within parafiction, there is a range: at one end, those
adjacent to fiction that depart further from the world as we know it; and, at
the other end, those that are careful to reconstruct "the real" so closely that
they are adjacent to non-fiction. The first examples within The Autonomous
City Series are more fictional, while the final example, the Mini City, is
closer to non-fiction.
All subprojects within The Autonomous Future of Mobility respond to the
rapid changes occurring in AI and AVs, examining their impacts on cities
and mobility systems. All perform this investigation via a worldbuilding
exercise, constructing sociotechnical narratives questioning how AVs and

AI are spatially implemented. This research also considers that, while
automobiles have been a transformative technology that has dramatically
improved our lives by reducing the time required to travel and the effects of
distance, they have also led to catastrophic consequences. The ramifications
of automobiles include inordinate vehicle crash fatalities, environmental
and atmospheric damage, military conflicts related to resource control,
insufficient infrastructure to support individual travel, and economic
injustice and segregation in cities as a result of the expansion of highways
and the use of eminent domain.8 While AVs promise to improve our lives
with increased safety and by promoting ridesharing and electric vehicles,
they also present tremendous risks. Primarily, the safety of AVs remains
relatively unproven, and their spatial implementation has not yet been
adequately explored.
The Autonomous City Series
Autonomous City I, the first in the series, studies Amazon's AI patents,
clustering them into an urban context that need only respond to autonomous
agents.9 Amazon intends to deploy their patented AI systems in a
distributed network of facilities and mobile units, many of which hook into
existing infrastructural systems. In contrast, Autonomous City I benefits
from clustering these AI agents and systems into a city that need only
respond to machine vision and robotic operations, as seen in the plan
drawing (Figure 7.1).10 This post-human urban strategy results in a hyper-
density that would be unnavigable for humans. Like Giovanni Battista
Piranesi's Campo Marzio dell'antica Roma, the plan drawing in Figure 7.1
characterizes instauratio urbis, eliminating the axial organization of typical
cities in favor of a horror vaccui of artificial intelligences. The imagined
city seen in the detailed axonometric drawing and rendering is fully

autonomous architecture, a post-human landscape in which AI delivers new
materials, builds, cleans, maintains, and operates it in its entirety. The
human is removed from the center, remote from the city, and displaced from
its spectacle (Figures 7.2 and 7.3).
FIGURE 7.1 Plan drawing of Autonomous City I, composed
entirely of a selection of Amazon's AI patents circa 2018. ⏎


FIGURE 7.2 Axonometric detail drawing of Autonomous City I
showing a selection of unmanned city operations. ⏎

FIGURE 7.3 Axonometric rendering of Autonomous City I
demonstrating a free field of clustered structures and systems
made possible due to the lack of need for human navigation. ⏎
The next in this series, Autonomous City II, serves as a preliminary study
for an AV proving ground that is later iterated and built as the Mini City.
Autonomous City II creates a highly variable environment, maximizing
urban, off-highway scenarios for the vehicle to encounter, as seen in the
axonometric rendering (Figure 7.4).11 In the spirit of Colin Rowe and Fred
Koetter's Collage City, this study collects and fuses existing and imagined
urbanities from a number of cities, indexing a range of conditions for future
analysis.12

FIGURE 7.4 Axonometric rendering of Autonomous City II. ⏎
The resulting projective city maximizes urban conditions with multiple
grid systems at play, establishing disrupted networks that challenge
traditional modes of urban orientation, depicted in the model photographs
(Figures 7.5 and 7.6). Building forms are based on motherboard parts,

alluding to the digital systems that underlie spatial matrices in smart cities
today. The project highlights key paradoxes: whether smart cities are
designed for people or machines, spatial networks or data warehouses; and
whether the people democratically govern them or are algorithmically
coded and under the control of the entities that own and use the data.

FIGURE 7.5 Aerial detail model photograph of Autonomous City
II. ⏎

FIGURE 7.6 Photograph of details from Autonomous City II
model, taken from the Campo Marzio fragment, looking toward
the Paris fragment. ⏎
Autonomous City III builds on its predecessor, examining the space
surrounding the miniature AV proving ground, and envisioning its
operational use by the research team and in potential public engagement.13
Taking cues from Dutch artist and architect Constant Nieuwenhuys's New
Babylon, the spatial organization of Autonomous City III explores the
shifting boundaries between human and machine agency in reconfigurable
arrangements that allow for creative exchange, scenarios of which can be
seen in the diorama models (Figures 7.7 and 7.8). Where Autonomous City
II asked critical questions about the purpose and control of smart cities,
Autonomous City III offers a vision for the social and ethical implications of
proving grounds as sites of design in and of themselves. It considers
whether privatized research facilities, typically hidden from public view,
might evolve into transparent arenas of public discourse, thereby fostering

greater accountability and inclusivity in the development of autonomous
systems.
FIGURE 7.7 Photograph of Autonomous City III in four diorama
models, studying the architectural design of an experimental
platform for autonomous driving. Autonomous City III focuses on
the city's modular construction in stage flats and risers,
reconfiguring the platform to form the surrounding research
spaces. Entourage is built at 1:8 scale for repurposing in the
testing platform. ⏎

FIGURE 7.8 Photograph of a detail from Autonomous City III in
one of its four diorama models, studying the architectural design
of an experimental platform for autonomous driving. ⏎
To do this, Autonomous City III mocks up experimentation scenarios,
configuring the platform in various arrangements for iterative testing with
adjacent data analysis and model-building stations, as shown in the tableaux
(Figures 7.9 and 7.10). Simultaneously, it explores how the broader public
might be invited to witness, critique, and even participate in the
development of autonomous technologies in observation areas overlooking
active testing zones, extended reality (XR) interfaces that allow visitors to
envision AV navigation systems, mock-ups of possible alterations to the
built environment, and interactive demonstrations and workshops that
explore AV systems in action. The proving ground transforms into a hybrid
space: part laboratory, part exhibition, and part participatory theater for

educational and civic engagement. Through speculative scenarios,
Autonomous City III demonstrates how proving grounds might operate not
only as testing platforms but also as spaces of public discourse that
democratize narratives of technological innovation.14


FIGURE 7.9 Aerial photograph of Autonomous City III as a
tableau, studying the architectural design of an experimental
platform for autonomous driving. ⏎
FIGURE 7.10 Detail photograph of Autonomous City III as a
tableau, studying the architectural design of an experimental
platform for autonomous driving. ⏎
Mini City: Autonomous Vehicle Proving
Ground
The WashU Mini City builds on the Autonomous City Series with increased
stakes in studying key problems created by AVs and other forms of urban
AI.15 The Mini City is an operable model, an immersive scenic design that
stages events, both in miniature and full scale (Figures 7.11 and 7.12). It is a
1:8 scale (the same scale as an F1/10 model AV) proving ground for
autonomous driving that accommodates a model AV with a full perceptual
stack situated in a research lab. The project is an interdisciplinary

collaboration between architect Constance Vale and computer scientist
Yevgeniy Vorobeychik at the McKelvey School of Engineering at
Washington University in St. Louis (WashU).
FIGURE 7.11 Axonometric rendering of the Mini City, an
architectural design of an experimental platform for autonomous
driving. ⏎

FIGURE 7.12 Plan of the Mini City at the McKelvey School of
Engineering at WashU. ⏎
From an engineering perspective, the testing platform enables
intermediate-scale physical experimentation that can evaluate the limits of
autonomy to increase safety at significantly lower costs and with a greater
scope of stress testing than full-scale facilities. This allows for explicitly
adversarial testing, causing the cars to malfunction or crash without
incurring the unnecessary risks and costs associated with full-scale testing.
From an architectural perspective, the proving ground is an environment
that stages speculation about how AVs can be integrated into cities to
improve public health and safety, encourage equitable access to public
transit, and support climate resilience. Addressing these challenges involves
simulating sociotechnical narratives that explore the introduction of AVs
and urban AI in cities, with consideration given to their spatial
implementation. The model city takes on the qualities of realism essential
for research to be applied to full-scale AVs in real-world scenarios. The
project's representation tactics include composite digital and hand
fabrication techniques to achieve equivalency with urban environments
(Figure 7.13). Material qualities like color, texture, sheen, opacity,
translucency, albedo, and luminosity are tuned to sensing technologies and
layered onto architectural form and urban space. The design process
accounts for machine vision, remote sensing, and navigation technology,
calibrating to these systems rather than human optics. For example, the
level of detail is determined by camera resolution and LIDAR indexing,
with relatively low pixel data and laser points at about 1-inch (2.5-cm)
spacing, thus reducing the need for details smaller than they can detect.
Additionally, interior spaces and levels above the first floor are not
necessary to describe for this training.

FIGURE 7.13 Street entourage prototypes at 1:8 scale, built for
the Mini City experimental platform for autonomous driving. ⏎
Despite the platform's equivalency to real-world environments, the
limitations of machine sensing create a training facility that is decidedly
constructed, which human optics can detect instantaneously. The model's
false facades are made to deceive, compressing the urban scale with
foreshortened floor heights and city blocks. The model need not be hyper-
detailed, just enough for the sensing apparatus to be able to train at its low
resolution, creating an uncanny effect.
Importantly, the Mini City's immersive environment can be transformed
over time, like a film set. Facades and roadways are built in low relief,
based on stage flats and risers typically used in theatrical productions. This
modular construction is designed to facilitate changing conditions for

staging a wide array of physical simulations, allowing various actors to
create different scenarios (Figure 7.14, Figure 7.15 and Figure 7.16).
Building form, public space, material characteristics, and atmospheres can
be controlled and altered, maximizing the urban, off-highway scenarios for
the miniature AV to encounter. Given its scenic and operative capacities,
events play a central role in shaping the model; they can be scripted,
animating the Mini City as an urban stage set attuned to the sensing
capabilities of autonomous vehicles.
FIGURE 7.14 Photograph of research assistants staging the 1:8
scale Mini City experimental platform for autonomous driving. ⏎

FIGURE 7.15 Streetlamps designed to receive electrical wiring
in the 1:8 scale Mini City. ⏎

FIGURE 7.16 Photograph of a detail from the 1:8 scale Mini
City experimental platform for autonomous driving. ⏎
This operability of the Mini City extends beyond its ongoing
reconfiguration for experimentation to its role in staging academic and
public engagement concerning AVs and AI in cities. We have invited the
academic community and the public to participate in active testing sessions
and discussions about autonomous driving and smart technologies. We also
envision future augmented reality (AR) components to demonstrate the
performance of navigation systems and design interventions. A forthcoming
exhibition will demonstrate parallel research related to this project in other
scales and digital testing environments. This layered role as a laboratory,
participatory theater, and exhibition hall situates the platform as a public
space structured around discussion and the building of trust. That is, the
project reimagines the relationship between research and public
engagement, proposing a future in which testing facilities become catalysts
for collective reflection and action, encouraging interactive discourse in

rethinking and transforming space and technology in relation to their
implications for society.
The latest phase of this project, An Index of Urban Artificial Intelligence
and Mobility Networks, extends the research at the Mini City to urban and
territorial scales. Research involves analyzing multimodal transportation
hubs and smart cities, as well as gathering data on AV ridesharing fleets,
autonomous trains and buses, new technologies in infrastructure, and public
policy surrounding autonomous driving under development by
corporations, institutions, and governments. This compendium of
information is fueling projective urban and architectural speculation on
innovations in public space, infrastructure, facades, and signage to be built
for testing with the scale model AV in the testing platform.
This ongoing research phase explores the interfaces designers might use
to guide interactions between humans and autonomous agents, aiming to
increase public safety. In this effort, we are looking at how to modulate
access ways and apertures in urban spaces and buildings shared between
humans, AVs, drones, and autobots, creating robotic portals in facades and
designing surface textures and patterns to communicate agents' operations
and sensing protocols to human actors. To achieve this, we are creating
drawings of interrelated digital systems and operable facade elements in
both 2D and 3D models that can be inserted into the Mini City to create
feedback loops between AI testing and environmental design.
Our study also investigates what opportunities autonomous mobility
offers designers to innovate in the built environment in ways that improve
public health. We are exploring how networked traffic information might
reduce roadways' scale, affording more room for public and green space
and promoting healthier cities. To do this, we are drawing into existing
transit networks to identify possible alterations to complex systems, using

traffic simulation and modeling software to test the impact of these changes,
and creating mockups in the Mini City.
We are also exploring how cities can implement AVs sustainably to
support climate resilience. This involves examining ways to connect AV
ridesharing with transit hubs to encourage multimodal travel and reduce
energy consumption, congestion, emissions, and sprawl. To implement this,
we are designing key attributes of a multimodal hub and considering how
those can integrate into existing networks by drawing and constructing a
model that plugs into the Mini City.
We are also researching how cities can incorporate AVs in ways that
encourage equitable access to public transit. This access is crucial for
people with disabilities, older adults, and people with lower incomes who
may not own a personal vehicle. Our study assesses how AVs can connect
public transit to those living outside existing routes or how autonomous
buses might be able to operate without set routes in response to real-time
data. This facilitates a reduction in the need for individual vehicle
ownership, which could, in turn, decrease the scale of infrastructure,
allowing cities to avoid perpetuating and even reverse the destruction of
neighborhoods along racial and economic lines. This study relies on traffic
simulation and modeling software.16
Speculative Scenography
Technologies like AVs and smart cities significantly impact the built
environment, and accounting for their effects is crucial to human safety and
trust, the health and sustainability of cities, and equitable access to and
implementation of transit. The consequences of this technology are even
more challenging to address, as cities and corporations gather real-time
feedback and form closed loops with little room for public input. Such

processes relate to what Picon describes as a new urban intelligence based
on emerging technology, "leading to the transition from the networked city
to the event-city." He goes on to explain:
Even more than urbanists' plans, it is the narratives and scenarios that
are inspired by them that allow cities to set objectives for themselves.
The often-denounced crisis of urban planning is revealed to be
contemporary with the rise in power of an event-based city, in which
reality and fiction are often difficult to distinguish.17
Architects and other spatial designers can effectively operate in this space,
working with other disciplines and engaging the public in design processes
to challenge the fictions being written into space and propose alternatives.
While cities fill with vast fleets of artificially intelligent actors feeding data
streams, they become hyper-stimulated by a cycle of events. Our
representations must follow suit in order to foster open, dialogical, political
exchange.
The operational quality of the digital models that architects design within
follows the event-city's narrative structure, much like animations, films,
and video games. A useful articulation of this kind of media can be found in
German filmmaker and media theorist Harun Farocki's 2014 lecture
"Computer Animation Rules," in which he stated, "A computer animation is
less a reproduction and more a production ... or creation of a model
world."18 Many of today's architectural models do not deal with
representation so much as theatrical production design or worldbuilding.19
In hybridizing architectural and scientific research, The Autonomous
Future of Mobility is scenography for machines used to train AVs, for
designers to test AI's implications in the built environment, and for
interactive performances for scholars, students, and the public to engage in

discussions, exhibitions, and workshops. The project examines how
architectural models can operate as interfaces that enact sociotechnical
narratives. The model has affinities with what Trevor Paglen describes as
"activations and operations"20 or what Benjamin Bratton refers to as the
"instrumental and mediational"21 highlighting how image recognition and
data feedback loops operate in cities today. In response to these
functionalities of the contemporary world, the model is not a static
architectural representation. Instead, it is an operable platform, a physical
simulation that can move and change over time, connecting digital and
material data. The Autonomous Future of Mobility is not merely a
reproduction of the world; it is a production, a performance taking place in
a world unto itself.
The Autonomous City Series and the Mini City provide examples of how
architects can inform the sociotechnical imaginary and participate in
authoring and co-authoring narratives that shape the future of the built
environment relative to technology. These projects frame speculative
fictions as spatial, collaborative enterprises, positioning them as places for
public discourse that can facilitate conversations addressing the ethical
questions that technologies like AVs and smart cities raise and their law and
policy implications. The Mini City proposes opportunities for collective
agency in designing proving grounds that better consider our shared ground.
In this way, the project aims to create an inclusive testing environment that
becomes a site of public imagination, activating space through its
operability. By envisioning models as activated objects informed by a logic
of events, situations, and scenarios, the project recognizes architects as key
contributors to the changes occurring in emerging technology.22
This research supports the position that proving grounds are vital spaces
for architects to engage with in examining the implications of emerging

technologies for the built environment and civic life. By participating
directly in the sociotechnical imagination, the narratives we can create
engage with the event-based nature of cities, much like those of computer
scientists, tech entrepreneurs, corporate entities, government agencies, and
the media.23 Critically, architects' narratives are co-creations, informed by
multiple constituencies through public engagement, approached from
various vantages, and often operate in research spaces that are free from the
incentivization of profit.
As parafictional scenic designs, proving grounds replace static objects
with mediated environments that activate space through performance, in
both the theatrical and operative senses. Envisioning architectural models as
spaces of performance affords new opportunities for architects to participate
in the changes taking place in emerging technology. We might see this as
what James Bridle describes as a "return to the communal and the social,"
facilitated through "greater collaboration with our technologies" and one
another.24 In this way, we might advance collective agency in addressing
how technologies are integrated into the built environment.25

8
ATADO CON ALAMBRE
The Alternative Production of
Ingenious Architectures
Carolina Dayer and Jonathan Foote
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-9
In the main street of a town, there was a large pothole that impeded passing
cars. This generated long queues of at least 20 minutes. Rather quickly, the
locals realized that they could creatively appropriate the inconvenient
situation. Near the pothole, shops started to appear, selling fruit, drinks, cell
phone accessories - products that drivers could buy while waiting. One day,
the town elected a new mayor who decided to fix the fault. To his surprise,
the locals were strongly opposed as it would mean the end of their business.
The mayor had no alternative but to accept that the pothole should remain.
This story, told by the Argentine professor, columnist, and business
administrator Claudio Zuchovicki, summarizes what happens when
precarity ignites practices of social production.1 It exemplifies a common
phenomenon in the Global South, where things happen spontaneously
around moments of disruption. The quick reaction to figure things out
produces unexpected networks of exchange, unique configurations, and,
when it comes to the built environment, distinct spatial practices.

In Argentina, a particular expression is used to refer to such situations
that are sticky and have no logical or apparent solution: atado con alambre,
or "fixed with wire." This common saying, indicative of cultural everyday
practices, is also employed by architects and non-architects to refer to
decisions made at construction sites and design development phases. To
resolve a predicament with 'wire' is to keep it working for another day, and,
when that day comes, a better solution will present itself. Sometimes that
day never comes, or, as in the case of the pothole story, when a solution
appears another may already have become a better opportunity. Each act of
resolution under such a paradigm is characterized by a personal touch of
creativity and a surplus sense of pride and effectiveness. Often, these
fabrications and resolutions are culturally recognized as witty and
humorous due to their graceful execution and unconventional pragmatism.
Such practices, highly present in self-built domestic constructions and
repairs, are not unique to Argentina. All across the globe, as scarcity and
precarity grow, this form of ingenuity increasingly populates urban and
suburban built environments, offering not just solutions but character and
unmapped cases of practical wisdom. Similarly, formalized architecture
design practices are often faced with economical and logistics constraints
that contribute to an attitude towards thinking astutely in order to get the
most out of the least. "Poor architecture," as Italian architect Lina Bo Bardi
explained in 1984, is not about poverty but about the capacity to express the
maximum through minor means.2
Examining three case studies from the South America, this chapter
exposes ingenious architectures that operate in environments of material
and economic scarcity. Connecting with a long tradition of related practices,
from ad-hoc to hacker to bricoleur, these architectures work with what is
within reach, not accepting materials not for what they supposed to be but

for what they could be. They salvage what they can from the residue of
mass materials, developing shrewd and cunning use of stuff. In contrast to a
regime of industrialized supply chains, ingenious architectures see materials
as part of an at-hand ecology. Perhaps it is even a new kind of
contemporary vernacular, showing another way of using resources, relating
to the environment, and benefiting the social aspects of a place.
Atado con alambre
In the Global North, the expectation of fixing life with a roll of cheap wire
is hardly imaginable. Rather, thousands of off-the-shelf solutions and ready-
mades emerge from the vast entanglement of oversaturated consumer
society. This has led, in advanced societies, to the alternative notion of
"home remedies" - do-it-yourself (DIY) solutions that are generally of
lower status when compared to the predominant options available through
consumer gateways and networks. Within architecture, in the early 2000s
the promise of "mass customization" led to the belief that technology had
finally given us tools to bridge the gap between the droning same-ness of
consumer society and the specific needs of individual consumers, giving
rise to a "bespoke" approach to design and construction.3 Yet, nothing in
these regimes empower individual and communal agencies beyond their
capacity to make consumer choices which are ultimately controlled by
architects and manufacturers operating with exclusive, digital means. Even
if today the focus on digital fabrication has waned, exceptional architecture
studios with small-scale projects continue to grapple with the luxury of
time, money, and use of the best crafts and materials for one-off projects.
Larger studios, in close collaboration with industry, struggle to fit within a
project's budget and time the attention needed to be inventive with details.
Building information modeling (BIM), building codes and regulations

complicate matters even more, and built solutions become highly repetitive
and standardized from project to project.
The operating paradigms between large and small studios in the Global
North hardly recognize the built production that is administered and
executed by non-architects, what Bernard Rudofsky called the "communal
vernacular."4 Although the production of the communal vernacular today is
not as pretty, organized and gradual as Rudofsky's examples, it is also not
very present in affluent countries. Where it happens, mostly in the Global
South, it is messy, precarious, sometimes humorous and witty (Figures 8.1
and 8.2). Where economic issues and less invasive industrial systems exist,
examples of the communal vernacular and its influence to architects have
much to teach us.


FIGURE 8.1 Added exhaust duct for a barbecue shop in La Plata,
Argentina. Photograph by author. ⏎
FIGURE 8.2 Membrane over roofs in São Paulo, Brazil, to
simplify repair work of localized leaks. Photograph by author. ⏎
To solve building problems atado con alambre provides an entry point
for examining what could be understood as a contemporary communal
vernacular. One can begin by taking a closer look at the phrase. Atado, from
the verb atar, denotes an act of tying or binding together. Rather than
binding with traditional material such as string or rope, generally derived
from plant materials, alambre (wire) references an archetypical material of
industrialized society. Impossible to produce autonomously, wire can
nonetheless be manufactured at a cost that practically anyone can afford.
Unlike the Global North, where metal wire is often highly specified and
marketed, a roll of bendable steel wire in Argentina is a general-purpose

staple of any household. The applications are endless. It is the quintessential
"at-hand" tool - able to bind just about anything with only the most
minimal means, creatively employed by individuals in the service of their
immediate needs and community.5 A roll of steel wire, then, is what Ivan
Illich, might wittingly call a "convivial tool" - "those which give each
person who uses them the greatest opportunity to enrich the environment
with the fruits of his or her vision."6 Following Illich, to solve a problem,
atado con alambre is fundamentally unproductive in the industrial sense of
efficiency; rather, it fosters "conviviality" in the "autonomous and creative
intercourse among persons, and the intercourse of persons with their
environment."7 A roll of steel wire is thus the ultimate convivial tool for
cleverly binding situations, relationships and, for architects, tricky
construction problems.
The dominant, industrialized approach to problem solving in the design
fields, particularly in the Global North, is embodied in the engineer, a
highly trained professional who imagines ideal and efficient solutions.
Arguably, the first engineer-architect in the Western context was Filippo
Brunelleschi. Using ingenious devices of his own invention, he rationalized
space by demonstrating mathematical perspective, initiated the construction
of the world's largest masonry dome at Santa Maria del Fiore in Florence,
and took control of medieval construction labor through a series of novel
building site protocols. He was also the first to be awarded a patent, one of
the legal foundations of industrialized society.8 Reflecting on the power
structures set by Brunelleschi at the construction site, Brazilian architect-
historian Sérgio Ferro wrote that "the complete separation between
construction labourers and the 'inorganic' (material, instrumental., etc.)
conditions of their labour" occurs at this time in history. Design, Ferro
argues, was formed to validate its capacity to "re-unite" labor on the

construction site.9 Such positioning of the discipline and its inherent control
over those who build buildings has since then shaped the state of
architecture as a form of capitalist production.10 To what extent this has
been done has tended to point to a well-known use and abuse of laborers
and a consequential erasure of their creativity and autonomy.11
In contrast to the engineer, Claude Lévi-Strauss famously described the
figure of the "bricoleur" as one who re-claims creativity and autonomy in
the shaping of the built environment. In La pensée sauvage from 1962, he
wrote:
The "bricoleur" is adept at performing a large number of diverse tasks;
but, unlike the engineer, he does not subordinate each of them to the
availability of raw materials and tools conceived and procured for the
purpose of the project ... the rules of his game are always to make do
with "whatever is at hand."12
In a more recent work, Irénée Scalbert examined the bricoleur in relation to
the figure of the architect, positioning bricolage as a "state of mind and
mode of design that embraces the rise of ecology." Moving away from an
emphasis on form in the practice of architecture, he sees bricolage as "a life
process ... [that] values flair, wisdom and forethought, resourcefulness,
deception and vigilance, opportunism, skills, and experience."13 It is
something quite close to the Roman notion of sollertia (from the Greek
metis), a kind of cunning intelligence which was described already by
Vitruvius as one of the fundamental skills of the architect.14 In other words,
the architect-bricoleur works cunningly with the residue of mass materials
and the left-overs of capitalism - collecting, assembling, and making do
with what is at hand. Ultimately, unlike the engineer, for whom materials
are realized through abstract, mathematical means, the architect-bricoleur

works with materials that are pre-constrained, concrete, and within a
specific context. It is a subversive practice that takes back power from the
traditional dependence of architecture on the separation of design and
construction.
The figurative aspect of atado con alambre obviously encompasses much
more than can be actually fixed with wire, to include practically any
material or resource that has been used before and is at hand. This is not
only a matter of ecological consequence. Just as industrialization creates
competition for scarce commodities, so do alternative, convivial practices
such as bricolage lead to abundant, cultural surplus - a reconnection
between architects and the work of the building site. In their 1960s' effort
with Arquitetura Nova, Sérgio Ferro, Rodrigo Lefèvre, and Flávio Império
considered how to empower workers through the use of technologies
known to them and through the design of buildings that would allow them
to perform autonomously within a particular set of skills. Throughout his
work, Ferro consistently held the belief that the "combination of autonomy
and solidarity can bring much joy in construction."15 For better or worse,
the messy and unstable dynamics that continue to shape life in the Global
South, not without important issues, can allow, more so than in the Global
North, for convivial exchanges in collaboration not just between architects
and workers but also environments.
As has been pointed out in relation to tools, Illich's notion of conviviality
is an ethical value that goes beyond the interaction between persons to also
consider interaction with environments, both built and natural.16 To extend
forms of collaboration beyond interpersonal relations implies looking at and
sympathizing with what is there, what is available; what are its qualities,
potentials, identities, and even problems. Bricolage and atado con alambre
are not forms of individual intelligence, but rely on and are validated within

the everyday life of a community. In the 1950s, Alison and Peter Smithson,
in searching for new ways of rethinking architecture, wrote about how
encountering mundane realities could allow for "an openness as to how
prosaic 'things' could re-energise our inventive activity."17 Making
architecture, they implied, should not just rely on obscure, isolated design
processes; it should be open to the existing ordinary worlds that make up
everyday life. Being attentive to existing environments, built and natural,
thus nurtures forms of conviviality among all participants of an
architectural project because it allows for collective and careful alliances
between workers, things, places, and design.
Many efforts by different groups of practicing architects, often small,
continue to show forms of resistance that support workers' emancipation
and production in the complicated and complex network of realizing and
defining architecture. Three cases from South America briefly examined
below look deeper into how such architects are working with material
scarcity and at-hand conditions to foster a new kind of convivial practice.
The insights result from a series of interviews and site visits with the
architects, who generously opened their doors and offered their reflections.
By understanding socio-economic situations, they make the most out of the
least. Without raising loud political, environmental or discursive flags,
emerging practices in South America offer alternative ways of working in
times of global crises.
Vagueness
Wonder is induced by the beautiful, the horrible, and the skillfully
made, by the bizarre and rare, by that which challenges or suddenly

illuminates our expectations, by the range of difference, even the order
and regularity, found in the world. - Caroline Walker Bynum18
At the reception of the Debut Award of the Lisbon Architecture Triennale of
2022, Anna Juni from vão, an architecture practice in São Paulo, started by
explaining their company name, which in Portuguese means both a
structural span and something vague. Exemplifying what vão is with images
of Lina Bo Bardi's Museo de Arte de São Paulo (MASP), Anna recalled that
their practice embodied both the engineering and structural efforts of what
it takes to make a span and the possibilities of free occupation generated by
the space Bo Bardi allowed to exist below the generous concrete structure
spanning a side of São Paulo's Avenida Paulista (Figure 8.3).19

FIGURE 8.3 Space beneath the expansive Museo de Arte de São
Paulo, designed by Lina Bo Bardi. Photograph by author. ⏎
To be vague, the vão architects argued, is to allow architecture to be
experienced in a multiplicity of forms. Vão's vagueness is also present in

how they work and organize their studio: they teach, they collaborate with
artists, they look at each project as an individual entity, they collaborate
with builders and acknowledge their skills, they research, and so on. While
vagueness is often associated with lack of precision, it can also be
associated with the acts of wondering, moving slowly, being attentive.20
One could connect this form of wonder to what historian Caroline Walker
Bynum wrote in her study on medieval notions of wonder: "wonder was a
response to something novel and bizarre that seemed both to exceed
explanation and to indicate that there might be reason (significance-not
necessarily cause) behind it."21
In our conversation with Anna Juni, Enk te Winkel and Gustavo
Delonero in November 2022, the architects explained:
When we talk about our work, we like to talk about processes. We
don't like to show the work as a great idea that emerged from nowhere.
Ideas emerge from possibilities and impossibilities. Ideas emerge to
justify and to enable that something is done, they don't emerge from
an aesthetic dream detached from a reality.
In their early award-winning project Subsolanus (Under the Sun) from 2016
- co-authored with Marina Canhadas and realized at the LIGA gallery,
Espacio para Arquitectura in Mexico City - vão's architects aimed to bring
natural air from the rooftop of a building, where the association held
conferences and meetings, to its ground floor, where a small exhibition
space existed. The installation on the existing building, designed by
Augusto Álvarez, stretched 30 meters high (Figure 8.4). The competition
call was entitled Geometrías Invisibles, and it called for an installation
piece at the very small gallery. Within the constraint of smallness, the
architects imagined instead a large, yet minor intervention full of air that

would acknowledge the area winds and the invisibility of such movements
into the little room while connecting to the rooftop activities. Such an idea,
they explained, came from a set of accidental and known references that
connected with São Paulo's everyday urban life and their studies from
architecture school.

FIGURE 8.4 Subsolanus installation by vão in Mexico City.
Photo courtesy of vão. ⏎

Inspired by situations they would normally encounter in the city, the
project emerged from two scenarios: a humorous arrangement of discarded
materials found randomly on the street (Figure 8.5) and the common
practice by homeless people of drying clothes/bedding at metro ventilation
openings (Figure 8.6). These encounters reminded the architects of
Rudofsky's published photographs of wind structures in West Pakistan, and
they related these references to the atmospheric conditions of Mexico
City.22 Because the gallery was so small, it seemed the only possible
intervention was to fill it with air. The project was realized with very little
money (2,000 USD) and a lot of courage, as they recalled. Silver tape,
plastic membranes and a minimal steel structure brought not just air to the
small gallery but also public attention and visibility due to its unimagined
scale (Figures 8.7 and 8.8). The effort was possible due to a lot of
mentoring, consultations and interactions with the engineer Rui Furtado, the
constructor BonifÁcio López, and a former professor, Marta Bogéa.

FIGURE 8.5 Arrangement with discarded materials found in the
streets of São Paulo by vão. Photo courtesy of vão. ⏎
FIGURE 8.6 Drying bedding over metro ventilation grills. Photo
courtesy of vão. ⏎

FIGURE 8.7 Subsolanus installation detail by vão in Mexico
City showing plastic membrane. Photo courtesy of vão. ⏎

FIGURE 8.8 Subsolanus installation detail by vão in Mexico
City showing steel structure. Photo courtesy of vão. ⏎
Vão's light, humorous way of working can be found in many of their
built projects, where a sense of wit and joy surrounds their decisions. This
form of knowledge appears to rely on both their formal education and the
diverse conditions they encounter in their everyday life, confronted by
socio-political and economical struggles and a constant vibrancy in the
cultural practices of making ends meet. Like a bricoleur working with what
is available, they humbly augment the convivial life of the city through the
built environment.
Differential Thickness
Marginal objects, objects with no clear place, play important roles. On
the lines between categories, they draw attention to how we have
drawn the lines. Sometimes in doing so they incite us to reaffirm the

lines, sometimes to call them into question, stimulating different
distinctions.23
- Sherry Turkle
The Argentinean parrilleros (grillers) who sell sausages and other meat on
the city's streets have a particular technique to attract customers. They place
a small lump of fat in the fire to expand the reach of the smoke in the hope
of attracting more customers within a larger radius. Something local and
marginal, as Turkle argues, becomes expansive by increasing the possibility
of how far it can stretch. Spatial practices as such are important to Juan
Pablo Berbery, an architect and teacher working in the capital, Buenos
Aires. Juan Pablo's studio is called OTRO ("Other") and he is a member of
the collective Dispositivos Nómades, which studies and documents urban
nomadic devices such as those that the grillers and other businesses build
and move around cities. His interest in urban and suburban socio-cultural
artifacts and built conditions manifests also in the way he practices the
discipline.
The project Casa Yaraví in Moreno, exemplifies Juan Pablo's approach.
Moreno is a city bordering Buenos Aires that he associates with the Guaraní
term yeré, which he explains is something that surrounds and protects
something else.24 A yeré can be a good friend or an enclosure around a
house to protect it from wind and heat. He considers Moreno to be this
bordering structure of the capital, wanting to have a bit of the center but
always remaining on the periphery. The project is located in a modest
middle-class neighborhood, and he was commissioned to essentially make a
yeré, an open carport for one vehicle on one side of a house and a gallery
for drinking mate (a herbal infusion) or similar outdoor activities on the
other. The project's minimal budget entailed that most materials had to be

found, and labor was conducted by the architect himself together with his
cousin.
Leftovers from another construction site determined the materials to
begin the work. More than 200 French roof tiles were transformed into two
columns, one at the front and one at the back. The architect's aim was to
maximize the space on the ground floor with minimal disruption, and, if
needed, to expand the compact carport to fit two cars if needed for visitors.
Garages/carports in Argentina are extremely versatile spaces - used for
anything from birthday parties and Sunday barbecue lunches to gym classes
organized among neighbors. The garage embodies a space quality that Juan
Pablo likes to call "differential thickness." The columns to allow room for
such spaces were then made by covering the stacked French tiles with
cement mortar to try to erase their identity as much as possible (Figure 8.9).
Two holes were made in each tile to tide them structurally with reinforcing
bars, a literal example of atado con alambre (Figure 8.10).

FIGURE 8.9 Column project at Casa Yaraví by OTRO, Buenos
Aires Photo by Federico Cairoli. Courtesy of Juan Pablo Berbery.
⏎
FIGURE 8.10 Construction of column project at Casa Yaraví:
using a tuna can to smooth the mortar joints. Photo courtesy of

Juan Pablo Berbery. ⏎
The two columns sit on small, non-visible concrete bases. They are
topped with a steel beam that connects to a flexible joint to allow for
discrete movement due to wind and other factors. The beam is helped by a
steel cable connected to the column's foundation at one end and the existing
roof of the house at the other. From this beam, two smaller ones hang
perpendicular on one side and on the other side rest on the party wall of the
lot (Figures 8.11 and 8.12). The new garage and gallery coverings are made
from a simple steel frame with a corrugated metal sheet. The witty,
seemingly fragile structure lightens the entrance and the back of the house,
maximizing the space and responding to the clients' needs with the minimal
use of materials.

FIGURE 8.11 Drawings of Casa Yaraví project by OTRO
showing the two new additions at the front and back of the house.
Drawing courtesy of Juan Pablo Berbery. ⏎

FIGURE 8.12 Column project at Casa Yaraví. Photo courtesy of
Federico Cairoli. ⏎
Like the bricoleur, when it comes to designing buildings, Juan Pablo
Berbery's approach encourages the accumulation of partial fragments. He
begins projects by understanding different spatial situations that involve
people, physical components and their performance to make "life" happen.
In his design thinking, he insists on expressing the weight of things and
their potential associations. Within this vibrant intellectual position,
fragments, pieces, devices can respond to different problems, and in their
material and structural accumulation can create new spatial possibilities.
Differential thickness is a concept that Juan Pablo employs to expand on
what perhaps anthropologist Lucy Suchman called "situated action."
Suchman elaborates on this notion to explain that
the course of action depends in essential ways upon the action's
circumstances. Rather than attempting to abstract action from its
circumstances and reconstruct it as a rational plan, the approach is to
study how people use their circumstances to achieve intelligent
action.25
Suchman gives agency to particular actions to indicate that fragments and
smaller units of execution are determinant of larger plans (or designs) and
not just subordinates. Differential thickness is then the space of architecture
to embody many places, to reach out far and near, to transform thin
materials into thick ones, and the capacity of exercise intellectual
permutations that shuffle and recombine known things into unexpected and
surprising ones.

Opportunism
Sometimes we have to talk to explain what is it that we are not yet
sufficiently clear about, but in the conversation it appears with great
force and that's good. We are made by others.26
- Solano Benitez
While opportunism can be associated with morally selfish acts, in
architecture it can also be a form of knowledge that finds chances among
defined systems or seemingly useless materials or conditions. Forms of
positive opportunism at many scales are found the built work of Argentine
architect Agustín Berzero, based in Córdoba, capital of the eponymous
province. During an interview at his house in the city, when asked, for
example, about the kitchen table at which we were seated, which was a
concrete slab, he responded that he had made it from an earlier table which
he did not like and, to give it a purpose, he used it as a framework for the
present one. The house he built for himself mostly comprises exposed
thermal bricks, a common and inexpensive material produced in Córdoba
that defines mostly low-cost constructions by non-architects (Figure 8.13).
Agustín used this material in multiple ways, such as for load-bearing walls
and as a thin screen on the front facade that provides security and privacy to
the house. He created the screen by cutting each thermal block (on-site) into
three parts. By thinning the bricks, he reduced the weight of the screen and
maximized the use of the material. The house, which is full of details made
with recycled/repurposed items and a clever use of materials is a modern
monument to opportunism. Agustín believes that each project is indeed an
opportunity to do something graceful, joyful. Instead of repeating solutions

from project to project, each exercise is a chance to investigate materials
differently.
FIGURE 8.13 Agustín Berzero's house in Córdoba, Argentina
showing thermal blocks cut to make a thin screen. Photo courtesy
of Federico Cairoli. ⏎
As Solano Benitez quoted above, a project appears by both the architect
and the conditions - materials, labor, time - in a conversation. In two
projects, he has been exploring how to articulate brick bonding so that all
the plumbing and electricity infrastructure can be built concurrently within
the wall section. To make this happen, in the Tres Casas project, Agustín
Berzero developed a bonding typology that works vertically, leaving linear
ducts to embed the various tubes and pipes needed to supply the house
(Figure 8.14). Working closely with construction workers, they tested the
bonding detail on site, which proved to work well. The brick walls work
simultaneously with concrete columns and slabs that together provide
stability to the entire spatial system (Figure 8.15). Agustín challenges

himself to make a wall section that simultaneously considers aspects of
structural and infrastructural resolution driven by several factors. One of
them is the desire to honor the skill of bricklayers by avoiding any post-
demolition to install pipes on the walls. Another factor is the desire to
reduce the use of materials by avoiding any rendering or plastering of walls.
To attain this, wall resolution must be done in the tidiest, most skilled way.
And a third aspect is the concurrent planning of workers' schedules,
bricklayers, plumbers and electricians, so they can all work in the most
efficient way as the walls go up. The resourceful handling of workers' time
reduces costs for the client while providing the workers with a clear
understanding of the project's logic, which they appreciate as they are not
faced with surprises when tasks are disconnected for each other's field of
expertise.
FIGURE 8.14 Brick bonding of Tres Casas project in Córdoba by
Agustín Berzero. Photo courtesy of Agustín Berzero. ⏎

FIGURE 8.15 Brick walls showing vertical bonding and concrete
columns of Tres Casas. Photo courtesy of Agustín Berzero. ⏎
In Agustín Berzero's Casa Cerámica project in Córdoba, another
experiment with brick walls occurs. In this case, the use of thermal ceramic
blocks, which are thicker than regular bricks, opened up the opportunity for
the architect to imagine with its hollowness (Figures 8.16 and 8.17). The
vertical cavities are obtained by making strategic cuts in some of the blocks
where the required ducts need to go through the wall. Again, the decision
was made with the desire to avoid any post-demolition of the wall to install
infrastructural elements. For Agustín, the premise of working with scarcity
in the most graceful way is manifest in all the projects he undertakes.

FIGURE 8.16 Construction of Casa Cerámica in Córdoba by
Agustín Berzero, showing internal cavities for service pipes and
ducts. Photo courtesy of Agustín Berzero. ⏎

FIGURE 8.17 Construction of Casa Cerámica by Agustín
Berzero. Photo courtesy of Agustín Berzero. ⏎
The practice of "turning things around," just like the atado con alambre
expression, has its own formalized significance in Spanish: buscarle la

vuelta, which means to look for the right "turn" so something can work, so
something can fit well. In the constant movement of searching for
something through conversations around models, one can realize and
discover structural and spatial qualities of different architectural
components. This practice of "turning things around" resonates with the
work of Argentinean architect Rafael Iglesia, who saw architecture in
constant potency towards new arrangements and opportunities. Speaking
about his work, Ana María Rigotti explains that Iglesia understood
architecture components as
objects that assert themselves in their autonomy and matter and refer to
playful, childish experiences, to the simple logic of support and
balance of wooden blocks, puzzles, chopsticks, playing cards ...
always out of tune with the logic of codes and reconsidering the
elements as interchangeable pieces to assemble.27
The playful nature of Iglesia's approach to architecture is embodied in
Agustín's methodology.
"Unconcludingly" ...
For both the philosopher and the humourist, nothing is to be taken for
granted: everything can be looked at with a questioning, experimental,
even irreverent eye.28
- John Morreall
Morreall's observation puts the humorist and the philosopher in the same
pot. Although from quite different disciplines, they both deal with
understanding human conditions and, for this reason, the approach is the

same. Perhaps one could make a similar observation between architects and
non-architects involved in shaping their built environment. Instead of
seeing a formal divide, separated by education, certification, and access to
specialized tools, we could frame their approaches through the common
pursuit of conviviality. As the practice of architecture in the Global North
faces its dependence on economic growth and accumulated capital -
arguably in place since Brunelleschi - it must discover alternatives to the
continued complicity in the status quo. Such emerging architects as have
been exposed here, only a few of the many unheralded practices throughout
the Global South, work with humor and wit and embed themselves in their
communities, thus offering inspiration for doing more with less - for fixing
difficult life situations with just a spool of metal wire.
