9
DRIVEN TO DISTRACTION
Matthias Altwicker
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-10
A quantifiable understanding of the environment - how architecture
responds to it, how architecture impacts it - has taken an outsized position
in the profession since the mid-1990s. Meeting environmental performance
standards and certification forms an immense part of the responsibility of
today's architect. With these standards adopted and codified by
governments, municipalities, and private clients, they have become as
critical to the profession as health, safety, and welfare codes.
This standardization of environmental responses creates an illusion that
this should be considered progress. In almost all cases, the response to
environmental performance has left architects in the position of controlling
the environment rather than understanding it. Do we give away ownership
of decisions on materials and systems to experts who can use the
quantifications to make decisions? Do we invest time and knowledge in our
understanding of these standards to manage them and use them to our
advantage, even at the expense of other project goals we may have? Or do
we instead try to understand the environment, and discover new ways to
interact with it beyond quantifiable science? How architects respond to the
increased pressures and responsibilities generated by environmental

concerns is the primary decision we make today, and the path forward has
many forks in it and distractions along it.
Biosfera and Definitely Maybe
The Biosphere - the conception of synchronized elements of the natural
world - is less than 100 years old, first appearing in the lectures of Russian-
Ukrainian mineralogist Vladimir Vernadsky and published in Biosfera (The
Biosphere) in 1926. Its broader influence began to take root in the western
world in the 1960s, paralleling the political directives for the quantitative
measurement of the environment that developed out of a scientific-
industrial directive that itself was a response to the 1973 oil crisis.
Vernadsky provided the scientific community and ecologists with a newly
conceived notion of the interrelationship of planetary processes in Biosfera.
It posits that all parts of the earth, not just the living ones, are inextricably
linked in a self-regulating complex system that maintains life on the planet.
With this thesis, Vernadsky simultaneously asks two things of scientists and
researchers: The first is that everything in the Biosphere needs to be
measured. The second is that our understanding of the Biosphere must
extend through time, from the beginning of life back to the pre-geological
stages of planetary evolution. He was not concerned with degradation of the
environment by human development because degradation happened as part
of the natural world.
For Vernadsky, one of the problems with gaining a complete picture of
the environment was the disparate understandings of how to look at the
environment. For him, "Observations of nature gives indications of this
mechanism in the biosphere"1 - and this measuring and observing natural
phenomena could allow us to better understand the things we could not
measure or were unable to measure. While this appears on the surface to

indicate an approach based in quantification, the nuance of the work is
about the gaining of knowledge, not only through measurement but through
a variety of observations.
Arkady and Boris Strugatsky's science fiction novel Definitely Maybe: A
Manuscript Discovered Under Unusual Circumstances picks up on these
concerns and offers a parable to all professionals, including architects, who
deal with the measuring of the environment. First published in 1976 (and
written in the same year as the oil crisis), it uses the authors' familiarity
with Vernadsky's work on the Biosphere as a basis for the novel's unseen
protagonist - the homeostatic universe. In the novel, this universe subtly
adjusts the lives of scientists poised to complete breakthrough research in
fields of understanding the environment. The distractions are in human
form - cerebral, comic, erotic, mundane - and present themselves as minor
adjustments to the path of human thought, so subtle as to (almost) avoid
suspicion and detection. Yet they lead the scientists away from pushing their
breakthroughs forward. The novel is structured around two groups of
scientists, each a reflection of various attitudes towards science (and
parallel concerns of scientific research): the believers - those who accept
the scientific parameters and allow them to guide their choices; and the
dialecticians - who seek a deeper understanding of the environment through
their connection to it, both through experience and study.
Believers
The first group in Definitely Maybe, the main characters, are scientists and
researchers - Malianov (an astrophysicist), Vecherovsky (a mathematician),
and Weingarten (a biologist) (Figure 9.1). Each is committed to their
research for their own intellectual reasons, and they neatly reflect the
sciences critical to the Vernadskyian multilayered system of the Biosphere.

It is the character of Vecherovsky - himself a surrogate for Vernadsky -
who analyzes the situation of distraction as a theorist of progress, and who
defines the homeostatic universe to his colleagues:
If only the law of nondecreasing entropy existed, the structure of the
universe would disappear, and chaos would reign. But, on the other
hand, if only a constantly self-perfecting and all-powerful intelligence
prevailed, the structure of the universe based on homeostasis would
also be disrupted ... a constantly developing intelligence can have but
one goal: to change nature. That is why the gist of the Homeostatic
Universe consists in maintaining the balance between the increase in
entropy and the development of reason. That is why there are no and
can be no supercivilizations, since the term supercivilization is used
for intelligence developed to such a degree that it transcends the law of
nondecreasing entropy on a cosmic scale. And what was happening to
us now was nothing other than the first reaction of the Homeostatic
Universe to the threat of humanity becoming a supercivilization. The
universe was defending itself.2

FIGURE 9.1 Malyanov, Snegovoi, and Vecherovsky consider the
seemingly random proceedings. Still from Days of Eclipse, 1988;
Arkady and Boris Strugatsky, screenplay. (image courtesy
Sputnik/Lenfilm) ⏎
Of the believers, Vecherovsky remains calm about the distractions
happening to all of them as he has already theorized why they are
happening. This calms him down and he presents himself as the voice of
reason to the rest of the scientists. Malianov, on the other hand, only sees
his work going off-track, and is increasingly bothered by the distractions
which continue throughout the novel - many of them coming from the other
scientists themselves. He is resentful that he cannot get his brilliant work
finished. Weingarten, the third believer, is also the one who has been
dealing with the distractions the longest. His approach is to continue his
work at a level that will avoid the distractions but continue to satisfy his
mind. The believers represent certainly the basic positions of scientific
research within a centralized, authoritarian decision-making process, one in
which ordinary people have no role. The characters are very much a
reflection of scientists under the environmental approaches of the early
20th-century Russian regimes, changes in the management of the
environment and ecology of Russia that impacted the research agendas of
its scientists.3
Dialecticians
The second group in Definitely Maybe are those considering the events and
questioning themselves about how to respond to them: Snegovoi, a
physicist and colonel; Gubar, an engineer and craftsman; and Glukhov, an
Orientalist. Together with the scientists, they characterize the problems with

the scientific community that Vernadsky believes lead to a
misunderstanding of the Biosphere.
preconceived ideas, long a part of geological thought, seem to
contradict the method of empirical generalization in science,
generalizations which constitute the fundamental discoveries of natural
scientists. One of these conceptions is that geological phenomena are
accidental coincidences of causes, essentially blind, and obscure
because of their complexity and number. This preconceived idea is
only partly related to certain philosophical-religious interpretations of
the work; mainly it is based upon incomplete logical analysis of our
empirical knowledge.4
The dialecticians questioning themselves and their experiences - rather than
the science - creates a strong counterpoint in the book to the believers.
Snegovoi, the most vocal of them, is the first to state his paranoia - that his
interrupted research is caused by secret matters they cannot explain. When
he is later killed, the others discover that he is both a member of the
military and a scientist, which to them calls his science and very being into
question. It propels the characters into trying to resolve the events in their
head. Gubar, whose distractions are headaches keeping him from writing
and a newly discovered son, sees positive energy in the distractions that
allow him to keep researching without drawing any conclusions.
The third character in this group, Glukhov, the pure scholar, has decided
to become "one with nature, not to dig around in it". In this way he
articulates similar observations made by a group of scientists in the 1970s
led by chemist James Lovelock and microbiologist Lynn Margulis that
formed the beginnings of the Gaia principle - a self-regulating system
between living and non-living.5 This was the first time in the 20th century

that the concept of the self-regulating system was articulated. It was also a
concept that was presented simultaneously as a spiritual belief system, as a
way to bring individuals into connection with the scientific principles
behind the theory. Glukhov articulates this as only a scientist who has
undergone a change in his thinking can:
You must choose life! What else? Surely not your telescopes and test
tubes. Let them choke on your telescopes! And interstellar gases! You
have to live, love, feel nature - really feel it, not dig around in it!
When I look at a tree or a bush now, I feel. I know that it is my friend,
that we exist for each other, that we need each other.6
This method of connection to the earth was especially prevalent in Russia at
the time Definitely Maybe was written. What informed this diverse set of
attitudes towards science and discovery? Arkady and Boris Strugatsky (born
1925 and 1933) grew up and lived through major transformations in
Russian/Soviet society until their deaths (in 1991 and 2012, respectively).
Their professional work in the military and at university as they began their
writing careers brought them into contact with a wide variety of opinions on
the changes taking place their home country.7 They came into contact with
people whose work was directly impacted by the shifting changes and
approaches to its environment and ecology. The country implemented
various environmental approaches to land and ecology during the life of the
Strugatskys. Under Lenin, over 6 million hectares of land were preserved to
protect the environment. Under Stalin, the industrial and productive agenda
outweighed the preservation of resources and drove large portions of the
preserved land into deep contamination. In parallel, the autonomy of the
scientific community (of which Vernadsky was a part) was lost.8

During the Cold War, leaders from Khrushchev to Brezhnev to
Gorbachev constantly altered environmental agendas, always driven by two
critical parameters: the role of the environment in relation to military
concerns and food production. These two goals worked to regenerate
pastoral pride and agricultural archetypes in the 1970s and 1980s - local
ecological groups who, like Glukhov, saw a different set of values in the
environment. The characters in the parable of Definitely Maybe pick up
clearly on this changing environmental policy and its seeming lack of effect
on human difficulties. Indeed, all the main characters in the story are the
result of the return of the autonomous scientific community under
Khrushchev's de-Stalinization.9 Here, Weingarten represents the scientific
research system tied to the government, with Malianov representing a
broader, more global significance of Nobel Prize research, much like his
colleague Vecherovsky, whose research is done in perfect isolation toward a
greater goal.
The book poses important questions about how humans are deeply
complicit in distracting themselves from achieving the goals that we hold
for our work and our world, while also offering possible ways of engaging
with those questions. As each character reacts to a situation, they respond
and adapt in different ways. The situation of these scientists is like that of
architects today - professionals who need to respond to the hard facts of
specialists (with specialized knowledge) while maintaining a focus on the
larger goals of the work. The larger goals necessarily incorporate
considerations of environmental performance, but also need to embody a
much more holistic view of the homeostatic universe.

The Biosphere and Architecture
The questions posed by Vernadsky, articulated by the Strugatskys, remain at
the forefront of an architect's task today: what to measure and, more
importantly, what the value of the measurements should be. There are over
600 sustainability certifications for projects worldwide, each with a various
standards and different ranges of focus, from single-attribute product
certifications to multiple-attribute building certifications (Figure 9.2).10
Large-scale impacts within the measurement of comfort and energy,
building scale measurement of construction to site effects, and detail and
component measurements are all part of the scope of the architect.
The majority of the certifications place the largest focus on criteria
within the environmental dimension which accounts for an average of
52% of the overall weighting across the certifications. The social
dimension accounts for 43% of the overall weighting across
certifications, with a large focus on the indoor environment
represented by the health aspect ... economy is only represented in the
certifications to a very low degree (an average of 5% of the overall
weighting). However, the aspects in the environmental and social
dimensions can potentially have an impact on the economic value of
the building and thus the economic dimension could be argued to
indirectly become a focus through the other dimensions.11

FIGURE 9.2 Global Distribution of Primary Sustainable
Certifications. Guide to Sustainable Building Certifications;
SBi/GXN, 2018 (image courtesy GXN) ⏎
Sustainability certifications can be categorized into three types: single-
attribute product certifications, multiple-attribute product certifications, and
multiple-attribute building certifications. Product certifications focus on one
or more sustainability aspect or quality of a product's performance, such as
energy efficiency rating, reduced water usage or sustainable procurement of
natural resources, such as timber. They may also extend to a range looking
at various characteristics of a product, such as life-cycle cost, quality
control, and energy usage. Product certifications, in general, improve
possibilities for comparison but can mislead about the overall sustainability
of the product. Multiple-attribute building certifications seek to establish a
holistic rating that goes beyond aggregating individual components of the
project.
This articulates the difficulty architects face in balancing scales of
measurements of detail and urban qualities and impacts. How do architects
deal with these parameters? Architects are inherently problem solvers, so it
is not a surprise that the profession has embraced a quantifiable approach.
The basis of all codes/certification is human comfort. Is this even

measurable? And what about human performance that is necessary to put
comfort levels in place and make them operational?
Hard Technology = Believers
One response would be analogous to the efforts of the scientists in
Definitely Maybe - ongoing study and quantification toward improvements
that benefit the researchers and the human interests in the studies. Scientific
research and development around the built environment have primarily
worked in the realms of technical solutions to problems. Much like the
certifications themselves, the research agendas occur at three scales: the
building, the planning, and the material.
In the United States, environmental building standards began to influence
development and construction as early as the inaugural Earth Day in April
1970, when New York and Michigan adopted environmental standards into
their Bill of Rights. This was prescient, as the 1973 oil crisis accelerated
small, measurable changes that occurred over the next 15 years. These
changes were large and impactful - speed limits for automobiles, permanent
daylight saving time, and energy department tax credits all reduced oil
consumption by 37%. Additional American Society of Heating,
Refrigerating and Air-Conditioning Engineers (ASHRAE) code
developments based on increased ventilation exchanges and the elimination
of chemical elements in many materials further improved the health of
buildings and environments.12 However, despite their success in specific
areas, they had no directives or impact on the construction industry.
The first established environmental assessment method for buildings was
Building Research Establishment Environmental Assessment Methodology
(BREEAM) in 1990, which grew out of a government-funded research
entity that used the scientific-industrial directive as a way to tie together

policy and standards toward a common goal. In its earliest manifestations it
was about both better building performance and safety - along with
protecting government financial investment.13 The scale of this
environmental response - a single building - is easy to understand but has
minimal impact. The certified buildings being constructed each year make
up just 1-2% of the total building stock, meaning it will be generations
before there is a tipping point between certified and uncertified buildings
globally.14
The language of assessment and improvement was adopted from that of
the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP), started in 1972.
Although the broad gesture sought to begin the development of a global
energy policy, much of the document focuses on developing countries and
their "underdevelopment" as an environmental risk to the inhabitants and
the investments in those countries by other countries. The UN was using the
concepts of the Biosphere as a way to contextualize its work with an eye
towards larger-scale, centralized methods of management:
As far as deliberate weather modifications were concerned, there was
support for the Executive Director's proposal that action in this area
should be initiated; one representative called for legal studies
concerning this subject. Another representative suggested that an
international technological assessment should be carried out of all
future large-scale projects which were likely to change· the climate.
Yet another representative suggested that expert groups should
evaluate the likelihood and magnitude of the effects of changes in the
micro- and macro-climate on man and the biosphere.15
Environmental reporting from the 1970s onward, including the Brundtland
Report of 1987 (Our Common Future), insisted that "it is obvious that the

problems cannot be solved by one group, one group working in separation.
That is impossible."16 Rather than encouraging cooperation, this tended to
centralized thinking and planning on both the policy and the planning level,
continuing the fascination of politicians and architects with global-scale
environmental solutions. Projects as wide-ranging as Atlantropa by Herman
Sörgel in 1932, The Transformation of Nature by the Stalin regime in 1949
(Figure 9.3), or Eneropa by Rem Koolhaas' Office for Metropolitan
Architecture (OMA) in 2010 all use the language of reorganization and
control as a path to improving environmental performance and quality of
life.
FIGURE 9.3 Territorial reorganization under the guise of the
environment: "We Shall Overcome the Dry Season Too"; Stalinist
Propaganda Poster, 1949 (courtesy Wikimedia/Russian State
Library) ⏎

What there is very little of in these proposals is an agenda to understand
the biosphere; instead, we see the extreme investment of thought on how to
change the Biosphere. These agendas tended to develop without significant
impact on either economic or social levels. They also could not survive
financial stress, whether temporary shocks to labor markets or large-scale
slowdowns such as the 2008 crisis.
At the material scale, the construction industry needed to adapt to the
certifications and performance standards. On the base level, it has done so
with increased product verifications and studies that are subject to the
required third-party testing for approval; on another level, they have needed
to develop new products to meet those criteria. Perhaps the most interesting
and significant changes have been on the component level. Concrete is well
known as the worst material system for embodied carbon and circular
performance - in addition to being poor based on simpler metrics related to
thermal transfer. In response, the concrete industry has developed recycled
concrete aggregates, lightweight insulating aggregates, and reinforced
thermal bridging elements to improve the thermal performance of the
overall assembly system. Unfortunately, the building material assemblies
that exist globally have changed very little over time, and the expectations
of most developers have remained focused on minimal improvements.
Building an all-glass building with better-performing recycled glass and the
air-conditioning system powered by photovoltaics is indicative of the many
problems that the hard technology approach brings to architecture.
Soft Environmentalism = Dialecticians
The efforts of the dialecticians in Definitely Maybe - those searching for a
balance between humanity and science - parallel the soft environmentalism
movement. First championed and outlined by Denton Morrison, it offered

an alternate approach to the hard technological approach championed by
both government decisions and those that put business first.
The general enthusiasm, idealism, and even euphoria over the
environment that peaked around Earth Day facilitated the rapid
development of the legislative and regulatory means of implementing and
enforcing environmental reforms. Implementation of these reforms rapidly
changed this mood to one of realism as a stream of conflicts arose over the
substance, costs, methods, and rates of environmental protection.17
As mentioned earlier, environmental building standards began to
influence development and construction from the first Earth Day in 1970,
with New York and Michigan introducing environmental standards into
their Bill of Rights. As these developments continued through the 1973 oil
crisis, they had an impact on oil consumption and policy related to it. In the
20-year period after the crisis, oil consumption was reduced by 37%
through relatively simple changes in policy - for example, the
implementation of speed limits on the interstates, improved automobile
standards for efficiency, and the introduction of daylight saving time.18 Yet
the impact of these changes was minor within the bigger picture and did not
change problematic societal habits and activities.
Morrison's environmentalism advocated for an emphasis on local
production or sustained provision of local basic needs, which in turn would
create a focus on improved quality of life, not economic growth. Support
for local works, resources, and economies plays a large role in many
certifications, both to minimize embodied carbon levels by reducing
material travel times or by requiring local labor to be used for construction
teams. The Living Building Challenge in particular focuses on the social
impacts of sustainability, and it remains the certification with the least
certified buildings among the major ones.

Architects
Medicine, architecture, law, education, transportation, social work, and civil
engineering have all followed the path of increasingly professionalized,
more restricted, and less beneficial application of their skills. Our reaction
to their increasing damage and failure to achieve their aims has been to
escalate their technological, bureaucratic, and energetic growth. Such
fundamentally wrong assumptions can be countered only by de-
professionalizing the social agencies, setting limits to their operation, and
reassuming individual responsibility for their purposes.19
Today many architects immerse themselves in the entire building project,
and doing so requires a reframing of what the practice might be. While it
may include having certification-qualified architects on staff, this would be
the minimum level of engagement. Practices such as Gray Organschi
Architecture (GOA), based in New Haven, USA, embed research on carbon
transects and fabrication processes into their design process, allowing them
to use scientific approaches to architecture to better understand the issues
directly and to create new methods of communicating these concerns to
their clients. Architects emerging from the era of environmental movements
similarly reframed their practices to involve methodologies that would
understand the broader impacts as a way to better control them. Christopher
Alexander understood well all aspects of a development project, from
financing to construction, to supervision and decision making - in this way
controlling the product and the values it was intended to hold. The method,
more than the science, became the focus of the work.
At the same time that Alexander's research and development office began
to flourish, in 1973 the US National Academy of Sciences completed a
study of strategies for research in the environmental realm. Recognizing the
absence of an orderly development of research, the study posited two key

positions. The first was that of a collective conscience - that research is
necessary as a means of preventing problems through knowledge. This
leads to the second position: that risk taking needs to be considered as part
of both development and prevention models, not only on the side of safety
and business protection but also to allow flexibility in standards and
research. This flexibility is based on decision-theory thinking that does not
allow for rational decisions to be made under conditions of uncertainty.20
At the end of the novel Definitely Maybe, Glukhov (the engineer/soldier)
and Gubar (the scholar) retire to "the quiet life, for irresponsibility".
Weingarten (the biologist) accepts his appointment as head of a
government-funded research institute, where he will "do ten Nobel-winning
projects ... It's sort of like my birthright. The right of Weingarten to have
freedom of scientific curiosity."21 Malianov (the astrophysicist), despondent
and overwhelmed by events, gives up his research and breaks off contact
with the others, leaving Vecherovsky to work alone, in resistance while
understanding the futility of his approach:
The laws of nature must be studied and then put to use ...This law did
not manifest itself before our time, though it may be no accident that
Newton got caught up in interpreting the apocalypse and Archimedes
was cut down by a drunken soldier. The problem is that the law
manifests itself in only one way - through unbearable pressure.
Pressure that threatens your mind and even your life. But nothing can
be done here. Perhaps with time we will learn to channel the pressure
into harmless areas, and maybe even harness it to our own goals.22
Vecherovsky's attitude to the situation changed subtly over the course of the
novel; it could be understood as change from believer to dialectician. This
gives us much insight into a path forward for architects. Both hard

technology and soft environmentalism need quantification and science to
further their goals, but for architects the hopefulness of Vecherovsky's last
statement is to see that, with time, there is a way to direct the pressures
toward one's own goals. We need to set the goals, not the standards, and
then move forward with our work. If those goals are simply meeting
numerical values, then we are not seeing the totality of the way our work
connects with the entire environment, with the Biosphere. If we understand
the complex interactions of the Biosphere and become overwhelmed by the
complexity, we have not reframed the situation as one that is beneficial to
the work. Then the setting of standards will not only fail to have significant
environmental impact, but will also keep us from finding other avenues to
move our work forward.

10
SYN(ES)THETIC FUTURES
Lessons from the Hermetically
Sealed
Brian Ambroziak and Katherine Bambrick
Ambroziak
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-11

Part One: Quantitative Dimensions
Icarian Vantage Points: It's Hard to Be Down
When You Are Up (scale 30,000′ - city)
Since the Scientific Revolution of the 16th century, science and technology
have incrementally removed us from reality into the realm of the inherently
abstract form of the image and the word, the signified. Examples such as
the mapping of the human genome and the colliding of super particles in
Lucerne demand a complete trust in numbers and theoretical models over
direct observation. Trust in mathematical models and systems of indirect
observation have become all-consuming, crossing over into all disciplines
and becoming part of our collective culture. Antoine de Saint-Exupéry, in
his seminal work The Little Prince, comments on this phenomenon that he
portrays as "grown-ups" who align themselves with higher knowledge and
"serious pursuits," the world of words and numbers, over that of drawing
and imagination. He writes,
The grown-ups advised me to put away my drawings of boa
constrictors, outside or inside, and apply myself instead to geography,
history, arithmetic, and grammar. That is why I abandoned, at the age
of six, a magnificent career as an artist.1
The theorist and psychologist Rudolf Arnheim expands upon this
dependence on scientific exploration and a disregard for more creative
individualistic pursuits when he writes:
Western education has been concerned foremost with words and
numbers. In our schools, reading, writing and arithmetic are practiced

as skills that detach the child from sensory experience, and this
estrangement intensifies during the high school and college years as
the demands of words and numbers grow and childish things must be
put aside. Only in kindergarten and first grade is education based on
the cooperation of all the essential powers of the human mind;
thereafter this natural and sensible procedure is dismissed as an
obstacle to training in the proper kind of abstraction.2
In architecture, this transformation from subjective realities and direct
observation to a world whose meaning and legitimacy is derived from
quantitative dimensions and objective geometries was a long time in the
making. But it can now easily be argued that the designer of the 21st
century, assuming the role of Icarus perched some 30,000 feet (over 9000
meters) in the air, fully embraces this technological realm. Taken for
granted, the plan and the aerial image provide a puzzle that is first and
foremost a set of geometries, lines, and forms that detach themselves from
the scale and the speeds of human experience and provide an image largely
devoid of phenomenal meaning.
For the past century, society has been consumed by scientific pursuits
and by the acts of classifying and calculating. Ultimately, as technology has
evolved and we have collected "more and more information," we have
found ourselves in a world, as Jean Baudrillard commented, with "less and
less meaning."3 This has never been truer than in the digital age, where our
understanding of place and origins has moved further away from ancient
texts and worn photographs towards new software that promise landscapes
in their entirety and drawings that convey information over emotion.
Transformed into a place without qualities, Robert Musil's primary
character in his novel The Man Without Qualities, Ulrich, has been replaced
by the mechanical eye of astronauts Armstrong, Aldrin, and Collins. The

flickering transmissions that arrived in our living rooms at 4:17:42 p.m.
Eastern Daylight Time, July 20, 1969 were a final dramatic step in a
perceptual voyage to visualize our environment. Brunelleschi's linear
perspective ultimately has been transformed into an infinite holistic
perspective devoid of qualitative possibilities (Figure 10.1).
FIGURE 10.1 Hermetically Sealed Box Syndrome (HSBS):
Suburbia, How Did We Get Here? Tetra Images/Alamy Stock
Photo. ⏎
If we consider for a moment the perceptual transformation that occurred
in the representation of our natural environments, we witness one of the
primary reasons for this shift: advances in and increased trust in science and
technology. In The Vision Machine (1989), theorist Paul Virilio describes
the perceptual transformation in detail, writing:
It was partly thanks to the information provided by the Entrepreneur,
the first battlefield observation balloon, that General Jourdan won the

victory of Fleurus in 1794. In 1858 Nadar took his first pictures from a
balloon. During the American Civil War, the Union forces equipped
balloons with an aerial mapping telegraph. Soon the army was rigging
together the most varied combinations: camera kites, camera pigeons,
and camera balloons. The bombardier of the mid-twentieth century
caught quantitative glimpses, abstractions of the rhythmic pulsations in
the cities below.4
Our perception of the horizon-horizontal was metamorphosed with the
emergence of modern aviation (Figure 10.2). Virilio goes on to ask "Do we
construct the representation or represent the construction?"5 as a way of
acknowledging the intense amount of data that generate the images with
which we read context. His writing considers the speed and data-driven
nature of contemporary society which implies that, at best, society typically
relies on second-hand images of the reality in which we reside.

FIGURE 10.2 Norden Bombsight in the Nose of a B-29. Bill
Chizek Photography/Alamy Stock Photo. ⏎
The dialectic set-up between an all-observing eye, a quantitative glimpse,
and a reemergence of the view from 5 foot 6 (168 cm), the qualitative
phenomenological experience, is taken to its poetic extreme in Wim
Wenders's film Wings of Desire (1987), which follows the path of Damiel
and Cassiel, two angels who observe the world from above, providing
comfort to those in need (Figure 10.3).
FIGURE 10.3 Bruno Ganz, Wings of Desire, 1987: The View
from Above. Cinematic/Alamy Stock Photo. ⏎
The film opens with a shot looking down on Berlin, and a metaphorical
fall that takes us from this privileged point of view into a landscape of
trapeze artists and confused souls. Seen from above on the wings of
Heinrich Strack's angel in Berlin (or any metropolis for that matter) is, as

the theorist Michel de Certeau writes in The Practice of Everyday Life
(1988),
a theoretical (that is, visual) simulacrum, in short, a picture, whose
condition of possibility is an oblivion and a misunderstanding of
practices. The voyeur-god created by this fiction, who, like Schreber's
God, knows only cadavers, must disentangle himself from the murky
intertwining daily behaviors and make himself alien to them ...The
ordinary practitioners of the city live down below, below the thresholds
at which visibility begins. They walk - an elementary form of this
experience of the city; they are walkers, Wandersmanner, whose
bodies follow the thicks and thins of an urban "text" they write without
being able to read it.6
Certeau comments from a view atop the Twin Towers in Manhattan, "It is
hard to be down when you are up."7 Consistent with Certeau's critique of
the aerial view, Wenders searches for meaning in the streets below, and
generates his poetic text from the acts of the ordinary practitioner. He
provides us with this contrast as he moves the angels from their perch into
the urban realm: an auto dealership where Damiel and Cassiel discuss
events on a particular day throughout history; a moment when Cassiel
follows a disoriented elderly man against the backdrop of a prominent
Berlin Wall trying to find histories ... the lost Roman roads, and the
experience of simple pleasures. As the story progresses, we begin to place
increased value on instances that, while increasing our understanding of the
potential of the city, cannot be mapped. Certeau refers to these moments as,
"wandering lines ... indirect or errant trajectories obeying their own
logic."8

Probably no single work better represents the perceptual transformation
that occurred as the city street became but a pixel among millions than
Charles and Ray Eames's film Powers of 10. This 1977 short documentary
portrays a universe that is seamlessly interconnected and captures the
essence of the perceptual shift made possible by technological advances.
The film begins with a carefully constructed composition of a man
picnicking in a Chicago park. Captured in a state of sleep, the man in the
image looks away from the most recent scientific revelations. Our eye is
directed away from the copies of scientific journals and down his arm,
where we observe timepieces separated by a copy of J.G. Ballard's The
Voices of Time. The camera begins to pan out until we realize that the man
is picnicking in a park with a female companion. The narrator in the film
describes that,
The path continues upward to a view of 100 meters (102 m), then 1
kilometer (103 m), and so on, increasing the perspective that now
includes the picnic in the context of Soldier Field on Chicago's
lakefront. Ultimately, we journey to a distance of 1024 meters, or the
size of the observable universe, before we begin our voyage back to
where we began to the picnic. Then the journey continues to views of
negative powers of ten—10 centimeters (10−1 m) until we are viewing
a carbon nucleus inside the man's hand.9
A common tendency when viewing this visual masterpiece is to be
swallowed up by the technological achievement of the Eames as they
transition without interruption from one scale to the next. However, we
would argue that the true poetic motivation is defined by the intentional
placement of Ballard's text in the man's hand.

In referencing the Voices of Time, Ballard commented that it is the one
story by which he would wish to be remembered as it focuses on many of
the themes that concern him:
the sense of isolation within the infinite time and space of the universe,
the biological fantasies and the attempt to read the complex codes
represented by drained swimming pools and abandoned airfields, and
above all the determination to break out of a deepening psychological
entropy and make some kind of private peace with the unseen powers
of the universe.10
Ultimately, it is the scale of the everyday picnic that tells us the most about
the Eames's intent. Embedded within a scientific structure, the Eames's
grant us access to a seemingly anonymous picnic in a park that reveals
something about the human psyche and our fragile place within a greater
universe, a concept that science will never be able to fully dissect. The
daydream represents the realm of the qualitative within the quantitative
time signature of Ballard's framework. As such, the Eames's somewhat
hidden agenda is not one of vertical conquest, but exactly the opposite: an
attempt to obtain an honest reading of the planet on which they reside, and
to reveal qualities of the everyday, oftentimes hidden by scientific pursuit,
such as time - the ruin, temperature, and emotion.
Profane Landscapes: Domestic Space
(scale two 150′ - house)
In charting a collective perceptual history, the defining mechanism for
change has almost always been the introduction of new technologies. Often
funded and primarily used by the military, these technological advances

typically allow for greater efficiency and speed of seeing. Without regard to
cultural traditions so lamented by Ballard, society embraces such changes,
idealizing and appropriating them into the humanities and reinterpreting
them through artistic acumen. The most recent example of such a
technological appropriation can be seen with the proliferation of artificial
intelligence (AI) in the architectural academy in its infant stage.
While such examples are seemingly endless, no technological moment
affected the discipline of architecture and the transformation from a more
qualitative realm to a quantitative, where the symbol ultimately overtook
the physical reality, than that which occurred on July 17, 1902. With the
introduction of the first modern air-conditioning system, Willis Haviland
Carrier launched an industry that, it was claimed, would fundamentally
improve the way we live, work, and play.11 Or would it? Carrier's "package
unit" replaced/negated the window as the performative aperture for natural
ventilation, erasing any opportunity to appreciate thermal aesthetics and our
ability to perceive diurnal and climatic shifts. In so doing, it challenged the
relationship between interiority and exteriority, the metaphorical sacred and
profane. The constant 68 degrees Fahrenheit (20°Celsius) and 40 percent
relative humidity made irrelevant the ruthless simplicity of the dog trot
(roofed passage) and made thoughtful design decisions such as solar
orientation and cross-ventilation bygone concerns. Ads for the new
technology show this new machine seemingly being worshipped (Figure
10.4). As well, it becomes painfully obvious that, once the view to the
actual world had been blocked, something needed to fill the vacancy. So,
adding injury to insult, we became consumed by a new portal - the
television, and now the monitor (laptop/smartphone/virtual reality[VR]
headset). The retooling of our domestic realm and our workspaces
compromised our sensory perceptions; and, inundated by visual cues and a

pixelated spectrum of RGB, we eventually found ourselves in a state of
dystopic numbness.
FIGURE 10.4 Advertisement for Carrier and York Air
Conditioning: A Strange Kind of Worship. Personal Collection of
Author. ⏎

During a recent visit to our family's lake house, we were reminded of the
damage caused by this technology, whose unimaginable byproduct might be
titled hermetically sealed box syndrome or HSBS. Drifting on a pontoon
around the edge of the lake, our rest was disrupted by the various non-
designers in the company who would routinely point out nondescript glass
facades and comment, "That's a really pretty house." Clearly, the idea, the
signifier, had abolished any true connection to reality. The "window"
carried with it such a strongly signified image-caricature of a Pella six-over-
six frame that nothing else seemed possible. This symbol of home
ultimately translated to the siding, the Doric-framed entry, and the falsely
picturesque yard. As such, the houses are the same, randomly placed, and
maintain no connection to the sun or the prevailing winds. Similar to
Icarus's vantage that removed him from the qualitative, the ability to
mechanically regulate "air" in the hermetically sealed box superseded the
atmospheric possibilities so unknowingly cherished by one's subjective self.
One only needs to look 50 years earlier at a painting by American artist
Andrew Wyeth, Wind from the Sea (1947), to understand what has been lost
(Figure 10.5). Throughout his 60-year career, Wyeth painted lonely rural
landscapes, closely observed portraits, and crisp interior still lives in a
characteristically realistic and finely detailed style. Wind from the Sea is one
of the artist's earliest paintings of a window. The scene captures a moment
on a hot summer day when Wyeth opened the seldom-used window in an
attic room. The painting is eerily alive with movement as the wind blows
the curtains into the space. The tattered, transparent fabric is light and airy,
with small embroidered birds along the edges that seem ready to dart into
the house. In contrast, the sun-bleached wooden sill looks sturdy and solid.
The interior of the room is dim, while the landscape beyond is stark and
bright. The tree-lined view includes no figures, but, as in so many other

works by Wyeth, a strong sense of their presence is evident. Two well-worn
tire tracks running across the dirt lead the viewer's eye toward the sea in the
distance. The close vantage point and the tightly cropped frame at the edge
of the painting create the illusion that the viewer is, themselves, looking out
a window. Wyeth's masterpiece can be celebrated as an outlier, one that
combats perceptual numbness by privileging the coolness of the breeze, the
patina of centuries past, the sound of the attic floorboards, and the smell of
the country.
FIGURE 10.5 Andrew Wyeth, 1917-2009, Wind from the Sea,
1947, tempera on hardboard, overall: 47 × 70 cm (18½ × 279/16
in); framed: 66.4 × 89.5 × 7 cm (26⅛ × 35¼ × 2¾ in). Gift of
Charles H. Morgan. National Gallery of Art, Washington
2009.13.1. ⏎
Another valuable precedent that champions the qualitative, a warning of
what is being lost in contemporary design as a result of HSBS, can be found
in Hans Baumgartner's photograph titled Student Housing, taken in 1936

and referenced by the architect Peter Zumthor in his manuscript
Atmospheres. What makes the ideal café, or, as Zumthor terms it, the
"Magic of the Real"? Based on the visual argument put forth by this
photograph, it has little to do with quantitative dimensions, R-values, or
lumens. Rather, we strain to hear the whispered conversation between the
two main figures. The overscale windows let in an emotionally charged
light filtered by the cigarette smoke. The viewer is asked to hear the
clanking dishes in the kitchen and feel comfortable in the space. The
numbness and sterile reality created by the hermetically sealed box have no
place in such an image. Zumthor asks himself,
Can I achieve that as an architect - an atmosphere like that, its
intensity, its mood. And if so, how do I go about it? And then I think:
yes, you can. And I think: no you can't. And the reason I can is
because there are good things and things that are not so good in the
world.12
As such, one would argue, the designer's primary ambition resides simply
in the possibility of altering perception so as to be able to discern the "good
things."

Part Two: Reclaiming the Qualitative
Noplace/Everyplace: The Perception Chamber
(scale 5′6″ - mind)
Salvation calls.
In the late 1960s, three protagonists entered an anechoic chamber at the
University of California in Los Angeles. Artists Robert Irwin and James
Turrell, in collaboration with scientist Edward Wortz from the Garrett
Corporation, a leading West Coast defense industry, were participating in
the highly publicized Art and Technology Program of the Los Angeles
County Museum of Art (LACMA), whose simple aim was to encourage
mutually enlightening conversations that would, ideally, result in new
work.13 What resulted was a documented experience that would offer a cure
to an otherwise dystopian wasteland of predetermined realities.
The anechoic chamber was designed to eliminate the sensory stimuli of
light and sound reflections. The team would spend time in isolation,
initially for short periods and then building up to approximately six hours.
Their working hypothesis was that there would be a phase shift, or a change
in the quality of perceptual experience, produced by a combination of
sensory deprivation and sensory heightening. They described that, while in
the chamber, the research subject would become aware of sensations
usually beyond the range of normal attention, including the sound of blood
coursing through one's veins near the ears, the cracking of bones in one's
feet, breathing and occasional stomach gurgling, retinal and aural
hallucinations, and so on. Summing up the transformative impact of the
experiment, Irwin wrote:

I think what happens is we set up such a very strong kind of a
structural expectation and expectation-fit ratio that we tend to use as
much to block out information—and I think for good reason most of
the time: we block out that information which is not critical to our
activities. And after a while, you know, you do that repeatedly, day
after day, and the world begins to take on a kind of fairly uniform look
to it. So that what you're seeing is simply the fact that the sense
mechanism is extremely complicated, and we don't use it fully all the
time. We edit from it very severely, in time to only see what it is we
expect to see. So, it's not maybe a matter of seeing more or better; it's
a matter of simply seeing things you didn't previously see or placing
the emphasis somewhere else, the basic information you're taking in or
acknowledging is altered so that when you construct that into a mental
picture, make your picture of the world, it's simply not the same
picture. I mean, it's not made up of the same information, so therefore
it's not the same picture. It is an altered one.14
Additionally, this perceptual shift was not limited to the time spent in the
chamber, but had an impact on participants' experience in feeling their
environment after they came out, as Irwin goes on to describe:
I'd walk down the same street that I'd walked down coming in, and the
trees were still trees, and the street was still a street, and houses were
still houses, but the world did not look the same - it was very, very
noticeably altered.15
Irwin, Turrell, and Wortz were heavily influenced by the writings of
behavioral psychologist Arthur Deikman, and their experiences in the
anechoic chamber closely mapped to his studies on contemplative

meditation. Foregrounding his research, Deikman claimed that we
understand the world around us through a kind of short-hand simplification,
a perceptual behavior. He described this process as a psychological
mechanism that condenses the infinite number of images and data that we
are presented with on a daily basis into a few hierarchically significant
ones. Once simplified, our immediate environment requires only part of our
attention, the rest of which is filled with unrelated thoughts. Contemplative
meditation challenges this, requiring that the subject "relinquish his
customary mode of thinking and perceiving. Thoughts must be stopped,
sounds and peripheral sensations put out of one's mind, and the
contemplation of the meditative object be conducted in a nonanalytic,
nonintellectual manner."16 Deikman's research led him to formulate his
"Perceptual Expansion Hypothesis" and develop the concept of "de-
automization."
De-automization is a liberating process that leads to a third explanatory
hypothesis for the meditation phenomena: perceptual expansion - the
widening of perceptual intake to encompass "new" external stimuli, with a
new perceptual route strongly implied. Perceptual expansion is made
possible by de-automization of the selective gating and filtering processes
that normally are in constant operation.
There is a developmental concept implicit in such a hypothesis; namely,
that our earliest experience is probably one of being in more direct contact
with numerous, vivid, primitively organized stimuli. As we mature, a
learning process takes place in which stimuli and percepts are organized
towards a high level of differentiation based on formal characteristics. This
learning process not only takes place at the expense of the vividness and
variability of sensory stimuli, but also possibly involves a loss of perceptual
functions other than those to which we are accustomed.17

Similar to Deikman's interest in de-automization is the writing of Aldous
Huxley in The Doors of Perception (1954). However, unlike Deikman,
Huxley accelerates the perceptual transformation with the addition of an
external drug: peyote or mescaline. Huxley writes that, "Administered in
suitable doses, [peyote] changes the quality of consciousness more
profoundly and yet is less toxic than any other substance in the
pharmacologist's repertory."18
To be shaken out of the ruts of ordinary perception, to be shown for a
few timeless hours the outer and the inner world, not as they appear to
an animal obsessed with survival or to a human being obsessed with
words and notions, but as they are apprehended, directly and
unconditionally, by Mind at Large - this is an experience of
inestimable value to everyone and especially to the intellectual ... [We
must] intensify our ability to look at the world directly and not through
that half opaque medium of concepts, which distorts every given fact
into the all too familiar likeness of some generic label or explanatory
abstraction.19
Huxley's experiment leads him to speculate about what he terms a
"reducing valve," a mechanism that protects us from being overwhelmed
and confused by the mass of largely irrelevant and useless knowledge that
we encounter on a daily basis. He writes,
To make biological survival possible, the Mind at Large has to be
funneled through the reducing valve of the brain and nervous system.
What comes out at the other end is a measly trickle of the kind of
consciousness which will help us stay alive on the surface of this
particular planet. To formulate and express the contents of this reduced

awareness, man has invented and endlessly elaborated those symbol-
systems and implicit philosophies which we call languages. Every
individual is at once the beneficiary and the victim of the linguistic
tradition into which he has been born.20
Huxley goes on to suggest that "certain persons [such as artists], however,
seem to be born with a kind of by-pass that circumvents the reducing
valve."21
While I would argue that this "by-pass" is not inherent, but comes about
through a lifetime of practiced observation, this unique ability possessed by
the artist is an ability to comprehend and portray the world in a way that a
majority of people cannot. In the words of Matisse, "When I eat a tomato I
look at it the way anyone else would. But when I paint a tomato, then I see
it differently." To focus on the non-narrative components of a work and
become obsessed by the patterns and shifts in texture and color is a
wonderful possibility, one that holds the key to unlocking our reliance on
the signified - a predetermined existence registered in our mind from a life
of experience and expectations. Clearly, based on the research of such
artists and scholars as Irwin, Deikman, and Huxley, one can easily posit that
there is a profound benefit acquired by immersing oneself in a meditative
environment that alters one's "reducing valve."
Anechoic Chamber: CALANDO I
There is a striking contrast between the hermetically sealed box of the air-
conditioned house and the encapsulated space of the anechoic chamber.
HSBS suggests a denial of engagement with the exterior world, the removal
of sensory aesthetics, resulting in perceptual numbness. This is
symptomatic of a society so invested in data technology that we've taken

this notion of the reducing valve to an extreme. The anechoic chamber
similarly removes sensory stimuli, but without simultaneously filling our
consciousness with extraneous matter; it enables us to refocus our
perceptual energy and find a new appreciation of the qualitative.
To explore these assumptions, we introduced a design/build anechoic
chamber as a preliminary exercise in a recent joint architecture-interior
architecture studio. The research goal was to simulate the Irwin-Terrell-
Wortz experience (without Huxley's hallucinogens) and to assess the effects
of sensory isolation on our own sensory acuity, as well as on a sample
group of young designers. Performatively, our aim was to achieve total
silence (0 dB) and to separate the occupant as much as possible from their
physical environment. Working closely with physicists and sound engineers
from the University of Tennessee, Knoxville (UTK), the chamber went
through numerous design iterations and yielded a capsule-like space that
could hold one occupant at a time (Figures 10. 6 and 10.7).22 Each student
was responsible for logging a minimum of one continuous hour and
documenting their thoughts through structured reflection.

FIGURE 10.6 Anechoic Chamber Showing External Cladding,
CALANDO I: Atrium of UTK College of Architecture and
Design. Photograph by David Matthews. ⏎

FIGURE 10.7 Anechoic Chamber with Cladding Removed,
CALANDO I: Atrium of UTK College of Architecture and
Design. Photograph by David Matthews. ⏎

FIGURE 10.8 Entering the Anechoic Chamber, CALANDO I:
Atrium of UTK College of Architecture and Design. Photograph
by David Matthews.
Based on a series of these sessions, the construct proved to impact the
way in which participants perceived familiar spaces in unexpected ways.
Most participants reported that their sense of time, color, temperature, and
sound became much more acute. A few suggested that this effect lasted a
few hours afterwards, that their breathing became more paced, and that they
perceived greater layers of space. Such reflection draws upon the Irwinian
moment, upon exiting the anechoic chamber, when color itself emanates a

sort of energy and the "expectation-fit ratio" that we use on a daily basis is
shattered.
By embracing a current trajectory of total sensorial disruption and
isolation, a claim is staked on humanity's state of numbness, pushing it to
its extremes into one of profound feeling and experience. Through the fog
of data and automized thought processes, is it possible to see the world
from 5′-6″, to look again through Wyeth's window? If I may be so bold to
encourage - yes, "Yes, it is."
Personal Notes from the Chamber*
The door was sealed shut and, like the Apollo crew some half century
before me, I was isolated and alone but not nervous. Layers of black inside,
an almost euphoria of nothingness. The blackness is so intense that it
becomes an infinity. Also, the body that I was experiencing was quite strong
but completely perceptual as there was no physical object to be abstracted
through sight. I strained to locate familiar noises in the world I had just
departed. It was no use, and I moved on to more important things like
positioning myself in the woven hammock and preparing for the one-hour
mission. For a brief moment it occurred to me that I was back in the womb,
and an overwhelming emotion of innocence made me smile. It was then that
the machine went to work on my perceptual being. First, I felt the hairs on
the back of my neck for some strange reason, perhaps triggered by the smell
of the residue of the mounting material for the acoustic panels. And then
came my heartbeat; the blackness of this rhythm was accompanied by a
warm sensation, a redness. I became aware of the vessels and veins
extending out from my chest and into my arms. Finally, silence consumed
my being and I found myself alone, floating. I felt complete absentness but

was not consumed by the thought of such an existence as I, for a short
period of time, felt nothing and, as such, thought nothing.
Upon exiting the chamber, the light was overwhelming. I immediately
put my sunglasses on but that did little to help. And I was quite disoriented
by the cacophony of sounds emanating from the atrium, the woodshop, and
the outside world as I left the building within which the chamber was
housed. The immediate effect upon exiting was that space seemed to flatten
out; my surroundings seemed as if they were being permeated on a two-
dimensional panorama. I became hyper-aware of the space around me.
The slight breeze was incredibly refreshing and provided the perfect
counter-stimulus to the stillness of the chamber. Of course, I was on the
prowl for elements that would respond or stimulate my deprived senses, and
the chirping of birds was one of the first stimuli I welcomed. I tried at this
point not to squint so as to not diminish the contrast of the two very
different experiential realms. It was after about two minutes of adjusting to
this newly rediscovered reality that the most honest experiential notes
appeared. I went to the center of the courtyard and lay on my back in the
long winter grass, its bifurcated green stalks like soft blades. The thickness
of my body seemed to sink deep into the landscape and the coolness was
almost overwhelming on my backside. My eyes fixed on the cloudless blue
sky which was infinite in every way. While this would have been a pleasant
experience on any occasion, what seemed different to me at this moment
was the isolation of all the different items: the ground, the grass, the sky, the
sun, the breeze, shadows, temperature, sounds. I became hyper-aware of
each individual component and was able to appreciate the experience as an
ensemble rather than an overload of multiple senses. I speculate that such
overloads, more often than not, cause us to see less.

After staring at the sky and falling into a truly engaged meditative state, I
continued to enjoy the effects of my time in the chamber. Walking along the
street, I observed two students having an argument. One of the students was
wearing a hijab of the most brilliant oranges and pinks. I'm not certain if
the chamber was still altering my perception of the current experience, but
the tails of the head garment were flowing in the wind and establishing a
visual rhythm in slow motion that seemed to correspond to the
conversation. The other student was speaking in a dialect that was not
totally clear and at times almost seemed like a repeating mantra. It was only
as the second student turned to walk away that I noticed the shirt he was
wearing, a light blue, seemed to be reflecting the sky above. Perhaps the
best way to describe it was almost as an afterimage of the sky I had first
observed immediately after leaving the chamber.
*A common misconception is that, after spending an hour in an anechoic
chamber a switch will be flipped, and the world will forever be changed.
After logging approximately 12 hours, I have found that it takes practice to
gain the most from this unique environment and to best leverage one's
meditative state. After several sessions, one begins to better understand
one's breathing patterns and can more easily regulate external thoughts.
