OceanofPDF.com

11
NEWER BABYLONS
Matt Hall
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-12
Constant Nieuwenhuys' New Babylon proposed a utopian world in which
humankind was free to roam and create within a layered city of organic
networks, where every need and desire was solved through automation—an
outsourcing of all production unrelated to play. Like many visionary
architectural schemes, New Babylon was primarily a visual exploration:
models, collages and intense aesthetic studies—a grand and provocative
production of aesthetic possibility rather than premise. Utopian schemes
tend to skip the hard part, often blind to the problematic nature of the
production and struggle required to realize and sustain them. New
Babylon's promise, durability, critique and influence present a dichotomy
between aesthetic progress and actual progress, illuminating a tendency to
be seduced by surface over substance, where optics become a means to an
end.
The relationship between utopian progress and pragmatic production is
intrinsic yet distant. They are codependent, yet their outcomes seem
mutually exclusive. The benefits of progress are clear selling points, but the
warranty for the detriments is rarely honored—those side effects are often
outsourced, managed at a comfortable distance. This observation applies
even to the most quotidian forms of architectural production, where best-

case scenarios are rendered as optimistic stage sets, seldom devoting
attention to the rendering of potential negative outcomes. Every design has
side effects; how can we better predict them, or allocate our design talents
toward imagining them more precisely, as a kind of process of elimination?
New Babylon was both critique and proposal. It negated culture's
relationship with functional production to explore the production of culture
itself. The project's premise and potential influence on contemporary
practice present a provocation, not only questioning utopian aims but also
the very concept of "solutions" as we seek newer Babylons in a never-
ending pursuit of more: more comfort, convenience, security, meaning,
power, wealth—and, for the design professions, more novelty. This is a case
study grounded in skepticism toward any solution claiming absolute or final
status, prescribing a healthy dose of doubt for architects to apply to their
own work by recalibrating the balance between the conditions for design
and the conditions of their designs. To address a progressively problematic
world, the architect must walk the margins of their work—establishing a
self-critique that examines both the premise and the project in order to
illuminate a more comprehensive inventory of consequences. No design can
fully escape critique, and theoretical projects offer a unique venue for the
criticism of process rather than outcome. Instead of investigating Constant's
formal, spatial and aesthetic decisions, we might instead probe the
motivations behind them to more critically interrogate the project's premise
and imagine other outcomes. As Jorgensen and Wilson aptly state in their
essay "The Utopian Failure of Constant's New Babylon:" "New Babylon's
failure lies in Constant's failure to fully imagine the conditions by which it
might come into being, as if automation could be the single cause by which
utopia could be realized."1

This assessment could apply to myriad architectural projects and
theoretical projections that reduce complexity to simple concepts, to which
they maintain strict allegiance—a form of intellectual consistency in the
context of a rapidly shifting and inconsistent context. A compelling and
clear concept does not guarantee a thoughtful design, especially when the
concept is about form rather than performance, or when forms lack
comprehensive information. Conceptual motivations may seem inherently
neutral, even benevolent, until they are projected into the world, where they
may steadily diminish the potential for resistance, restraint, dialog and
refinement. There is also the question of who gets satisfied: the designer or
the recipient of the design, who is more often a victim of it rather than a
beneficiary. This is not to posit that Constant's work was a failure, or that
concepts have no place in design. On the contrary, the provocation here is to
subject our concepts to the worst possible consequences, and to use our
imagination to conjure potential problematic circumstances rather than
novel aesthetic scenarios. With this in mind, we can now interrogate New
Babylon for what it doesn't consider or achieve—and what its premise
might foreshadow about our contemporary condition, and the futures we are
building.
Egalitarian Free-Range Utopia
We are now gradually arriving at a stage in human culture where all
those useful functions of life, due to automation, industrialization and
mechanization, fade into the background and where every man is left
more or less empty handed as a utility animal, as a productive being.
Energy is released. A surplus energy, which will have to be used in a
different way. For the first time man is given the opportunity on a

massive scale to free himself from that compulsion to labor which has
turned the earth into a vale of tears.2
-Constant Nieuwenhuys
Constant Nieuwenhuys developed New Babylon from 1959 to 1974, a
sprawling project that shifted forms but maintained its primary premise: the
masses occupy a nomadic, non-transactional world unencumbered by any
concept or notion of work, responsibility or duty. The project stands in
opposition to "functionalism," which in Nieuwenhuys' context represented
an increasingly alienating capitalist "spectacle," a concept borrowed from
the Situationist International, of which he was at one time a member. While
the audience can be trusted to understand this concept, a simplified reading
of Guy Debord's thesis is that our world is mediated by images: images of
what we might become based on what we consume. As we surrender our
agency to an ever-exploiting force, we transition from being concerned with
who we are to what we have.3 We, in short, become what we own and exist
to own more, participating uncritically and almost involuntary in the
"spectacle." Modernism, functionalism and capitalism are all complicit, yet
the role of individual human agency seems diminished as a subject to an
overarching and exploitive system. As postmodern critiques go, it was a
substantial one that still holds some validity today, even if only as cultural
critique rather than revolutionary exploit.
It should not be forgotten that, once the functions are established, play
will succeed them.4
Constant's solution to the capitalist spectacle, built on the back of an
exploited working class, is to supplant it with all-providing automation. It is
apparently not the vulgar abundance or the spectacle of consumption that is

problematic, but rather the method of production. How this shift is achieved
isn't addressed in the premise or project, aside from a suggestion that it lies
"underground," forming the foundation of automated industry. The ground
becomes a network of circulation, replacing the street as the primary artery
of urban interaction. This diagram resolves all that is messy before
ascending into the free-range and free-plan creative roaming of the layers
above.
The post-revolutionary context is thus the starting point where
Nieuwenhuys can focus on the spatial and aesthetic forms the built
environment may take—a negation of architecture as praxis to instead
explore it as an aesthetic. He approached the project via the perspective of
art rather than architecture, so an investigation of potential over outcome
seems reasonable, if not self-admitted. Constant's 25-year project was not
without critique in its time. In the lecture "New Babylon 10 Years On," he
stated:
I seldom encountered any genuine sympathy for the ideas
underpinning this project and I was constantly having to defend myself
against accusations of utopianism or technocracy, depending on
whether the attack came from the right or the left. My models appeared
to sow confusion instead of fostering understanding for my efforts to
visualize a world that was fundamentally different from the world in
which we live or the worlds we know from history.5
Here, Constant demonstrates a keen awareness that his work invites critique
and accepts that it was based in visualizations rather than concrete
proposals. While it's clear he avoided tackling the transformation of the
production systems that sustain civilization, his premise was prophetic—
and it's proving to be quite durable. The scale of automated production,

along with the delivery and consumption of physical goods, suggests the
spectacle is alive and well. So too is the premise that it can all be
outsourced to economies and contexts less environmentally, economically
and ethically restrained. The developed world continues to prosper, both in
concentrations of wealth and in time available to play (aka consume). The
average working hours in the U.S. have steadily decreased since 2020, no
doubt due to the pandemic;6 yet, well before this event, statistics show a
steadily decline in working hours for over 150 years.7 In addition to
working less, the nature of our working lives is in constant flux, as is our
technological relationship to production and delivery. The gap between
unskilled labor and high-skill or knowledge work continues to widen, along
with disparities in wealth, health outcomes and opportunities to achieve
security, let alone success. The future is now, and it's clearly not for
everyone.
Perhaps the major difference between Constant's vision and our
contemporary context is that the burden of production remains directly tied
to the human condition. The blood, sweat and tears of the wage slave still
fuel the supply of cheap goods, as shopping for them continues define our
leisure time. Work is also transforming. Automation has been advancing
since the industrial revolution, yet we still need humans to turn screws—or,
in the future, humans to maintain robots that turn screws. In architecture,
building information modeling and new artificial intelligence (AI)-driven
tools are continually evolving how we visualize, communicate and deliver
projects. Technology has always transformed and advanced all aspects of
work, but never at this pace. Rarely do we ask what we sacrifice with each
new convenience or advancement, and neither, in general, does Constant.
Work, in this vision, seems to have no intrinsic value relative to the human
condition. It simply disappears or moves underground.

Unlike the premise of New Babylon, our society is not truly liberated
from work, but the privileged are now more capable than ever of
negotiating work on their own terms. Constant's vision of freedom and
creativity in a layered city can be currently contextualized as our networked
digital world, where nomadic exploration takes the form of clicking,
scrolling and swiping through a seemingly endless world of content to
satisfy any desire or interest. Creativity may be less about artistic
exploration and more about how to find the best deal, best date, best how-to
video, or how to become the wittiest or most offensive troll. Cynicism
aside, our level of connectivity and access to knowledge has unprecedented
consequences as our experiences are increasingly reduced to entertainment.
The Dictatorship of Experience
Is New Babylon a place where we are free to be creative or, conversely, a
context where we are forbidden to work? Is it the freedom to roam, or an
oppressive environment in which one can never stay put? Rem Koolhaas
observed that "there is a scale to Nieuwenhuys' work that is quite
unrealistic in the current circumstances. You might call his underlying
narrative an almost authoritarian dissemination of a kind of liberation
ideology."8 In this sense, one is forced to be free, a highly suspect premise.
Subjecting New Babylon to realism may be unfair, but what good is a
utopian provocation if it can't suspend doubt? While provocative visions of
the future need not be executed to test their potential, counter-fictions,
critiques and foils to such visions can offer productive insights into where
they may go wrong, and how they might actually be achieved.
Constant's New Babylon was an exploration of possibility based on the
premise that one can skip one existential struggle to introduce another—the
transition from all work to all play—and, even more radical, the elimination

of our traditional relationship with housing as we return to a hunter-gather
society, reconceptualized as creator-roamers. His provocation lies not in
how this shift is achieved, or even maintained, but what it might look like.
As Koolhaas observes, "It was above all the aesthetic of the work that
impressed me. It affected me much more than the narrative behind it."9 He
further elaborated, distancing the aesthetic from the ethic:
you can say that from the 1960's the utopian ideal and the architecture
grew apart completely. Of course, that is because of the domination of
market economics, the sworn enemy of utopian ideas. That widens the
gap between Constant and the present even further. It does not
however diminish the importance of the political dimension, though it
does make it less plausible. On the other hand though, you have the
aesthetic, many aspects of which are still plausible.10
This raises multiple questions. Is progress in architecture limited to only
aesthetic considerations, surrendering the design of substantial
improvement to crafting of the appearance of improvement? What is
architecture's relationship to its own production—is it simply a means to an
end? How do Koolhaas and Nieuwenhuys define the aesthetic? If New
Babylon is a utopia emerging from a post-revolutionary society, could the
revolution itself be part of the design premise, or become the design
process?
The profession rarely lacks visionary ideas; it more often lacks the
processes to achieve them, let alone reliable tools to assess their validity
and impact. This is not to suggest that idealistic design lacks utility.
Temporary distance from reality can illuminate theoretical modes of
thought not yet bound by constraints. With each step away from reality,
freedom to explore emerges, but at the risk (and reward) of venturing into

territory not on any relevant map, redefining the landscape through
unconstrained exploration, a creativity without consequence. Yet every plan
and rendering is, by nature, a projection into the unknown future—the
foreshadowing of a potential reality. It's fiction until, suddenly, it's not. In
this lies the inherent tragedy of architecture: it is always a prototype, only
tested in the field when it is too late to change course. New Babylon exists
in the safety of theory, as most ideas do, but once realized design is
positioned to create as many problems as it solves. This is a relatively
durable model: the designer seduced by their own ideas, blind to their
impact, convinced by the authority of expertise that they know what is best
for society, culture and the environment. Consequences can't always be
premeditated, but we may only know what's best if we understand what's
worst.
Architects continue to act is if they believe in utopia—in the form of the
best-case scenarios, even if the nature of their idealism is often limited to a
single project, theory or rendering. To abandon this may be to reject the
very notion of innovation, let alone hope—to surrender to being a manager
or estimator of impact. A de-aestheticizing of our approach risks reducing
design to a reductive numbers game of only that which can be measured.
For the banalities of practical architecture, progress may mean more
efficiency, durability, profit or ecological responsibility—metrics that can
be measured. How then do we establish and understand progress in
aesthetics, which cannot? Perhaps aesthetics is all the profession is good for
—after all, if architects don't consider such things who will? If such is the
case, the profession could use better arguments for them. These might begin
with defining what aesthetics are, how they relate to our work and the
manifold systems of estimating, measuring and experiencing them. While
there is feasibly no greater used and under-defined term in architectural

discourse than "experience," it often lacks a shared definition or discourse.
It is subjective, and thus simultaneously full of promise and ripe for failure.
Philosopher Vilém Flusser defines aesthetics as "capable of being
experienced," in opposition to habit, which he argues is not experienced but
simply enacted.11 To encounter the aesthetic is to break from habit. To play
is to break from work; to be surprised suggests a break from the familiar.
New Babylon proposes a perpetual break from habit—a permanent
transformation in which habit becomes aesthetics, outsourcing all that is not
worth experiencing. Yet the potential oppression and often violent
extraction related to producing and sustaining the aesthetic dream state
must also persist. Any aspect of the human condition tied to function or
necessity is conspicuously absent. What if work is rewarding or one finds
meaning in it? What happens when "roaming and being creative" ceases to
be an aesthetic experience and is reduced to habit? Perhaps Constant's
project ultimately serves to eliminate all labor, only to evolve humankind
into a new form of it. If so, what is the aesthetic of labor and how does it
temper other experiences, from the creative to the consumerist? What then
is the worth of our experiences and how do they relate to our identity,
agency and our ability to shape the world rather than being subject to
someone's design of it?
Robert Nozik's hypothetical "experience machine" presents a
provocative lens. The machine would allow individuals to feel as though
they are having any experience they desire, curated from a massive
database of millions of others' lives. Every two years, one disconnects
briefly to choose the next two years of experiences. In an estimation of the
consequences of such a machine, Nozik writes:
It will seem to some, trapped by a picture, that nothing about what we
are like can matter except as it gets reflected in our experiences. But

should it be surprising that what we are is important to us? Why should
we be concerned only with how our time is filled, but not with what
we are? ... plugging into an experience machine limits us to a man-
made reality, to a world no deeper or more important than that which
people can construct.12
Nozik's machine questions whether experiences come ready-made or
require effort to construct. Are they generated from the architect's spatial
and formal exploits? Perhaps they are more the product of knowledge
workers, algorithms and programs? What is the state of design when it
considers that they may also be individually and personally built from the
ground up? Utopian visions rely on the generalization and perfection of
future people, thus the potential destruction of their diversity, dissent and
individuality. Utopia ultimately demands a form of cultural collectivity, the
absence of which is paradoxically why such visions seem impossible to
realize. Constant clearly understood this, and this a priori is both the
enabler of his project and its potential horror. In "The Path to Unitary
Urbanism" he states:
All attempts this century to initiate cultural reforms have failed
because they took the individual as their starting point. The collective
imagination was not taken into account, was misunderstood or
despised. Nevertheless, the face of our world today is largely the result
of collective effort.13
As experience becomes increasingly mediated by technology, the
"established functions" Nieuwenhuys referred to are likely destined to be
outsourced within a gig economy where engagement with the extradomestic
world can be almost entirely avoided. This suggests a Newer Babylon where

space and form matter little, as the design aim is to atomize the individual
into the collective mist. Will the future truly be a collective "Unitary
Urbanism," evolving us towards neutrality in a collective stew of shared
perspectives and efforts, or into Our Babylon, where the tyranny of the
lowest common denominator demands the collective reality?
Our Babylon: The Collective City
Welcome to 2030. I own nothing, have no privacy, and life has never
been better.14
- Ida Auken
In 2016 Ida Auken, a member of the Danish Parliament, published a short
essay on the World Economic Forum website describing life in the year
2030. In her seemingly utopian projection, she outlines a collective city—
not her city, but "our" city. She owns nothing: no car, no house, no clothes
—admitting that this may "seem odd ... but it makes perfect sense for us in
this city." Products have become services. Inhabitants have access to all the
things they need in daily life, for free of course. She lays out an order of
events starting with digitized free communication, then free clean energy,
and a dramatic drop in the cost of transportation. The new transportation
will be driverless, perhaps even flying—yet somehow without traffic jams
or congestion.
The programmatic and spatial consequences of Auken's utopia are
fascinating. "In our city we don't pay any rent, because someone else is
using our free space whenever we do not need it. My living room is used
for business meetings when I am not there," she writes, suggesting a sort of
hot-room typology in which every domestic space is for hire—and every
human act must therefore be premeditated, scheduled, programmed and

coordinated. Even the idea of a kitchen is questioned: "Why keep a pasta-
maker and a crepe cooker crammed in our cupboards we can just order
them when we need them." Imagine the potential for app development ...
This transformation of products into services is assumed to usher in an
age of "durability, repairability and recyclability," as though any designed
object, now owned by a service organization instead of a private individual,
would automatically be fed by a magical, clean-energy, post-planned-
obsolescence ethic. There are multiple instances where "environmental
problems seem so far away," repeating how "clean" everything is. But if
history and our current context tell us anything, it's that "far away" does not
mean "nonexistent," and "clean" here must mean "filthy" somewhere else.
To be charitable, Auken's provocative vision is earnest, optimistic and
clearly aimed at suggesting a world—like Constant's—that is open to some
other form of humanity. Yet that humanity remains undefined and
reductively collective. She does not explicitly suggest a world without
work, but does hint at a changed relationship with it, stating it can be done
at "any time." The only real concern posited in Auken's writing is for those
who choose not to live in "our city." These are the Neo-luddites, described
as feeling obsolete, left behind to live in smaller "self-supplying
communities" or "abandoned houses in small 19th-century villages."
The most compelling vision of both the 2030 Collective City and New
Babylon is the counter-narrative—the potential landscapes of production,
revolt and resistance that serve as both foil and lifeblood to each utopia.
These forces are often overlooked in design, as our concern gravitates
toward results rather than the complexities of getting there. Much like the
fetish for urbanization, the city runs on the back of an increasingly
conglomerated agricultural industry. The machine that feeds utopia is surely
not a pleasant place. We need not think or look too hard to discover these

waste-scapes and badlands—the loading docks, water treatment plants and
sanitation departments of both New Babylon and the Collective City are
just as worthy of design and most definitely will have an aesthetic. Artificial
cleanliness is possible, but the garbage must go somewhere.
These scenarios are issued as proclamations, much like the architect's
typical "concept statement," which seems to guarantee only the things a
project will not address. In Auken's vision, all interactions seem reduced to
transactions; in Nieuwenhuys' all actions become pure creative hedonism.
Both seem to fall squarely within the discourse of "Unity Urbanism," as
articulated by the Situationist International. As declared in a manifesto-like
paper in Internationale Situationniste from 1959, "[Unitary Urbanism] is
not a doctrine of urbanism but a critique of urbanism." The exploitation of
the worker is, of course, derided, but the exploitation of established forms
of urbanism are ripe targets for subversion and exploitation. The theory, like
many, is binary: if the established is bad, then its détournement must be
good.
As a result, it is just as important that we exploit the existing decors—
through the affirmation of a playful urban space such as is revealed by
the derive—as it is that we construct completely unknown ones. This
interpenetration (employment of the present city and construction of
the future city) entails the deployment of architectural détournement.15
The future city—the revolutionary city—is always on the horizon, never
fully grasped. To attain it would be to achieve something worth keeping: to
progress. But the tragedy is that revolution, often necessary, can never
realize its aims without condemning its modus operandi. The city may itself
be revolutionary without any utopian or political overlay. It evolves and
expands as an organic entity with both grids of authority and undercurrents

of subversion. The city is the streets, the streets are the people, the people
are the politics, and the plurality is the spectacle. Its unknowns are built
into its flexibility and evolving nature. It needs no forced or assumed
collective identity to progress, and, even if that were possible, problems will
surely persist.
Constant positioned architecture not as an aim, or even a result, but as a
gameboard for play with a loose tolerance, fewer rules, and less order. His
work was not concerned with solving problems, but proposed what the city
could be post-problem solved. This may be the most honest expression of
architecture: it can do nothing, it can change nothing, but it can provide
critiques and open avenues for myriad somethings. It is dialectic between
influencing culture and culturally influenced—just one force in the broader
feedback loop. Before concluding her essay with the assertation that life in
"our city" is such a good life, Auken writes:
Once in a while I get annoyed about the fact that I have no real
privacy. No where I can go and not be registered. I know that,
somewhere, everything I do, think and dream of is recorded. I just
hope that nobody will use it against me.16
Both New Babylon and this vision of the 2030 city feel more like fodder for
Black Mirror scripts than possible futures. They're compelling until they're
occupied by the complex and uncertain nature of individual behavior.
Utopia is always for the "we," and it invariably requires the sacrifice of the
individual. Design must necessarily generalize, but in doing so it risks
marginalization and reduction. Auken's statement above may mistake
annoyance for fear—a refusal to contend with the potentially horrifying
consequences of a collectively defined vision of human interaction,
domestic life, ownership and agency. These may be outdated concerns if a

newer Babylon emerges as one of hyper-individualistic curated satisfaction
—where the collective merely fuels individual desire, rather than the other
way around.
New Babylon may free us from work so we can roam, but an alternative
consequence that negates collective identity is that we are now free to stay
put and consume our precisely curated individual worlds. This world does
not have renderings—it is a rendering. It requires no architect to visualize
or estimate its spatial and material reality, as such a reality may no longer
need to exist. It is a world reduced to an appliance—a portal to access a
digital Your Babylon, precisely calibrated to each person individually.
Your Babylon: The Individual City
The door opens for me according to my motion—it senses so I don't have
to. The door handle used to be the handshake of the building, but we don't
even fist bump anymore. Not for fear of a virus—one need not fear
touching when there is nothing tangible left. The world opens according to
my values, so give me convenience or give me death. Automatic faucet,
soap, air dry. Automatic driving, garage door, robot vacuum. Sense my
sense of heat and cool me. Anticipate my hunger and feed me. Surround my
attention with surfaces of screens so I can consume constantly. The senses
are outsourced to sensors, freeing me from the burden of feeling. The
paralysis of choice is no longer relevant if I need not choose at all. I only
need to accept my truth (or my program) and the requisite facts are
delivered to match a virtual me—calculated and constructed from the
unredacted archive of my digital path. There is no longer any present
without a digital footprint.
I think, therefore I appear to be. But nothing truly is, apart from my
precisely yet involuntarily constructed bubble. A bubble might suggest

something light, floating, fluctuating and transient, fragile. My bubble is
harder than any material, though it has none. My world is one of programs.
My recreation is a program, my work is a program and my dwelling is a
program—all designed to free me from feeling, choosing, struggling ...?
Reality is no longer tangible; my life no longer determined by happenstance
and choice, but haptics and calculations. My architecture is not a stage set
but a surface that gains meaning entirely through what is projected upon it
or emanates visually from it.
This is a story of humanity redefined in an alternative immaterial present.
A world without gesture, nuance, individuality or agency—yet still a
paradise of infinitely variable aesthetic experiences, where the only habit is
the interface. The world is tailored to me: my AI, my worldview layered
over "reality" via augmented perception. I have no need to search or
discover because the path is already laid out, following to the most precise
calculation of my every desire. Cooperation, communication, work,
struggle, purpose—all antiquated concepts. I'm free to roam my personal
virtual world or project it onto the worlds of others, warping theirs to fit my
perspective, desire and need. As they to do to me. Our disconnect allows
this. Our atrophy to harsh realities sustains it. Our apathy secures it.
As this brief fiction suggests, contrary to being creative, perhaps we will
just be lazy. Applying Nozik's experience machine in the context of New
Babylon as a digital plug-in, a dongle mediating between our brains and the
tangible world, we might imagine a revolution of convenience. No need for
designers, no need for understanding humanity or individuality. Only a
program, an algorithm, a dataset. Perhaps these are the inevitable
immaterial conditions of every future design.
Your Babylon is a counter-utopia—a speculative fiction that explores the
shadows to provide warnings, insight and opportunities as a counterbalance

to the optimism and excitement that fuels the design exploit. These shadows
fuel a voyage along the problematic margins of any premise, and thus the
edge condition becomes the project: one where the detrimental aspects of
the design become the concept. Here, the goal is not to achieve the solution,
but to articulate the predicament. This may require playing out the design
via an individual experience, something Constant and Auken did not
consider. They present their concept from the top down—a proclamation.
From the margins of doubt and the perspective of the potential occupants of
these projects we may yield diverse narratives that have utility in design
decision making.
Margin Walking
Auken and Nieuwenhuys give us no clear sense of the border between the
landscapes of roving creatives and the hidden systems of automated
industrial production. For Nieuwenhuys, the models were pure formal play
—collages of layered, networked cities, free from authoritarian grids or
systems of logic that might impede the dérive of its inhabitants.17 They end
like paintings in a gallery: bounded by a neutral white wall, isolating the
artistic endeavor from the corruption of reason and reality, outsourcing
measurable outcomes or consequences. These cities float, unmoored from
the climate, topography or ground—yet there is so much grey area in that
distance.
For the sake of argument, the utopias discussed here are presented in
binaries: consumption vs. production, play vs. work, distance vs. proximity,
utopia vs. reality, outsourcing vs. owning, collective vs. individual. But
reality lies in the grey area between these extremes. Auken expresses
"concern" for those who don't live in "our city," as if the choice were black
and white. Yet considerable territories exist between these binaries: retreat,

refusal and resistance. For designers, these forms of margin-walking hold
value. They suggest utility in non-participation. Architectural polemics and
projects often call for attack, or at least action, but retreat might offer clarity
to understand or better consider how to attack, reducing casualties and
unintended consequences. The boundaries between consumption and
production, intention and consequence, benefit and detriment are minefields
we cross at the peril of our clients and constituents.
Auken and Constant are presenting theory in the form of a concept—a
way to imagine action in the world. Neither Auken nor Constant advanced
their visions into models—or forms of action in the world. While their
designs may be unrealized, the discourse isn't. It's been published,
disseminated, discussed and continues to influence. Design need not
become reality to instigate progress; and, from another vantage point, these
visions aren't potential dystopias, prototypes—models for potential futures.
Every speculative future has winners and losers, which aligns with the state
of the world, drastically amplifying the distance between the best and the
worst. If we are interested in the image of progress, the architectural
profession can render those indefinitely. Real progress (if there is such a
thing), however, comes at a cost: the willingness to recalibrate our attention
and examine the arguments against our work, not just the arguments for it.
After hitting rock bottom, perhaps there's a chance to claw our way back
toward an architectural etic that facilitates more space for freedom, play and
creativity, while refusing to ignore the vast consequences that lie in the
shadows.

12
UNRESOLUTION
Fiction in the Space Between
Inquiry and Invention
Mark Alan Blumberg
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-13
One way we occupy the world is in the space between the obstacles we
come across and the progression beyond them. The development of the built
environment incorporates the conditions and struggles of a given society,
trajectories of thought that formalize into philosophical inquiries and
resolutions, and, for the purposes of this text, the resultant architectural
typologies that emerge from the combination of these two factors. This
chapter utilizes these instigations in an experiment with fiction as a tool to
explore the imagination of architectural typology.
The story inquires whether, in a societal circumstance of catastrophic
failure, humanity could reframe the notion of progress from something
inherently beneficial to an objective of basic survival. In the fiction, an
improbable typology - a facility developed for human euthanasia called the
"Terminal Terminal" - emerges as a mark of progress. It is the architecture
that accompanies a solution to a world that has come apart at the seams due
to a presupposed hyperbolic pandemic and the potential chain reaction of
globally scaled failures that follow. The Terminal Terminal evolves from a

proto-typology as a military-run facility into a cultural anchor that demands
as much presence in the urban fabric as the cathedrals, universities, and
temples of finance we know today. If it can be understood as an active
driver of progress, architectural typology becomes as much a driver of
society as philosophical, intellectual, political, cultural, and economic
forces. It serves as an example of how a fictional typology may allow for
inquiry into our uncertain future to better respond to and perhaps even
predict architecture's progression relative to relevant and contemporary
crises.
Throughout history, the circumstances of humanity have emerged via
disparate trajectories ushering in philosophical inquiry, and vice versa. To
name a few, religion as an ideological foundation and means of societal
control in the Middle Ages; education and the dissemination of knowledge
as moral responsibility and diffusion of power during the Enlightenment;
and class struggle and economic growth through the late Modern Era.
Margaret Meek Lange uses these as potential causes of societal and cultural
phenomena throughout time, and posits them as buttressing foundations in
attributing causality to historical development. This is termed "progress."1
Though it may go without saying, these significant human conditions are
accompanied by philosophical reflections, and these work together as
factors in the emergence of their respective architectural typologies.
Further, the architectures embedded in the built environment and their
various evolutions are concretized evidence of the societal progress
humanity has made in response to the multitude of circumstantial obstacles
it has faced.
For example, it may be argued that, during the Middle Ages, societal
progress was primarily controlled through religion. The great cathedrals and
mosques built during that period are architectural evidence of this societal

condition. Additionally, the Enlightenment period's morality and ethics are
other examples of societal circumstance. The spread of educational systems
and the resultant proliferation of schoolhouses, university campuses, public
libraries, and printing facilities throughout the Enlightenment are
architectural and urban evidence of an ethical progression toward pluralism
in society based on moral responsibility and a free and accessible system of
education - a diffusion of the authoritative rule of the clergy.
The Industrial Revolution and its subsequent Modern Era could be
considered as focused on economic growth. Steam engines, railroads,
factories, worker housing, and stock trading markets are all evidence in the
built environment of an economically driven concept of progress through
modernity. The amalgamation of the built architectures, urbanisms, and
infrastructures of these eras culminates in our present physically habitable
spaces that shelter humanity's progress.
Ideological development, moral responsibility, and economic growth are
past (and current) drivers for society that fuel progress. In the last 50 years
we have come to understand the Computer Age, or the Information Age, as
our circumstance and driver of society; and, arguably, we are currently at
the cusp of the age of artificial intelligence (AI) defining our current engine
of development - for better or worse. The hyper-connective quality of the
internet, paired with an increasingly globalized circumstance, has
compressed world geography into the spatiality of handheld devices
dependent on data centers - a supporting yet rarely experienced
architectural typology.
Each one of these circumstantial and philosophical impetuses provides a
foundation that defines historical development and contributes to the
formalization of the architectural typologies that organize, construct, and
express the tangible contexts of our built environments. Architectural

typology becomes the reduction of these complexities into conceptual
architectural components. These serve to organize the processes of society
into manageable and navigable volumes of space and function that are
ultimately transposed as formalized cultural tokens embedded in the built
environment. Architects use typology to fundamentally abstract
organizational and formal principles that occur after historical development
has become concretized as architecture. Therefore, typological formulation
plays a role in the causations of societal progress and culture.
Another causal role is that of catastrophe and struggle. Of the many
crises humanity faces, perhaps the most fresh is that of the COVID-19
pandemic. This event shifted humanity's circumstances, affecting our
communication, our connections with each other, and our relationship with
existing architectural typologies. Material between us increased, the space
between us expanded, and the organization of our lives was adjusted. Masks
placed a material barrier between us that changed our experience of direct,
face-to-face communication in visual, oral, and aural ways. A 6-foot (2-
meter) radius of space was, at the very least, recommended; this made space
a recognizable buffer, initiated a condition of isolation, and hindered direct
touch - arguably a necessity of humanity. Finally, quarantine mandates
spatially reorganized our relationship with the workplace and with our
homes. The isolating policies of the pandemic brought into question the
necessity of the commercially leasable office space typology and
reconfigured the housing typology as potentially a convenient hybrid of
work and domestic space.
For all we know, the COVID-19 pandemic was mild relative to the
potential virulence of future novel viruses run rampant. What would result
were a pandemic ten times as impactful to cripple the world? How far into
other fundamental societal structures would the chain reaction extend if

initiated by a pandemic far worse than our most recent experience? Would
catastrophe ensue - and could this catastrophe expand into the status of an
"era"? As a case study in utilizing fiction as a tool for understanding the
forces and consequences of new architectural typologies, the pandemic
serves as an accessible and available context to project alternative futures
that may open up space for inquiry and shape the way that we progress.
Fiction is a method of establishing context for exploring a specific
architectural typology as a progressive societal agent. It allows for
hypotheticals to be examined with imaginative invention and freedom of
inquiry. The story that follows posits an era of catastrophic failure as a
circumstance of humanity - a dystopian context. The intent is to use a
projected possible scenario and the resulting typology as a form of inquiry.
While every story is perhaps designed to raise questions, for the architect,
these may be used as predictors of consequences, or as a method to forecast
new circumstances, typologies, and thus novel philosophical inquiry.
Does an era defined by systemic disintegrations of societal support
structures reframe the notion of progress, and, if so, how? When
circumstances malignantly progress, such as in the case of a hyperbolic
pandemic, how might architecture/the built environment progress
benignantly? Can architecture progress even in the face of what might be
understood as regressive circumstances? At that point, an architecture that is
regressive by standards developed within progressive circumstances may be
seen as progressive within regressive circumstances. Is this a neutralization
of the concept of progress from innately beneficial to necessarily benign?
Or can the discipline of architecture create malignant progress to match the
regressive circumstances in which it finds itself?

Fiction
Terminal Terminals: A Future History of an
[Im]Probable Typology
We are at the end of an era at odds with itself. In the years and decades
leading up to the turn of the 22nd century, we endured bitter obstacles that
yielded sour solutions, offering sweet relief in portions too large to swallow.
Our first pandemic - COVID-19 from March 2020 to May 2023 -
conditioned us into what we called the "new normal", a normal that we
could live within, and even thrive. This new normal wasn't that much
different from the old normal relative to what we know now. The new
normal inflated our sense of autonomy as we were able to isolate in our
cocoons of home, first through the mandated quarantines, then through
what we thought was personal choice. We rejoiced in the freedom to work
from home. By isolating ourselves from the annoyances of daily commutes,
loud coffee shop chatter, slow service, and difficult coworkers, we became
conditioned to love the space between us and our neighbors - rather than
loving our neighbors themselves. Those of us who were able to take full
advantage of this part of the new normal became accustomed to the
autonomy it allowed, and we fought to keep it going.
At the scale of the individual or family, this space between each other
was like a warm blanket separating us from the emptiness of a winter chill.
But the first pandemic also sparked isolationist ideologies at much larger
scales. A seed was planted at the scale of nations by supply chain failures
and governmental regime changes as a globalized network of trade that had
worked relatively smoothly for nearly a century started to unravel in efforts
to contain COVID-19. This national isolation, though gradual and hard to
perceive at first, went largely unnoticed until the arrival of SARS-CoVid-

Theta-7X (Hypervariant) in December 2028, violently thrusting humanity
into the devastating second pandemic of 2029. We thought there would be
another century between pandemics, so we weren't prepared for what
happened next.
We still don't know where Theta-7X originated. All we know is that it
amplified the severity of the omicron variant from years before, had ten
times the bonding affinity, would lay dormant in any given host for up to
six months before becoming active, and, once activated, its virulence was
unmatched - and it killed painfully. In the dormant months, Theta-7X
would simply be a docile contagion ... undetectable, spreading from host to
host to host, and waiting to activate. Once activated, Theta-7X went straight
for the lungs, causing aggressive inflammation. Our alveoli would fill with
fluid, causing acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS) that would
deprive our organs of vital oxygen and hinder the expulsion of carbon
dioxide, labor our breathing to painful levels, and stiffen our lungs, taking
away their elasticity. Our lung tissue would rapidly start to break down,
causing tissue necrosis due to hyperinflammation requiring mechanical
ventilation almost immediately.
From here the virus hit all of us in different ways; no two cases were the
same. Theta-7X could send our immune systems into overdrive, triggering
an exaggerated immune response, flooding the body with inflammatory
chemicals that destroyed healthy tissue; or it would destroy immune cells
directly, crippling our natural defenses and allowing secondary infections to
take hold. The virus might have attacked our cardiovascular system,
damaging our endothelial cells lining blood vessels and causing widespread
clotting and strokes, and depriving our organs of blood and oxygen, leading
to rapid tissue damage. Or it might have targeted our nervous system,
crossing the blood-brain barrier, causing our brains to become inflamed,

leading to seizures, paralysis, rapid cognitive decline, or all the above, and
damaging nerve centers controlling vital functions like breathing, heart rate,
and digestion. Finally, the virus might have hit our gastrointestinal and our
renal systems, attacking the kidneys and liver, causing multi-organ failure
through a combination of direct viral damage and systemic inflammation. In
severe cases, the virus would damage blood vessels in the gut, leading to
internal bleeding.
The most terrifying aspect of Theta-7X was that it behaved almost like a
sentient parasite, calibrating its impact to drain as much life from its host as
possible, seemingly to prolong its own survival. Death did not come quickly
with Theta-7X; the virus clung to life within its host, feeding off the body
and causing immense suffering to sustain itself. The disease was a chronic
existence of painful breathing for months and months before it started
attacking our immune system, cardiovascular system, nervous system, or
other organs. We suffered. We hoped for a cure through stinging inhalations
and exhalations that felt like shattered glass in our chests. But no cure came
in the first year. Or the in the second year. Our vaccines were useless
against this virus for the first five years of the pandemic. Theta-7X and the
suffering it caused simply became a way of life. Death, as a concept,
became tangible. We saw it every day staring at us through the suffering
eyes of our loved ones. Death became the sweet relief in a portion too large
to swallow. But society knew what had to be done.
In March of 2032 U.S. Congress legislated the Provisional Euthanasia
Act of 2032 (PEA2032). It passed by a narrow majority in the House of
Representatives only after months of heated debate and violent protests
around the country. The vice-president dutifully, though hesitantly, cast the
tie-breaking vote in the Senate on March 23, 2032, making human
euthanasia based on measurable criteria for mass suffering legal. What was

unheard of two years prior became a reality once it became clear that a cure
for Theta-7X was a distant, if not impossible, hope. Medical resources for
palliative care dwindled to near non-existence, while psychological and
emotional relief for families took priority. The indignity of suffering
through a Theta-7X death became unbearable - both to witness and to
endure. Meanwhile, arguments for personal autonomy and the right to die
grew increasingly compelling. Above all, the imperative to reduce
unnecessary suffering became paramount. In such a period of intense
regression of life at a global scale, it became dichotomous that such a
morbid potential as human euthanasia came to be known as progress in the
fight against the suffering that Theta-7X caused.
By June of 2032 our hospitals were more than overrun with annex
structures assembled by military operations, facilitated by the Federal
Emergency Management Agency (FEMA) and operated by the Centers for
Disease Control (CDC). Any open space around any given hospital was
occupied by all sorts of temporary structures. Rapid-deploy tents, inflatable
structures, frame-supported soft-wall structures, containerized units,
shipping container conversions, modular and mobile medical tents and
units, expandable medical trailers, decontamination tents, and isolation
units filled parking lots, parks, streets, and sidewalks around every hospital
in the nation.
Within these fields of annexed operations, the first legal instances of
human euthanasia would take place on June 17, 2032, in the twilight hours
just as the bright white illumination of LED floodlights powered up with a
percussive thump of electricity in an otherwise quiet evening. With only
immediate family in attendance, they watched their loved ones through the
fuzzy translucence of frame-supported polyvinyl chloride (PVC) strips.
Dressed in full hazmat gear, they stood silently as other hazmat-clad figures

administered a painless cocktail of euthanasia drugs. Under the flickering
glow of old fluorescent lights, on balmy summer nights in hospital parking
lots across the nation, the right to die was quietly ushered into society.
The military, the CDC, and FEMA were the three entities in charge of
building and operating the initial Departure Processing Facilities (DPFs)
within the contexts of the annexed operations that surrounded our hospitals.
Little credence was offered to ritual, procession, empathy, or compassion in
these early models of the Terminal Terminals. These proto-typologies took
on a purely utilitarian approach in the interests of efficiency, concerns for
clinical sterility, and separation from those still in healthy society. The DPF
structures and operations required direct access to a loading area for
discreet transfer of the euthanized remains from the facility to vehicular
transport. For this reason, the DPFs were typically located with direct
access to a road on the perimeter of the annex shantytowns that occupied
hospital parking lots and adjacent road infrastructure. Dependent on
specific site conditions, the DPF modules were either incorporated within
the boundaries of the original hospital annex operations or they were
affixed to the annex outside of its original boundary as if it was a parasite
clinging to its host. In either case, a metal-arched deployable tunnel clad in
thick, bright white vinyl and lit on the inside with strips of cool LED lamps
reached from the controlled entry of the DPF to the center of the annexed
area. In some cases, an annex was large enough to warrant multiple centers
to which the DPF entry tunnels extended.
For those patients who were conscious on their way to their departure,
these long, illuminated vaults of white PVC became their final spatial
experience of threshold. Architecturally, this experience is broken down
into three distinct parts: entry to the tunnel, the tunnel itself, and the tunnel
exit/arrival in the DPF proper. These three threshold experiences define

what eventually became known as the entering - the architectural entry
component of the overall Terminal Terminal typology that we know today.
Phenomenologically, this three-part entering sequence first disconnects us
from the world in which we live - by changing the light in our eyes, the
sounds in our ears, and the air on our skin - before ultimately connecting us
to the world in which we die. In the early, deployed structure models of
Terminal Terminals, the mouth of the entry tunnel facilitated this initial
disconnection through a vestibuled space defined by two simple, sequential
planes of transparent PVC strips through which the gurneys were pushed
and the facilitators and family members followed.
Secondly, the next threshold - the tunnel itself - began its formalization
as the deployed corridor, positively pressurized with filtered air and lit so
sufficiently that no shadow would fall on any surface interior of the tunnel.
The consistency of the bright white light inside the tunnel replaced the
variegated and gradated light of the living world that shifted and adjusted
based on time of day, shadows cast by clouds, trees, a plane moving
overhead, or whether the sky was overcast or clear. The filtered air being
pumped into the PVC tunnels created a slight breeze pushing against the
patients' faces as if its intent was to blow the debris of life from their skin.
And, finally, the thick vinyl walls of the tunnel muffled the exterior sounds
and reverberated the interior footsteps into the patients' ears. Light, air, and
sound became the first reacclimating conditions that marked a departure
from the living world for the patients.
***
The architectural conditions described above were not the intended results
of designing the solution for how patients enter departure processing.
Rather, the solution of entry into the Departure Processing Facilities was

based wholly on the necessity for a centralized location among the mess of
lightweight, prefabricated, modular medical facilities hastily built to solve
previous problems of dwindling space available for Theta-7X patients, and
a conditioned and closed pathway to the DPFs themselves. The
phenomenological biproducts of urgent and hastily constructed solutions -
the two thresholds described above - have survived architecturally
typological evolution over 80 years to define what we know as the
"enterings" of our Terminal Terminals at the turn of the 22nd century.
However, the last component of the entering experience - the threshold
connecting the tunnel to the DPF - was not formalized until well after these
initial deployable models of the Terminal Terminal had evolved well
beyond their initiating forms.
While Theta-7X was the initial generator of the need for the Terminal
Terminal, it would prove not to be the lone sustaining progenitor for the
further development of the typology. The challenges presented by Theta-7X
on a global scale worked only to exacerbate the previous obstacles of
positive societal progress. While the world was busy simply trying to keep
Theta-7X at bay and save as many lives as possible, the many results of
ignored climate change, massive decreases in arable land, consolidation of
resource accessibility, mass urbanization, supply chain breakdowns,
exhaustion of natural resources, global economic catastrophes, isolationist
and nationalist ideologies, sea level rise, the effects of various failures to
initiate sufficient renewable energy infrastructure, drought, dwindling water
supply, hegemonic industrial agriculture systems, mass automation in the
labor force, and insufficient mass access to health services and medical
support, among many other post-modern global shifts, effectively strained -
and ultimately collapsed - society's capacity for supporting its population
centers. The globalized world found itself under-resourced and incapable of

supporting its human population. Though we knew how to extract, process,
and develop basic resources such as food, water, and shelter, we no longer
had the infrastructural capacity or connective networks to sufficiently and
effectively provide these necessities at the speed and efficiency we had
become accustomed to prior to Theta-7X and its global effects. We find
ourselves here in the year 2100, 81 years past COVID-19's trigger of the
"Great Isolation" during which the global populations started to consolidate
into enclaves of unsupported, and de-resourced, humanity. This entry into
the 22nd century also marks almost 70 years of the Provisional Euthanasia
Act of 2032 (PEA2032), the conspicuously humane advent of freedom-of-
lifespan for those who chose to engage its options of escaping the realities
of a world devoid of support.
As the conditions described above became more and more tangible in our
day-to-day lives (if you could call what we were living through "life"), the
hospital parking lot annex proto-typology of the Terminal Terminal was
given the chance to develop further into a more permanent architectural
reality three years further into the pandemic when the Euthanasia Act of
2035 (EA2035) was legislated, making freedom-of-lifespan available to the
broader population who were suffering from the ancillary global effects of
the pandemic. Our world had simply become unbearable to live in for those
of us for whom it may be more difficult to move around; to take the extra,
often near impossible, steps of finding the resources required for simple
survival. For many of us, our world had become something to escape as
opposed to something within which to simply be - to live. The Terminal
Terminal at this point broke free of its hospital annex sites and entered its
tenure as a municipal utility to be run by local authorities. EA2035 thrust
the Terminal Terminal into the hands of the purveyors of the built
environment - architects, landscape architects, urban designers, planners.

Cities all over the U.S. began planning for the addition of a new type of
civic structure within their building fabric. As a municipal service, sites
were centralized within city boundaries for equal access to all who wanted
to take advantage of their newly granted freedom-of-lifespan. Central
locations within cities were earmarked as potential sites for Terminal
Terminals. Larger cities such as New York, Los Angeles, Chicago, Atlanta,
etc. chose several points of centrality within their dense or sprawling
fabrics. As soon as the potentiality of the specific site locations became
publicly known, the other side of the human euthanasia issue became
visible and tangible. The concept of human euthanasia in general was, of
course, not accepted by all citizens in the first place. Further, the potential
of expanding the applicability of euthanasic practices outside of the original
intentions as relief from disease into relief from the general regression of
society and the decline of the survivability of everyday life pulled even
more pronounced protest. Protests became dense and rampant around the
sites that were chosen for the Terminal Terminals. Twenty-four hours a day
protestors picketed and chanted mantras against the development of the
Terminal Terminals, claiming euthanasia as immoral, sinful, unethical, and
against the natural order of things. It became clear that the protests were not
going away.
Each site became surrounded with dense crowds of protestors throughout
their design and construction periods. To facilitate the material
manifestation of freedom-of-lifespan facilities while simultaneously
facilitating freedom-of-speech for the protestors, architects designed zones
of protest into the site plan for each Terminal Terminal. These formalized
almost as moats filled with protestors, disconnecting the city fabric from the
"sites of passing" - the sites proper for the new DPFs. Terminal Terminals
around the country started to incorporate a protected "outer ring" intended

specifically for the protestors to voice their displeasure at the idea of human
euthanasia. This zone, this outer ring, soon became known as "the outering"
section of the procession from the living city to the sites of passing. From
the city, the euths - those of us who had decided to act on our freedom-of-
lifespan - would first encounter the outering, enduring the chants and
displeasure of the protestors as we passed through them in a narrow
protected corridor before reaching the mouth of the entering - the light-
filled tunnel that has survived from the initial proto-typology of the hospital
annex DPF.
So here we have identified two of the typological components for the
Terminal Terminals. In order of procession from the city, we first encounter
the outering - the space intended for protest against the very thing that
humanity deemed as a progressive step forward among unimaginable
suffering caused by society's failures in progressing past previous obstacles.
The second typological component we encounter is the entering - the light-
filled corridor left over from hospital parking lot annexes that
phenomenologically disconnects the euths from the experience of the city
and its life-filled stimuli and holds them in a space of blank transition;
washing them with light, air, and muffled sounds. The third typological
component - the passaging - remains inaccessible to anyone not intending
to practice their right-to-lifespan, and therefore is beyond the scope of this
exploration. The passaging - death's architectural formalization in Terminal
Terminals - was always intended to parallel the mystery associated with
departing the living world. It is unknown what happens beyond the far end
of the entering, and it is outside the boundaries of this unresolved history of
the Terminal Terminal.

Unresolution
Part of the allure of the future is its unresolved status - its characteristic of
being unknown. This potential of unresolution allows imaginative invention
to emerge from the inquiry of both our history and our potential futures.
Architects operate between givens and possibilities; much like humanity's
situation between obstacles and progress. Their role often requires diverse
exploration in the space between universally understood forces and an
uncertain future. Fiction is a tool for activating the space between the past
and the future, or obstacles and progress. It serves as a method for inquiry
and invention. Design itself can be said to be the practice of establishing a
set of inquiries based on relevant circumstances, and ultimately inventing
myriad possible solutions which result in an optimal final condition.
Perhaps this suggests that embedded within the processes of architectural
design are trajectories of fictional exploration that unfold as inquiries that
establish foundational frameworks for novel architectural typologies.
How this tool is used is based on our values. While our profession often
lacks foresight, it may be because our designs are imagined as (and limited
by) an aim for realization. For the author of fiction, realization, clarity, and
a precise inventory of cause and effect are not their responsibility. The
reader is left to interpret the meaning and decide how it may or may not
shape their worldview, and thus their method of progression. The fiction
presented here is prefaced by intent, yet not concluded with consequences.
If it is indeed a tool for inquiry, it may be the reader who initiates the
questions, much like an occupant or culture must contend with a work of
architecture in reality. What the next typology is remains unknown, but,
rather than surrender to the reactionary, architects act through the projection
of possibilities unlimited by clear goals and criteria. Through such an act
they may develop new processes for understanding obstacles to progress

before they occur, and, when they do, have a more facile method at their
disposal for playing the possibilities as open fictions to chart more diverse
courses for reaction.
