1. Meek Lange, Margaret, "Progress", The Stanford Encyclopedia of
Philosophy (Summer 2023 Edition), Edward N. Zalta and Uri
Nodelman (eds.),
https://plato.stanford.edu/archives/sum2023/entries/progress/. ⏎
OceanofPDF.com

13
ON ENEMY TERRITORY
Gregory Spaw and Patrick Rhodes
DOI: 10.4324/9781003479932-14
The word "manifesto, from the Latin manifestus and the Italian manifestare,
means to make obvious or to make public. The root of the last bit of the
Italian origin, stare, means to be rigid. So, when an artist, architect, or
anyone else puts forth a manifesto, they are presenting a set of ideas to the
world that is fixed. More than that, the manifesto bemoans the current state
of things and promises a tomorrow that never comes. They are generally
pretentious, lofty, arrogant, and make statements rather than asking
questions, while aiming high and almost certainly falling short. They tend
to be bursting with answers bent on solving problems when the thing that
drives us is problem posing. For all of their rigidity, they are static, soon
become stale, and eventually dead. Worst of all, they take time to write
when you could be out doing the actual work. I think if you asked us, we
would say that we don't particularly like manifestoes. But this is a
manifesto - or is it? It may more accurately be called an anti-manifesto, less
about grumbling over the obstacles in life than accepting them, keeping
them close, and celebrating the irritation they bring and the agitation in
them that keeps us awake. It is probably closer to a manifest rather than a
manifesto - the things we need to keep going. In the age of discovery, a
ship's manifest was more than a mere inventory of supplies and equipment;

it was an instrument of measure of the time and the distance remaining until
you die. For us, the following list is like that. Like the ship where you will
find that manifest, it is always moving, in constant flux, flexible and
reflexive, able to take advantage of the changing winds. It marks and
measures the fine line between success and failure, between the vitality and
the stagnation of the work, acknowledges and takes stock of the obstacles
ahead, and resolves to face them head on. It motivates us to get up, to get
going, and to do the work. While it may read as a sharp critique of the
academy and the profession, it is more of a critical self-reflection - an
accounting of our own failures and lessons learned, a memento mori, and a
reminder of the most crucial things we need, and need to avoid, in order to
progress.
Incompetence
* Architecture may be incapable, ill-equipped, or ill-suited to address
society's core concerns, from poverty to climate change to social
justice.
One of the most pervasive and persistent fears among people who make
things is that they may not know what they're doing. However, for those
with a certain level of experience and expertise who know how to make,
they may be ignorant or incapable of knowing what to make exactly or,
more importantly, why to make it. Aggravating the condition for those of us
on the ground is our inability to rely on the voices of a profession that have
been historically skeptical of, oblivious to, misguided about, or unwilling to
adequately engage with the fundamental social, economic, and
environmental crises we increasingly face, either ethically or practically.
Since at least the 1920s, the masters of our profession have espoused with a

kind of Dunning-Kruger-like certainty that architecture's most important,
yet enigmatic, contribution to the happiness, harmony, and progress of
society is through aesthetics and form.
In a social media post in 2014, Patrik Schumacher upheld these
convictions by stating that "FORM is our specific contribution to the
evolution of world society," while deriding critics for moralizing,
paralyzing, and arresting his "explorations" when we are unable to
"instantly demonstrate a manifest tangible benefit for the poor - as if the
delivery of social justice is the architect's competency."1 In agreement,
either wittingly or unwittingly, Edwin Chan is currently promoting the
ability to enrich people's lives, develop strong communities, and generate
"moving spiritual experiences" through what he calls "social sculpture" -
otherwise known as society's "silver lining."2 Chan's allusion to clouds is
fitting though, as Kenneth Frampton pointed out in a 2016 interview on
what he considered to be "spectacular starchitecture"- that the success of
architects "depends on their not being grounded."3
Following a global pandemic, years of societal upheaval, and a shift in
the global political discourse toward the right, Frampton has reversed
himself and thrown in the towel on architecture while conceding that its
current predicament is due to its continuing failure to realize a social and
ecological vision and its inability to cultivate local culture through the
branding of star architects. He laments that if we do not soon turn away
from our rampant consumerism and toward organizing for the social good,
"we have no hope surviving as a species let alone as a profession."4
Meanwhile, a concerning view among many everyday architects is that, if
we walk away from profitable, socially acceptable, and politically expedient
work, there won't be much left to design.5 From a profession that accounts
for 40 percent of the world's carbon emissions, is dominated by white males

(over 70 percent), and traditionally serves the wealthiest 2 percent of
society, these viewpoints evidence architecture's ongoing cognitive
dissonance - that our problems and the power to change them come from
within, and that we have no shortage of work left to do.
Complacency
* Despite our incompetence, we are compelled to make.
FIGURE 13.1 Neonomads taking in the expanse of the Rub al
Khali. Photograph by the Authors.
In 2014, archaeologists determined that paintings found in a remote
Indonesian cave were nearly 40,000 years old and the earliest form of
figurative art ever found. While scientists still debate the intentions of the
stenciled paintings made by spitting moistened pigment over human hands,
they suggest that these paintings and others like them found all over the
world were a necessary part of the cultural fabric for ancient people and

served as a kind of "social glue."6 Made tens of thousands of years before
the development of written language in an isolated part of the world when
humans were confronted daily with their own mortality in the face of the
relentless passage of time, these paintings seem to be reaching across the
millennia to simply say "We are here; we exist." Since our beginnings,
humans have been compelled to make as a part of our very nature -
something that Hannah Arendt referred to as Homo faber ("man the
maker"),7 and coming from a place that the ancient Stoics called the
commanding part of the soul.8
Across the history of human material culture, many of our artifacts have
had no apparent utilitarian value or practical use, and have been made in an
effort to express, experience, explore, and seek out a greater or higher
understanding beyond ourselves and our world. Thomas Aquinas defined
this as a "natural" process motivated by both intellect and a desire to gain
self-knowledge from external things through which we draw intelligible
concepts to better reflect and see ourselves. He suggested that we "proceed
from objects to acts, from acts to faculties, and from faculties to essence."
By describing our motivations as natural, Aquinas meant that our actions
are determined by our constitution as human and are not optional, driven
toward good and our general welfare.9

FIGURE 13.2 Neonomads MIG welding on the frame of the
prototype three shelter. Photograph by the Authors.
Although this philosophy has underpinned science and education from
the empiricists through to modern times, many of our institutions, including

academia, have become corrupted by complacency, the avoidance of
progress, and self-satisfaction with the status quo. Philosopher and
cognitive scientist Daniel Dennett compares academic scholarship to
"Chmess" - a hypothetical, intertextual, and self-referential game where,
rather than being derived from and applied to life, knowledge is applied to
other knowledge, and conclusions drawn from others' conclusions rather
than empirical experience.10 For a growing segment of modern society,
higher education has become an evil and what Aquinas may have referred
to as an object of avoidance. In a lecture given at Harvard in 1837, called
"The American Scholar," Ralph Waldo Emerson eviscerated the attending
students and faculty for living in a "degenerate state" detached from, aloof,
and irrelevant to the concerns of society as mere thinkers or, worse, parrots
of other thinkers, disconnected from doing and from life experience.
Emerson imagined a new kind of thinking that, rather than lazy theoretical
discourse, comes with a duty to raise and guide each other bound by facts
that are active and present in the living world. This new thinking he
envisioned takes the form of work through which we learn and discover
new things through experience, often confronted by our own ignorance,
while our new language takes the form of questions rather than answers.11
"We will feel the salt of the sea not by reading about it in a book, but by
plunging ourselves into the ocean."12
The words obstacle, manifesto, and complacency are curiously related -
meaning, respectively, to stand against, to make your position known to
others, and to be pleased with the position you hold. The danger of standing
in one place for too long, however, is that you might remain there, never to
move again - an obstacle to yourself. Inspired by Emerson, Thoreau,
Dewey, and so many others compelled to learn by doing despite that, as
Aquinas admits, we can never completely know the essence of anything; we

will move, we will plunge ourselves into the ocean and continue to express,
to experience, to explore, to seek out, and to learn by making.
Terrain
* We occupy the edge, the margin, and the periphery while taking full
advantage of the freedoms we find there.
There is a scene in David Lean's 1962 epic masterpiece, Lawrence of
Arabia, in which Lawrence and an Arab caravan have crossed an expanse of
desert so deadly that the locals refer to it as the anvil of the Sun, only to
find that one of their party has fallen behind during the night. Despite
protests from the Bedouin that their lost friend's fate is sealed and any
attempt at rescue would keep them from their eventual goal, a defiant
Lawrence turns back into that landscape to face almost certain death, while
roaring, "I shall be at Aqaba. That is written!"
The idea that humanity holds possession and stewardship of the Earth, its
terrain, oceans, and all of its natural resources by divinely ordained right is
an ancient one and, while wildly arrogant, still persists. Once isolated by
natural barriers like a mountain range, a desert, or a body of water, people
have cut passes, dug canals, scorched earth, and busted sod to expand,
annex, conquer, and bring nature to its knees to stake and claim as their
own, by providence. This human condition has been made no more evident
than when, in the nineteenth century, it became enshrined in the budding
American national identity as Manifest Destiny. But, what pulls us out into
the terrain is a deeper, more primal, and perhaps more innately human
longing just to see. More than a simple wanderlust and, although not
uniquely American, there is something about the freedom of being
American that is formed by the ease of movement, the open road, and the

urge once in a while to light out for the territories. From out of Africa to the
Vikings, the Inuit, Bedouin, the Maasai, and the Sami to modern migrants
and homesteaders, drifters, and perpetual travelers, we have always been on
the move. We inhabit every speck of land, no matter how small, lethal, or
remote - from arctic Utqiagvik, Alaska to the tiny South Atlantic Island of
Tristan da Cunha. We swim oceans, cross deserts on foot, and scale
mountain peaks for no apparent practical purpose. We test our bodies and
minds in the most extreme environments on the planet for no other reason
but to feel what it is like to have done it. We often don't make it, and we go
despite knowing that. Early in the spring of 1845, a 27-year-old Henry
David Thoreau quit a good income making pencils for the family business
and went to the woods. He went to confront the hardships he found there
and to live deliberately, to learn what it had to teach, and not, when he came
to die, discover that he had not lived.13
FIGURE 13.3 Neonomads hiking to the prototype four shelter
site with building components. Photograph by the Authors.

The sea's only gifts are harsh blows and, occasionally, the chance to feel
strong. Now, I don't know much about the sea, but I do know that that's the
way it is where I am. And I also know how important it is in life not
necessarily to be strong but to feel strong, to measure yourself at least once,
to find yourself at least once in the most ancient of human conditions,
facing blind, deaf stone alone, with nothing to help you but your own hands
and your own head.14
When we first dreamed up Neonomads and were faced with the prospect
of deploying a shelter into our own Sun's anvil, the Empty Quarter of the
Rub al Khali in the Arabian Peninsula, we immediately thought of
Pathfinder, Spirit, Opportunity, Curiosity, Perseverance, and all of the
other landers and rovers discovering the landscapes of Mars. The
comparison is not necessarily hyperbolic. For thousands of years, the
Bedouin have traversed the Arabian Desert and are the only masters of its
more than 650,000 square kilometers of deadly sands. The first foreign
explorers weren't able to breach the Quarter until 1931, with the first
accurate Western maps made by Wilfred Thesiger between 1946 and 1950.
Since then, only a few extreme adventurers have attempted its crossing,
leaving the rest of us to wonder at its edge. We began the project with no
site, no client, and no real purpose but to go out and see. Although it was a
series of brutal negotiations between the known and the unknown, between
success and absolute failure where we often found ourselves between a rock
and a hard place, we hoped to allow people to get a little farther out to
experience a little-known people and place, to penetrate the desert, to learn,
and to live as few of us have done before.

Technology
* It is neither technophobic nor technophilic in its production.
FIGURE 13.4 A manual hydraulic pipe bender used in
conjunction with laser-cut jigs and bending reference plane
toward the fabrication of the Neonomads prototype two shelter.
Photograph by the Authors.

FIGURE 13.5 A student utilizing augmented reality via a
HoloLens 2 headset to tweak positioning of an aluminum tube
prior to being bent by a six-axis robot and external positioner.
Photograph by the Authors.

While there may be broader chicken and egg questions of whether
particular analog or digital tools drive our processes and constructions, or
whether we simply seek out viable tools to manifest our designs, this type
of question doesn't really interest us. We consider ourselves free-range,
working by hook or by crook. Being grounded, resourceful, and clever to
make things happen has proven to be far more valuable than being a
disciple of a particular way of working or utilizing a specific set of tools.
The work isn't bogged down at the altar of a particular technoevangelist or
neo-luddite. We are aware, and have had to sit through sermons of the false
idol/ideal(ism) of Patrik Schumacher. And, unfortunately, we are not of the
privileged class of Williams and Tsien that once pined at the loss of
"Slowness" due to the "efficiency" of the "computer" as "tools of the hand
disappear."15 This is not to say that there hasn't been incredible work
produced by both of these offices, but it is quite clear that they are operating
in the rarified air via particular and ideal modes that they have had the
luxury of growing accustomed to over multiple decades.
As a counterpoint, we are opportunists working in a rough and ready
manner. Be it hardware, software, analog, or digital, we don't much care as
long as the technology can facilitate what needs to be done. A sketch on a
scrap piece of stock can prove to be just as valuable as a complete digital
model, depending on the particular situation and the construct to be built.
This mentality is something we try to instill in our student collaborators as
well. This get-it-done attitude has led students to fashioning their own
vacuformer via a kitchen oven and small shopvac to create a thermoform
mold to be used with a rotomolder of their own making. Ingenuity along
similar lines led students to utilize a 55-gallon (208-liter) plastic drum and
personally fabricated marble ball bearings for use with cardboard wheels to
serve as a device for transporting materials for a desert design build. For the

Neonomads subsequent series of desert shelters, the general approach,
beyond computer visualization, utilized analog jigs, a plethora of
metalworking tools, and extensive amounts of MIG welding. Digital
fabrication tools such as a large-format laser cutter were also employed in
support of the overall fabrication effort, but typically for very specific tasks
that would be impossible to achieve through other means. Exo-Skeleton, a
reimagined pinup surface, on the other hand was designed and fabricated
almost entirely through digital means. The computer model facilitated an
iterative design approach and eventually, through a custom parametric
definition, generated unique KUKA Robot Language (KRL) code for each
of the tubes, which were then bent robotically. However, analog tools were
critical in the fit, finishing, and assembly of the structure.
Surrender
* It necessitates the grit of its collaborators to find success.

FIGURE 13.6 Neonomads prototype three shelter onsite and in
use at night. Photograph by the Authors.
FIGURE 13.7 Neonomads prototype four shelter onsite and in
use at dusk. Photograph by the Authors.
Architecture and the business of architecture is not easy. We often tell
students who are questioning their choice of study that there are much
easier ways to make a living, ways that subsume far less of their time and
that do not take the same mental toll. Yet, in the vast majority of instances,
and for reasons that we often aren't privy to, they keep at it. While, for a
multitude of reasons, not everyone should continue to study architecture,
for those who do, grit is quite possibly the most important quality they can
possess to find success. Psychologist and MacArthur Fellow Angela
Duckworth's research on the value of grit seems to support such an
assessment.16 In architectural practice, projects take time; there is hardly
any instant gratification. Instilling perseverance in our students is critical

both for their immediate and their long-term growth. If architecture is hard,
then the act of physically designing and building architecture is
exponentially so. This is especially true when students often start a design
build studio with little to no practical knowledge of how things are built,
and even less experience of the processes and realities of building. But then
again, these are exactly the reasons we teach design build. In juxtaposition
to typical architectural design studios where viability and reality can be
skirted, physical artifacts don't lie. They are or they aren't. They do or they
don't. One way or another, they must be resolved.
With these very real constraints ever present, resilience becomes all the
more important on a day-to-day basis when the scale of the task at hand
often feels insurmountable. This is where the importance of showing up and
getting stuck in really matters. In this way we have to be relentless, like
Hemingway, showing up, day in and day out, attacking the keys of his
Underwood.17 Due to the particular demographics of the students that we
instruct, there is often an initial skepticism about attending university to
then engage with such physically demanding work. This hesitation means
that, as faculty members, we must uncharacteristically lead from the front,
at least for a time, until the students find their niches and assume positions
of leadership. No matter the particulars of our individual roles, our
collective determination is what allows us to dig deep and push a project
forward. By the time the photographic documentation of the particular
project in question is taking place, the traces of its circuitous route and all
the points of potential surrender along the way have been polished over,
leaving few discerning marks to the casual observer. However, by their very
nature, the processes and the eventual outcomes of design build projects are
the byproducts of taxing efforts by all involved - with little glory but with
plenty of blood, sweat, and tears expended. No doubt it is due to this shared

trauma that the experiences and lessons carried on are far more visceral and
the collective bond is that much stronger.
Iteration
* It is iterative, preferring the small, the fast, and the frequent in order
to fail more often and learn more deeply.
Our work is perpetually in progress and our process is inherently iterative.
Long ago we came to realize that the first time we do something, it's
typically a bit garbage. That is why we're not in the habit of doing things
only once. We therefore stack the deck and build opportunities for multiple
passes into the problems we pursue. Our cyclical approach happens
internally within projects, but also from project to project. In these
instances, we serve as the continuity, and the problems we engage with are
meaty enough that we can gnaw on them for years. For example, what
might start as a foray into bending steel tube utilizing a manual hydraulic
bender toward a preliminary concept for a design build project might
subsequently dramatically reduce scale and serve as an introductory
modeling project for an option studio, before finally, a few years later,
serving as a new research trajectory exploring the potential of multiplanar
tube bending facilitated through the accuracy of a six-axis robot and an
external positioner. Gradually, this refined technique has found its way back
into the instruction of an advanced digital fabrication elective, where the
workflow has facilitated the development of a micro design build project.
This way of working is antithetical to the typical modus operandi of
design builds. They are one and done. Design builds projects are moon
shots. The vast expenditures of resources - including capital, materials, and
time, along with the attention and oversight they garner - means failure is

not really an option. So, through remarkable force of will and resolve
emanating from the courageous space monkey (faculty member/s) strapped
in, projects more often than not escape the pull of gravity, and space is
delivered. Inversely, we have basked in the spectacle of a design build
project gone awry, disintegrating in the atmosphere, and somehow have
lived to tell the tale.18 While this was in no way pleasant, the scars of the
experience have shaped our approach going forward. We have embraced
small ball. We have found ways for design build, like our other work and
instruction, to become iterative as well. This iterative approach to design
build gives us more opportunities to get things right, provides moments to
step back and critically look at what is and isn't working, and eases the
collective pressure we feel.
FIGURE 13.8 The completed Exo-Skeleton micro design build
pinup board. Photograph by the Authors.

The End
* The work is unabashed, it is true, and it does not end.
We are nomads.19 Not like some traditional Bedouin, as described by
Wilfred Thesinger in Arabian Sands,20 but new nomads, defined by global
hypermobility, rootlessness, and an aesthetic and ethical reflexivity.21 We
feel akin to one of sociologist Richard Sennett's foreigners - painfully and
unavoidably adaptive, unable to take for granted ways of life that seem so
natural to natives.22 Constantly on the move since birth and having lived in
many places for no more than a few months to a year, we are itinerants,
finding ourselves perpetually in-between. Unlike stereotypical nomads, we
adhere to few borders, fewer memberships, and can be assigned to no fixed
territory. We do not claim affiliations to nations, governments, or
institutions, but to tribes - discovered, formed, and joined organically as we
have moved, found mostly in late-night design studios. Conditioned by this
instability, we have a ceaseless and almost nostalgic longing for home; a
home we have never really known. The work, we have discovered, has
become a search for belonging. Looking back at past projects, we have
come to realize that they all, in some way, revolve around the same themes
of place, community, memory, and disappearance. We are captivated by
loss, whether to time or to the horizon left behind us. Much of that work
requires that we get into a car and drive out into the landscape to where, as
Jean Baudrillard puts it, "snapshots aren't enough."23
At some point when we reach the sea of sand of the Rub al Khali where
the road ends, we will have to get out of the car and walk, like the old ones.
We will carry the architecture on our backs, away from modern society and
the things of humankind, walking back to the basics, to the past, back to the
elements of earth, air, water, and fire; back to Semper's hearth.24 Our

sincerest intention is that, through this process, our students, our new tribe,
will find some thing, whatever it is, and that it will latch onto them and go
deep to the bone, and last. Samuel Mockbee called this thing being "snake
bit";25 and, just like that, you have to go out in the wilderness to get it and
you'll never see it coming. We can only hope that they will carry this thing,
this lesson, memory, this value with them for the rest of their lives, and
return to it, now and then, as they continue their own work. We have
learned over time that this thing cannot be taught and must be experienced,
and it is hard won. It may be the only reward. Out there, finally, in the
isolation and scarcity of the desert, under the stars and around a fire where
stories are told, we find ourselves and each other. We find belonging. We
make our tribe. Through our stories, our memories, and passing down to
those who come after us what we have learned, the work will never end.
Progress
* We find the courage to persevere despite the risk of hardship and
failure that our obstacles promise.
Defined as onward momentum, to advance or develop toward a more
improved condition, or any movement forward through space and time,
"progress" is often expressed through optimism or with the hope and
confidence that future efforts will work out and lead, inevitably, to some
measure of success. But, as William Cain points out in his writings on the
American Dream, while "the greatest American dreamers say Yes ... their
power comes first from saying No."26 The motivation to affect great change
begins with the rejection of the past and the status quo, a dissatisfaction
with who we used to be and the staleness of who we are, and a hunger for
something more, something better, something new that may be just around

the bend up ahead. Our shared goal of a life fulfilled by freedom and
happiness is not achieved easily, without struggle, or by simply finding it by
chance on the path beneath us - it is a pursuit. We start from behind, we
give chase, and there are no guarantees that we will overtake it.
Risk and its relationship to progress have been integral parts of the
human experience from the beginning, and perhaps the single greatest
consideration in every human endeavor to advance civilization, from the
spice trade to the space race. The Chinese symbol for risk, in fact, is a
combination of symbols for danger and opportunity.27 Risk has been a
primary catalyst in shaping our present society and the dividing line
between modernity and what came before.28 Risk can be measured, its
origins defined, and its impacts predicted. Over the millennia, untold
resources have been expended toward understanding risk and developing
risk-taking capacities in religious, political, military, sports, and corporate
cultures. And yet we teach our students to avoid risk at all costs. The
emphasis in modern education on academic achievement that is measured
in points and scores develops in our students the practical belief that
intellect is more important than character, that potential is tied to Grade
Point Average (GPA), to be uncomfortable with the unknown, and that it is
wiser to avoid failure, even though it is the best way to learn.

FIGURE 13.9 A Neonomad hiking structural components out to
the site with the help of a custom-fabricated backpack and two
poles. Photograph by the Authors.

To combat the fear of failure that is so prevalent and deep-seated among
students, we should be establishing studio environments that demand
ambition and accept failure while exemplifying the spirit of entrepreneur
and engineer Andy Grove's catchphrase, "Make mistakes faster." The more
we put students in the position of making their own decisions, the more
risks they will take. As decision-making marks the end of deliberation and
the beginning of action, it always includes risk.29 But, instead of asking
students to chase innovative (but likely failing) solutions to the most critical
issues we face, the only "risk"-taking in many academic design studios is
through formal and aesthetic agendas, where the priority is on designing
beautiful objects rather than meaningful processes.
Our focus on form and individual artistic skill has led to an emphasis on
self-satisfaction and a lack of social consideration in the way that we teach
architecture.30 In order to progress, we must develop the kind of risk-taking
in students that affects society beyond a mere visual presence, that
considers the lives of ordinary people and the issues they face, that takes
responsibility for its impacts and failures, that listens. We must push for
expanded roles for architects, more relevant forms of practice, more
effective ways to engage emerging markets and diverse populations, for
innovation, for invention, for the freedom to fail. To progress we must look
back to the humble humanism that made architecture the "mother of all
arts" so socially relevant prior to the beaux-arts revolution, while situating
ourselves at the heart of culture rather than its margins, where we can
operate with agency. And then perhaps, by looking back and remembering
who we were and what we valued, we can finally move forward.

FIGURE 13.10 Neonomads joining bent tube sections into a
structural loop for the prototype two shelter. Photograph by the
Authors.

FIGURE 13.11 Neonomads prototype two shelter canopy test
fitting with tape demarcating the proposed extents of the base
platform. Photograph by the Authors.
